Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
6 min
Broker and Currency Devaluation: No Buyer Liability
Ruling on broker's liability for price loss.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a broker is a trustee and the buyer is not liable for loss of the price after delivery if the broker acted with owner's permission.

Core Idea
Broker is a trustee, not a guarantor; loss of price after receipt does not revert to buyer.

A common scenario in commerce: a broker collects the sale price and the currency becomes worthless before he hands it over. Is the buyer still liable? This ruling clarifies that if the broker acted with the owner's permission, the buyer is discharged and the loss falls on the owner–broker relationship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
The buyer pays the price to the broker (dallal), not directly to the seller. The broker is an agent of the seller.
The broker is authorized by the seller (bã'i' bi-idhn al-mãlik).
2
Determine the legal capacity of the broker
The broker is a trustee (amãn) regarding the price. He does not become a guarantor by mere possession.
Trustee status means he is not liable for accidental loss.
3
Apply the ruling to the scenario
Since the broker received the price with permission, the buyer's obligation is fulfilled. The subsequent devaluation is not the buyer's concern.
The buyer has no claim against the broker either; the seller may seek recourse from the broker if the broker was negligent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا مسائل مناسبة للثمن لا بأس بذكرها تكثيراً للفوائد : لو استوفى الدلال الثمن ثم كسد في يده فلا مطالبة على المشتري حيث باع بإذن المالك، ولو دفع المشتري إلى البائع أكثر من حقه غلطاً فضاعت فضاعت الألف فهو مؤتمن فيه، فإن ضاع نصفها فالنصف الباقي على ستة أسهم والأصل أن المال المشترك إذا هلك منه شيء فالهالك على الشركة والباقي يبقى على الشركة، فلو عزل منها مائتي درهم . المائتان قبل أن يردها كانت الألف بينهما على ستة ولو ضاعت الألف فللبائع أن يرجع في المائتين بخمسة أسداسها انتهت . وفي الواقعات شرى الدجاجة بالبيضات اشترى دجاجة بخمس بيضات فلم يقبضها حتى باضت خمساً، فإن كان الشراء بخمس بيضات بعينها ولم يستهلك البائع البيضات التي باضتها عنده يأخذ المشتري الدجاجة والبيضات ويدفع إليه الثمن ولا يجب على المشتري التصدق به لأنه يصير بمنزلة ما لو اشترى دجاجة وخمس بيضات بخمس بيضات وذلك جائز، فإن كان البائع استهلك البيضات أخذ المشتري الدجاجة بثلاث بيضات وثلث بيضة إن كانت قيمة الدجاجة عشر بيضات لأن الثمن ينقسم على قيمة الدجاجة وعلى خمس بيضات استهلكها البائع، فإن كانت قيمة الدجاجة عشر بيضات ينقسم الثمن أثلاثاً، فما أصاب خمس بيضات سقط، وما أصاب الدجاجة وهو الثلاث والثلث لزم فإن كانت بغير أعيانها، فإن لم يستهلك البائع البيضات التي باضت عنده يتصدق المشتري بالفضل لأنه لو اشترى دجاجة وخمس بيضات بغير عينها لا يجوز فكذا هنا، فإن استهلكها البائع فالحكم كما لو كانت بعينها اهـ. وفي الواقعات: اشتري شيئاً ودفع إلى البائع دراهم صحاحاً فكسرها البائع فوجدها نبهرجة فردها فلا شيء عليه لأنه لم يتلف عليه شيئاً، وكذا لو دفع إليه إنسان لينظر إليه فكسره . باع بدراهم جياد فدفع إليه المشتري فأراها البائع رجلاً فانتقدها فوجدها قليل نبهرجة فاستبدل فأراد أن يصرف في شراء الحوائج فلم يأخذها أحد وقالوا كلها نبهرجة، إن كان أقر للبائع أنها جياد لا يرد لأنه متناقض إلا إذا صدقه المشتري فإن لم يكن أقر بذلك يرد لأنه غير متناقض اهـ . والله أعلم.
And here are suitable issues related to price, there is no harm in mentioning them for increasing benefits: If the broker receives the price and then it becomes worthless in his hand, there is no claim against the buyer when he sold with the owner's permission. And if the buyer pays the seller more than his right by mistake, and it is lost, and the thousand is lost, then he is a trustee regarding it. If half of it is lost, the remaining half is on six shares, and the principle is that if something from joint property is destroyed, the destroyed part is shared, and the remaining remains shared. So if he sets aside two hundred dirhams from it, the two hundred before he returns them, the thousand is between them on six (shares). And if the thousand is lost, the seller may recourse against the two hundred for five-sixths of it. End. And in al-Waqi'at: he bought a chicken with five eggs and did not take possession until it laid five eggs. If the purchase was with five specific eggs and the seller did not consume the eggs that were laid with him, the buyer takes the chicken and the eggs and gives him the price, and it is not obligatory on the buyer to give charity because it becomes like the case where he bought a chicken and five eggs with five eggs, and that is permissible. If the seller consumed the eggs, the buyer takes the chicken for three and one-third eggs if the value of the chicken is ten eggs, because the price is divided over the value of the chicken and over five eggs that the seller consumed. If the value of the chicken is ten eggs, the price is divided into thirds: what corresponds to five eggs drops, and what corresponds to the chicken (three and a third) is due. If it was unspecified (non-specific), then if the seller did not consume the eggs that were laid with him, the buyer gives charity for the excess because if he bought a chicken and five eggs without specification, it is not valid, so likewise here. If he consumed them, the ruling is as if they were specified. End. And in al-Waqi'at: he bought something and gave the seller sound dirhams, and the seller broke them and found them to be defective (nubahrja), and returned them, so nothing is owed because he did not destroy anything of his. Likewise if someone gave him a dirham to look at and he broke it. He sold with good dirhams, and the buyer paid him; the seller showed them to a man who examined them and found a few defective; he exchanged them and wanted to use them to buy necessities, but no one accepted them and they said all are defective. If he had affirmed to the seller that they were good, he cannot return because of contradiction, unless the buyer confirms him. If he did not affirm that, he can return because there is no contradiction. End. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage gathers several subsidiary rulings related to price in sales. The first ruling: if a broker collects the price and then the currency becomes worthless, the buyer is not liable because the broker acted with the owner's permission. The second ruling: if a buyer overpays by mistake and the extra amount is held as a trust by the seller, they become joint owners of the mixed money. The third case: buying a chicken with eggs, where subsequent eggs produced complicate the transaction. If the eggs were specified, the buyer takes both chicken and new eggs; if unspecified and the seller did not consume, the buyer must give charity for the excess due to the prohibition of combining a commodity with currency as price in a single transaction. The fourth case: defective dirhams; if a seller breaks a dirham and finds it defective, he is not liable. If the seller affirms that the dirhams are good and then tries to return them, the return is not accepted because of contradiction.

Key Terms
دلال (dallal): A broker or middleman who facilitates a sale.
كسد (kasada): To become worthless or unsaleable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the buyer not liable when the broker loses the price?
A) Because the broker is a guarantor
B) Because the broker is a trustee with owner's permission
C) Because the buyer paid the seller directly
D) Because the loss is shared

Key Takeaway
An authorized broker is a trustee; the buyer is discharged from liability upon payment.

Overpayment by Mistake: Joint Ownership of Mixed Funds
Ruling on overpayment and joint ownership of mixed funds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle of joint ownership when mixed funds are partially lost due to overpayment by mistake.

Core Idea
Overpaid amount becomes a trust; if mixed with seller's funds, they become joint owners proportionate to shares.

A buyer accidentally pays more than the agreed price. The seller holds the extra as a trust (amanah). If the funds get mixed and part is lost, how is the loss distributed? This ruling explains the proportional sharing principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario: buyer overpays
The buyer pays more than the right (haqq) by mistake (ghalatan).
The extra amount is not owned by the seller.
2
Determine the legal status of the extra
The seller holds the extra as a trustee (amin) because it is not his property.
Trustee: no liability for accidental loss.
3
Apply mixing principle
If the extra is mixed with the seller's own money, they become joint owners (sharikat milk) in proportion to their shares.
The principle: المال المشترك إذا هلك شيء فالهالك على الشركة.
4
Calculate loss when part is lost
If half of the mixed fund is lost, the loss is shared proportionally between the buyer and seller.
Example: total 600 (200 extra + 400 seller), loss of 300: 100 from buyer's extra, 200 from seller.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
غلطاً (ghalatan): By mistake, unintentionally.
المال المشترك (al-mal al-mushtarak): Joint property, owned by multiple parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the overpayment case, if the total mixed fund is lost, what can the seller do?
A) The seller must bear the entire loss
B) The seller can claim back his share from the buyer's extra
C) The seller can demand the buyer pay again
D) The loss is split 50-50

Key Takeaway
Overpaid amount is a trust; if mixed and partially lost, loss is borne proportionally.

Chicken and Eggs: Specified vs Unspecified Eggs in Sale
Ruling on sale of chicken with eggs and subsequent eggs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between specified and unspecified eggs in a chicken-eggs transaction and state the ruling on excess eggs produced.

Core Idea
When buying a chicken with eggs, the subsequent eggs go to the buyer if the eggs were specified; if unspecified, the transaction is invalid and the excess must be given as charity.

A buyer purchases a chicken with five eggs. Before taking possession, the chicken lays five more eggs. This creates a complex transaction: are the new eggs part of the sale? The ruling depends on whether the original eggs were specified (mu'ayyan) or unspecified (ghayr mu'ayyan). This case illustrates the Hanafi principle of combining a commodity with currency as a price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction: chicken for eggs
The buyer gives eggs (the price) for a chicken (the commodity). This is a sale (bai').
Eggs are considered a commodity (mithli).
2
Check if eggs were specified or unspecified
The text distinguishes between specified (bi a'yânihâ) and unspecified (bi ghayr a'yânihâ).
Specified: particular eggs; unspecified: eggs as a generic quantity.
3
Apply ruling for specified eggs
If specified and the seller did not consume them, the buyer takes the chicken and new eggs, paying the price. No charity needed.
The transaction is valid because the price is a combination of chicken and new eggs ? actually the price is the original eggs, the new eggs are an addition.
4
Apply ruling for unspecified eggs
If unspecified and the seller did not consume, the transaction is invalid (because price must be specific). The buyer must give charity for the excess (new eggs).
The excess is considered a windfall gained from an invalid transaction.
5
Note exception when seller consumes
If the seller consumed the original eggs, the ruling is the same regardless of specification: the buyer takes the chicken for a reduced price (calculated proportionally).
Consumption of the price eggs changes the situation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
بعينها (bi a'yânihâ): Specific, particular (eggs).
بغير أعيانها (bi ghayr a'yânihâ): Unspecific, generic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the chicken-eggs case, when must the buyer give charity?
A) When eggs are specified and not consumed
B) When eggs are unspecified and not consumed
C) When eggs are specified and consumed
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Specifying the price is crucial; unspecified price leads to invalidity and required charity for excess.

Defective Dirhams: Seller's Affirmation and Contradiction
Ruling on liability for defective dirhams and effect of seller's affirmation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a seller who affirms defective dirhams as good cannot later return them except if the buyer confirms, and understand the liability for breaking a defective dirham.

Core Idea
A seller who affirms defective dirhams as good is barred from returning them due to contradiction; breaking a defective dirham incurs no liability.

A seller receives defective dirhams (nubahraja) from a buyer, but initially affirms they are good (jiyad). Later, he discovers they are defective and wants to return them. Can he? The ruling depends on whether his affirmation constitutes a binding statement. Also, if the seller accidentally breaks a defective dirham while inspecting it, is he liable? This lesson explains the Hanafi position on contradiction and liability for defective currency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario: defective dirhams
The buyer pays with dirhams that are defective (nubahraja). The seller does not immediately realize.
'Nubahraja' means counterfeit or substandard dirhams that are not accepted in transactions.
2
First sub-issue: breaking a dirham
If the seller breaks a dirham to test it and finds it defective, he is not liable because he did not destroy anything of value.
The dirham was already defective, so no loss.
3
Second sub-issue: seller affirms dirhams are good
If the seller states that the dirhams are good (jiyad), he later cannot return them because his statement contradicts his claim.
Exception: if the buyer confirms the seller's mistake, return is allowed.
4
Third sub-issue: no affirmation
If the seller never affirmed the dirhams as good, he can return them to the buyer because there is no contradiction.
The buyer must then take back the defective dirhams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا مسائل مناسبة للثمن لا بأس بذكرها تكثيراً للفوائد : لو استوفى الدلال الثمن ثم كسد في يده فلا مطالبة على المشتري حيث باع بإذن المالك، ولو دفع المشتري إلى البائع أكثر من حقه غلطاً فضاعت فضاعت الألف فهو مؤتمن فيه، فإن ضاع نصفها فالنصف الباقي على ستة أسهم والأصل أن المال المشترك إذا هلك منه شيء فالهالك على الشركة والباقي يبقى على الشركة، فلو عزل منها مائتي درهم . المائتان قبل أن يردها كانت الألف بينهما على ستة ولو ضاعت الألف فللبائع أن يرجع في المائتين بخمسة أسداسها انتهت . وفي الواقعات شرى الدجاجة بالبيضات اشترى دجاجة بخمس بيضات فلم يقبضها حتى باضت خمساً، فإن كان الشراء بخمس بيضات بعينها ولم يستهلك البائع البيضات التي باضتها عنده يأخذ المشتري الدجاجة والبيضات ويدفع إليه الثمن ولا يجب على المشتري التصدق به لأنه يصير بمنزلة ما لو اشترى دجاجة وخمس بيضات بخمس بيضات وذلك جائز، فإن كان البائع استهلك البيضات أخذ المشتري الدجاجة بثلاث بيضات وثلث بيضة إن كانت قيمة الدجاجة عشر بيضات لأن الثمن ينقسم على قيمة الدجاجة وعلى خمس بيضات استهلكها البائع، فإن كانت قيمة الدجاجة عشر بيضات ينقسم الثمن أثلاثاً، فما أصاب خمس بيضات سقط، وما أصاب الدجاجة وهو الثلاث والثلث لزم فإن كانت بغير أعيانها، فإن لم يستهلك البائع البيضات التي باضت عنده يتصدق المشتري بالفضل لأنه لو اشترى دجاجة وخمس بيضات بغير عينها لا يجوز فكذا هنا، فإن استهلكها البائع فالحكم كما لو كانت بعينها اهـ. وفي الواقعات: اشتري شيئاً ودفع إلى البائع دراهم صحاحاً فكسرها البائع فوجدها نبهرجة فردها فلا شيء عليه لأنه لم يتلف عليه شيئاً، وكذا لو دفع إليه إنسان لينظر إليه فكسره . باع بدراهم جياد فدفع إليه المشتري فأراها البائع رجلاً فانتقدها فوجدها قليل نبهرجة فاستبدل فأراد أن يصرف في شراء الحوائج فلم يأخذها أحد وقالوا كلها نبهرجة، إن كان أقر للبائع أنها جياد لا يرد لأنه متناقض إلا إذا صدقه المشتري فإن لم يكن أقر بذلك يرد لأنه غير متناقض اهـ . والله أعلم.
And here are suitable issues related to price, there is no harm in mentioning them for increasing benefits: If the broker receives the price and then it becomes worthless in his hand, there is no claim against the buyer when he sold with the owner's permission. And if the buyer pays the seller more than his right by mistake, and it is lost, and the thousand is lost, then he is a trustee regarding it. If half of it is lost, the remaining half is on six shares, and the principle is that if something from joint property is destroyed, the destroyed part is shared, and the remaining remains shared. So if he sets aside two hundred dirhams from it, the two hundred before he returns them, the thousand is between them on six (shares). And if the thousand is lost, the seller may recourse against the two hundred for five-sixths of it. End. And in al-Waqi'at: he bought a chicken with five eggs and did not take possession until it laid five eggs. If the purchase was with five specific eggs and the seller did not consume the eggs that were laid with him, the buyer takes the chicken and the eggs and gives him the price, and it is not obligatory on the buyer to give charity because it becomes like the case where he bought a chicken and five eggs with five eggs, and that is permissible. If the seller consumed the eggs, the buyer takes the chicken for three and one-third eggs if the value of the chicken is ten eggs, because the price is divided over the value of the chicken and over five eggs that the seller consumed. If the value of the chicken is ten eggs, the price is divided into thirds: what corresponds to five eggs drops, and what corresponds to the chicken (three and a third) is due. If it was unspecified (non-specific), then if the seller did not consume the eggs that were laid with him, the buyer gives charity for the excess because if he bought a chicken and five eggs without specification, it is not valid, so likewise here. If he consumed them, the ruling is as if they were specified. End. And in al-Waqi'at: he bought something and gave the seller sound dirhams, and the seller broke them and found them to be defective (nubahrja), and returned them, so nothing is owed because he did not destroy anything of his. Likewise if someone gave him a dirham to look at and he broke it. He sold with good dirhams, and the buyer paid him; the seller showed them to a man who examined them and found a few defective; he exchanged them and wanted to use them to buy necessities, but no one accepted them and they said all are defective. If he had affirmed to the seller that they were good, he cannot return because of contradiction, unless the buyer confirms him. If he did not affirm that, he can return because there is no contradiction. End. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage gathers several subsidiary rulings related to price in sales. The first ruling: if a broker collects the price and then the currency becomes worthless, the buyer is not liable because the broker acted with the owner's permission. The second ruling: if a buyer overpays by mistake and the extra amount is held as a trust by the seller, they become joint owners of the mixed money. The third case: buying a chicken with eggs, where subsequent eggs produced complicate the transaction. If the eggs were specified, the buyer takes both chicken and new eggs; if unspecified and the seller did not consume, the buyer must give charity for the excess due to the prohibition of combining a commodity with currency as price in a single transaction. The fourth case: defective dirhams; if a seller breaks a dirham and finds it defective, he is not liable. If the seller affirms that the dirhams are good and then tries to return them, the return is not accepted because of contradiction.

Key Terms
نبهرجة (nubahraja): Defective or counterfeit dirhams that are not acceptable in transactions.
متناقض (mutanāqiḍ): Contradictory, inconsistent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When can a seller who affirmed defective dirhams as good still return them?
A) Never
B) If the buyer confirms the affirmation was mistaken
C) If the seller pays a penalty
D) If the dirhams are completely lost

Key Takeaway
A seller who affirms defective currency as good is bound by his statement unless the buyer admits the mistake.

Validity of Sale with Immediate Price or Known Deferred Term
Commentary on the validity of sale with immediate price or known deferred term

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a sale is valid with an immediate price or a known deferred term, explain why knowledge of the term is necessary, and identify the default requirement (halul) of the contract.

Core Idea
A sale is valid with immediate payment or a known term; ignorance of the term invalidates because it causes dispute.

In this passage, the commentator from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a key rule from Kanz al-Daqaiq: a sale is valid whether the price is paid immediately or deferred to a known term. He clarifies that the default demand of the contract is immediate payment (halul), and deferment is an exception that must be explicitly stipulated. Moreover, the term must be known; otherwise, ignorance leads to conflict between buyer and seller. Let's unpack the legal reasoning behind this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The matn states: sale is valid with immediate price (hal) and with known term (ajal ma'lum). What is the ruling? Sale is valid (sahih).
2
Understand the default
The commentary quotes al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that halul (immediacy) is the requirement (muqtada) and effect (muijab) of the contract. Why? Because the contract's inherent nature demands prompt exchange.
Immediacy is the default; deferment is an exception.
3
Recognize the exception
Deferment (ajal) is not automatic; it is only established by an express condition (shart). So if no term is stated, payment is due immediately.
4
Apply the condition of knowledge
The author restricts validity to a KNOWN term (ajal ma'lum). Why? Because ignorance of the term leads to dispute (niza') where the seller could demand payment too early. So for a deferred sale to be valid, the term must be specified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وصح بثمن حال وبأجل معلوم) أي البيع لإطلاق النصوص. وفي السراج الوهاج أن الحلول مقتضى العقد وموجبه والأجل لا يثبت إلا بالشرط اهـ. قيد بعلم الأجل لأن جهالته تفضي إلى النزاع فالبائع يطالبه في مدة قريبة
His saying: (And a sale is valid with an immediate price and with a known term) i.e., the sale [is valid] due to the unrestrictedness of the texts. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that immediacy is the requirement and effect of the contract, and the term is established only by condition. End. He restricted [the validity] with knowledge of the term because ignorance of it leads to dispute, so the seller would demand payment in a short period.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage comments on the matn statement that a sale is valid with either an immediate price (hāl) or with a known deferred term (ajal ma'lum). The commentator explains that the permissibility of sale comes from the general texts of Quran and Sunnah that allow trade. He quotes from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that the default in a sale is immediate exchange (halūl) — the contract inherently requires prompt payment unless a term is stipulated. The deferment (ajal) only becomes valid through an explicit condition. The qualification 'known term' is necessary because ignorance of the term leads to dispute: if the term is unknown, the seller could demand payment too soon, causing conflict.

Key Terms
حلول (halūl): Immediacy; the price is due immediately upon the contract.
أجل (ajal): A deferred term or deadline for payment.
مقتضى العقد (muqtada al-'aqd): The inherent legal effect and requirement of a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, when is a term (ajal) valid in a sale?
A) The term is automatically valid
B) Only if explicitly stipulated
C) If the buyer and seller agree verbally without writing
D) If the term is less than one year

Key Takeaway
A sale is valid with immediate payment or a known deferred term; the deferment must be explicitly stipulated and the term known to avoid dispute.

Sale with Known Price and Loss in Joint Property
Explains sale validity and joint property loss

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid sale regarding the price (immediate or deferred with a known term) and apply the Hanafi principle that loss in joint property is shared proportionally, leaving the remainder joint.

Core Idea
The price must be known; loss in joint funds is proportional, remainder stays joint.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains two interconnected rules: first, a sale is valid only if the price is known—either paid immediately or deferred with a specified term. Second, when parties share funds (e.g., partial payment or joint property), any loss is borne proportionally, and the remaining sum continues as joint property. Understanding these rules is essential for resolving disputes over mixed payments and lost funds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling on sale validity
State that a sale is valid (sahih) if the price is either immediate (thaman haal) or deferred with a known term (ajal ma'loom).
The opposite—unknown term—makes the sale fasid.
2
Understand the treatment of excess payment
If currencies differ or an extra amount is paid, the excess is held as a trust (amana) by the recipient.
This is not a joint fund; the trustee is not liable for loss without negligence.
3
Grasp the principle of loss in joint property
When joint property is partially lost, the loss is shared proportionally by the owners, and the remainder remains joint property in the same proportions.
This is stated explicitly: 'al-halik 'ala al-shirka wa al-baqi yabqa 'ala al-shirka'.
4
Trace the examples given
Examine each example: agent segregating funds, loss of exact price vs. excess, buyer's negligence, mistaken bag of dinars, and the numerical example of 1000 and 200 dirhams.
Each illustrates a different application of the principle.
5
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Given a joint fund of a certain size and a loss, calculate how the loss is shared and what remains joint.
The shares depend on original ownership percentages, not equal division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب مشار وصح يثمن حال وبأجل معلوم ومطلقه على النقد الغالب وإن اختلفت النقود فالزائد أمانة فإنه ضاع نصف المدفوع فالباقي بينهما على الشركة، والأصل أن المال المشترك إذا هلك منه شيء فالهالك على الشركة والباقي يبقى على الشركة فإن عزل منها الرائد فضاع قبل الرد كان الباقي بينهما، ولو ضاع قدر الثمن دون الزائد فللبائع أن يرجع في الزائد بحسابه، ولو جعل الألف في كمه ودفع المائتين إلى غلامه فسرق الكل لا رجوع لواحد منهما، ولو دفع المشتري إليه كيساً على أن فيه الثمن دراهم فذهب به إلى منزله فإذا فيه دنانير فحملها ليردها فضاعت في الطريق فلا ضمان؛ الكل من التتارخانية. فضاعت الألف فهو مؤتمن فيه، فإن ضاع نصفها فالنصف الباقي على ستة أسهم والأصل أن المال المشترك إذا هلك منه شيء فالهالك على الشركة والباقي يبقى على الشركة، فلو عزل منها مائتي درهم المائتان قبل أن يردها كان الألف بينهما على ستة ولو ضاعت الألف فللبائع أن يرجع في المائتين بخمسة أسداسها انتهت.
The book: And it is valid with an immediate price and with a known deferred price, and its unrestricted [sale] is upon the prevalent currency, and if currencies differ, the excess is a trust. If half of what was paid is lost, the remaining is between them as joint property. The principle is that when something is lost from joint property, the loss is shared proportionally, and the remainder remains joint. If the agent segregates some [funds] and it is lost before return, the remainder is between them. If the amount equal to the price is lost but not the excess, the seller may claim from the excess proportionally. If he put the thousand in his sleeve and gave the two hundred to his servant and all was stolen, neither can claim from the other. If the buyer gave him a bag on the understanding that it contained the price in dirhams, and he took it to his home and found that it contained dinars, and he carried it to return it but it was lost on the way, there is no liability. All from al-Tatarkhaniyya. If the thousand is lost, he is a trustee regarding it. If half of it is lost, the remaining half is on six shares. The principle is that when something is lost from joint property, the loss is shared, and the remainder remains joint. If he sets aside two hundred dirhams, the two hundred before he returns it, the thousand is between them on six. And if the thousand is lost, the seller may claim from the two hundred five-sixths of it. End.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage opens with a ruling from the matn: a sale is valid with an immediate price or a known deferred price. The author then discusses cases where the price is paid in mixed currencies: the excess beyond the price is held as a trust (amana) by the buyer. If part of the payment is lost, the remaining sum is treated as joint property between buyer and seller until the price is satisfied. The text establishes the general principle (asl) that in a joint fund, any loss is borne proportionally by the owners, and the remainder continues as shared. Several examples illustrate this: if an agent (ra'id) separates some of the joint money and it is lost before return, the rest stays joint; if only the exact price is lost (not the excess), the seller can recover from the excess proportionally; if all money is stolen due to the buyer's negligence, no recourse; if the buyer mistakenly gives a bag of dinars thinking it is dirhams, and loses it while returning, the buyer bears no liability. The second paragraph repeats the principle with a different numerical example involving one thousand and two hundred dirhams, clarifying that if the seller had set aside part of the money, the loss calculation adjusts accordingly. All rulings are drawn from al-Tatarkhaniyya, a later Hanafi fatwa collection (fatawa mutakhkhirin).

Key Terms
ثمن حال (thaman haal): A price that is due immediately at the time of the contract.
أجل معلوم (ajal ma'loom): A known, specific deferred term for payment of the price.
مال مشترك (maal mushtarak): Joint property owned by two or more persons.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle stated in this passage, if joint property of two partners is partially lost, the remaining property:
A) Becomes the property of the partner who did not cause the loss.
B) Remains joint property between them proportionally.
C) Is divided equally between them regardless of shares.
D) Is returned to the original owner before the partnership.

Key Takeaway
A sale requires a known price, and any loss in joint property is shared proportionally; the remainder stays joint.

Uncertain Price in Sales: Hen with Eggs and Counterfeit Coins
Applies rules of sale with unknown price to specific scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for sales where the price is unknown or defective to the cases of a hen bought with eggs and payment with counterfeit coins.

Core Idea
A sale with an uncertain price is not automatically void; later events can validate or salvage the transaction.

This passage from al-Waqi'at presents two practical examples that clarify how the Hanafi school handles sales where the price is not fully known or defective. The first involves buying a hen with eggs as the price, where the hen lays more eggs before possession. The second involves paying with coins that turn out to be counterfeit. These cases illustrate that the ruling depends on whether the price was specified, whether the goods were consumed, and whether the buyer made a declaration about the coins' quality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the uncertainty in each case
In the hen case, the price consists of eggs that may change or produce new eggs before possession. In the coin case, the dirhams are later found to be counterfeit.
Uncertainty in the price can invalidate a sale if fundamental.
2
For the hen case, determine if the price eggs were specified or unspecified
The text distinguishes between buying with specific eggs (bikhams baydat bi'aynihaa) and unspecified eggs (bighayr a'yanihaa). This is the first branching factor.
3
Apply the rule based on consumption of the new eggs
If the seller consumed the newly laid eggs, the price is recalculated proportionally. If not, the buyer takes the hen and the new eggs and pays the original price.
Consumption by the seller changes the mode of performance, making an otherwise uncertain price determinable.
4
For the coin case, examine the buyer's declaration
If the buyer declared the coins good (aqara lahu annaha jiyad), he cannot later return them unless the seller agrees. If he did not declare, he can return them.
The buyer's own statement creates an estoppel effect.
5
Synthesize the underlying Hanafi principle
Both cases show that uncertainty in price is not incurable; subsequent events (consumption, agreed valuation, or absence of contradiction) can validate the sale.
This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on practical resolution over formalistic invalidation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الواقعات شرى الدجاجة بالبيضات اشترى دجاجة بخمس بيضات فلم يقبضها حتى باضت خمساً، فإن كان الشراء بخمس بيضات بعينها ولم يستهلك البائع البيضات التي باضتها عنده يأخذ المشتري الدجاجة والبيضات ويدفع إليه الثمن ولا يجب على المشتري التصدق به لأنه يصير بمنزلة ما لو اشترى دجاجة وخمس بيضات بخمس بيضات وذلك جائز، فإن كان البائع استهلك البيضات أخذ المشتري الدجاجة بثلاث بيضات وثلث بيضة إن كانت قيمة الدجاجة عشر بيضات لأن الثمن ينقسم على قيمة الدجاجة وعلى خمس بيضات استهلكها البائع، فإن كانت قيمة الدجاجة عشر بيضات ينقسم الثمن أثلاثاً، فما أصاب خمس بيضات سقط، وما أصاب الدجاجة وهو الثلاث والثلث لزم فإن كانت بغير أعيانها، فإن لم يستهلك البائع البيضات التي باضت عنده يتصدق المشتري بالفضل لأنه لو اشترى دجاجة وخمس بيضات بغير عينها لا يجوز فكذا هنا، فإن استهلكها البائع فالحكم كما لو كانت بعينها اهـ. وفي الواقعات: اشتري شيئاً ودفع إلى البائع دراهم صحاحاً فكسرها البائع فوجدها نبهرجة فردها فلا شيء عليه لأنه لم يتلف عليه شيئاً، وكذا لو دفع إليه إنسان لينظر إليه فكسره . باع بدراهم جياد فدفع إليه المشتري فأراها البائع رجلاً فانتقدها فوجدها قليل نبهرجة فاستبدل فأراد أن يصرف في شراء الحوائج فلم يأخذها أحد وقالوا كلها نبهرجة، إن كان أقر للبائع أنها جياد لا يرد لأنه متناقض إلا إذا صدقه المشتري فإن لم يكن أقر بذلك يرد لأنه غير متناقض اهـ . والله أعلم.
And in al-Waqi'at: He bought a hen with eggs; he bought a hen for five eggs, and he did not take possession until she laid five eggs. If the purchase was for five specific eggs and the seller did not consume the eggs that she laid at his place, the buyer takes the hen and the eggs and pays the price, and it is not obligatory upon the buyer to give charity because it becomes like the case where he bought a hen and five eggs for five eggs, and that is permissible. If the seller consumed the eggs, the buyer takes the hen for three and one-third eggs if the value of the hen is ten eggs, because the price is divided between the value of the hen and five eggs consumed by the seller. If the value of the hen is ten eggs, the price is divided into thirds; what corresponds to five eggs is dropped, and what corresponds to the hen, which is three and one-third, is payable. If it was unspecified: if the seller did not consume the eggs that she laid, the buyer gives charity for the excess because if he bought a hen and five eggs unspecified, it is not permissible, and so here. If the seller consumed them, the ruling is as if they were specific. End. And in al-Waqi'at: He bought something and paid the seller sound dirhams; the seller broke them and found them counterfeit and returned them, so there is no liability because he did not destroy anything of his. Similarly, if someone gave him [a dirham] to look at and he broke it. He sold with good dirhams; the buyer paid him and the seller showed them to a man who examined them and found them somewhat counterfeit, so he exchanged them and wanted to spend them on purchases, but nobody took them and they said all are counterfeit. If he had declared to the seller that they were good, he cannot return them because he contradicts himself, unless the seller confirms him. If he did not declare that, he can return them because he does not contradict himself. End. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage presents two cases from the work al-Waqi'at. The first case involves buying a hen with five eggs as the price, and then the hen lays five eggs before the buyer takes possession. The ruling depends on whether the eggs used as price were specifically identified or not, and whether the seller consumed the newly laid eggs. If the price eggs were specific and not consumed by the seller, the buyer gets both hen and new eggs and pays the price; no need to give charity because it is like buying a hen and five eggs with five eggs, which is valid. If the seller consumed the eggs, the buyer gets the hen at a proportional price. If the eggs were unspecified, the buyer must give charity for the excess if the seller did not consume the new eggs; if consumed, same as specific. The second case involves payment with dirhams that the seller discovers are counterfeit after breaking them. The seller is not liable if he broke them accidentally. If the buyer had declared the coins genuine, he cannot later return them unless the seller agrees; otherwise, he can return them because there is no contradiction. These cases illustrate how uncertainty in price or its quality affects the sale contract.

Key Terms
نبهرجة (nabharaja): Counterfeit or defective coins that are not acceptable as currency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of buying a hen with five specific eggs, and the hen lays five more eggs before possession, what is the ruling if the seller consumes the newly laid eggs?
A) The buyer gets nothing and the sale is void.
B) The buyer takes the hen but owes only three and one-third eggs (if hen value is ten eggs).
C) The buyer takes the hen and all eggs, and owes the original five eggs.
D) The sale is postponed until the seller replaces the eggs.

Key Takeaway
Sales with uncertain price are not necessarily void; if the price can be later determined through consumption or substitution, the sale remains valid.

Postponing the Price After Sale: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on post-contract price deferment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on postponing the price after sale and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to a concrete scenario.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa invalidates postponement after sale; Muhammad validates it as a gratuity.

The Hanafi school requires the price term to be known at contract formation. But what if the seller agrees to defer payment after the contract is concluded? This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa nullifies the sale due to uncertainty, while Muhammad sees it as a valid gratuity. Understanding the proofs and later tarjih is essential for correct fatwa in contemporary sale transactions.

Guided Path
1
State the default ruling
Recognize that the base matn requires the price to be immediate or deferred by a known term at the time of contract. Postponement after contract is not the default.
The passage begins with 'And it is valid with an immediate price and with a known deferred price.'
2
Identify the dispute
Pinpoint the contested scenario: seller sells validly, then postpones the price to an unknown term like harvest or threshing.
Text from al-Khaniyya: 'law ba'ath thumma ajjala al-thaman...'
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Understand the qiyas to a loan: if postponement is invalid in loan both as a condition and after, it is even more invalid in sale because sale requires certainty.
Qadi al-Nasafi also supports this by analogy.
4
Analyze Muhammad's proof
Muhammad treats postponement after sale as a gratuity (tabarru'), so it is accepted even to an unknown term, analogous to kafala (guarantee) to harvest.
The key difference: gratuity law is more lenient about uncertainty.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Note that later sources (Munyat al-Mufti) seem to follow Muhammad for close unknown terms, while Ghurar al-Afkar contradicts. The mufta bihi is not fully settled.
This reflects the mutakhkhirin tendency to accept deferred payment to customary close terms.

His saying: (And it is valid with an immediate price and with a known deferred price), i.e., the sale, due to the general import of texts. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: immediacy is the requirement and effect of the contract, and deferment is only established by condition. End. The qualification by knowledge of the term is because ignorance of it leads to dispute: the seller demands it in a short period and the buyer refuses, so it becomes invalid. In Sharh al-Majma' by the author from the chapter of option of condition: if he sold deferred and did not say 'until Ramadan', it is not permanent but rather three days according to some, and it is given as fatwa that it is deferred to a month. If they differ over the term, the word is of the one who denies it because the origin is its absence. Similarly if they differ over its amount, the word is of the one claiming the lesser, and the evidence is upon the buyer in both cases. If it has weight and cost, it is valid. From the unknown term is the stipulation that he give the price in instalments or each week some. If this is not a condition in the sale but mentioned after it, it does not invalidate and he may take the entire sum at once. Qadi Imam Abu Ali al-Nasafi said: this is problematic because if he lends and stipulates deferment in the loan, the deferment is invalid; if he lends and then postpones, it is also invalid; so the correct answer is what Shaykh Imam said: the sale is invalid with postponement to these times in the sale or after. End. I say: the author will mention from al-Siraj in this statement that postponement of the price of a debt unknown in its type is not permissible, and in its absolute form it includes postponement after the contract, and apparently it is for the postponement itself, not for the contract. In Munyat al-Mufti: whoever sells with an immediate price then postpones it to a known or unknown term that is close [like harvest, threshing, Nowruz, and similar] becomes deferred. End. This is based on what is narrated from Muhammad as previously mentioned, and the discussion of al-Siraj remains to be considered. In Ghurar al-Afkar Sharh Durar al-Bihar: postponement of the price of a debt to Nowruz, Mihrajan, Christian fasts and their break, harvest, threshing, and arrival of pilgrims is not permissible due to ignorance of the term, unless both [parties] know it, i.e., the two contractors, then the sale and the term are valid. Similarly, if the Christian starts fasting and he postpones until the break; if he sold absolutely and then postponed the price to these times, only the sale is valid. End. And this does not fit either of the two sayings mentioned in al-Khaniyya. His saying: (it does not invalidate and he may take the whole at once) which he presented
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the matn phrase 'And it is valid with an immediate price and with a known deferred price'. The author explains that the default requirement of a sale contract is immediate payment; deferment requires explicit agreement. The term must be known to avoid dispute. There is a discussion about the duration of a deferred term not specified: some say three days, but the fatwa is one month, analogized to the custom in salam and oaths. The core of the passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about postponing the price after the contract is concluded. According to Abu Hanifa, if the seller postpones the price after sale to an unknown term like harvest or threshing, the sale is invalidated. According to Muhammad, such postponement is valid because it is a gratuity (tabarru'), similar to guaranteeing a debt to an unknown time. Qadi al-Nasafi criticizes Muhammad's view by analogy to loan: if deferment is invalid in loan both as a condition and as a subsequent postponement, it should be invalid here too. The author then cites later sources: Munyat al-Mufti rules that postponement to a known or close unknown term (like harvest) makes the price deferred, implying validity. Ghurar al-Afkar says postponement to festivals is invalid due to ignorance, but if both parties know the term, it is valid. The discussion ends inconclusively, noting that the view of Ghurar al-Afkar does not match either of the two positions in al-Khaniyya. The passage also covers related issues: place of payment, instalments, and the evidentiary rules when parties dispute the term.

Key Terms
تأجيل الثمن (ta'jil al-thaman): Postponing the due date of the price after the sale contract has been concluded.
جهالة الأجل (jahalat al-ajal): Ignorance or uncertainty regarding the date of payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale becomes invalid (fasid).
B) The sale remains valid and the term is enforceable.
C) The term is set to one month by default.
D) The buyer must pay immediately regardless of the agreement.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of postponing the price after sale is disputed among Hanafi imams: Abu Hanifa invalidates due to uncertainty, while Muhammad validates as a gratuity; later fatwa tends to follow Muhammad for close unknown terms.

Rules for Resolving Disputes over the Deferment Term (Ajal)
Evidentiary rules for disputes over deferment term

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi evidentiary rules to three types of disputes over a deferment term in a sale contract.

Core Idea
The default is no deferment; the one who claims a term or a longer term bears the burden of proof.

When a buyer and seller disagree about whether a payment term was agreed, how long it was, or whether it has passed, how does a Hanafi judge decide? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides clear rules based on the principle of default (al-asl) and the priority of evidence (al-bayyina). By understanding these rules, you will be able to predict who must bring witnesses to court and who will win the dispute by default.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of dispute
Determine whether the disagreement is about the existence of a term, its length, or whether it has already passed.
2
Apply the default presumption
If existence is disputed, the default is no term (asl 'adam al-ajal). If length is disputed, the default is the shorter period. If passage is disputed, the default is that the term has not passed (since the buyer claims it hasn't).
The default aligns with the party denying the existence or claiming the shorter period.
3
Determine who gives the word (al-qawl)
The party whose claim matches the default is given the benefit of the doubt; their statement is accepted without evidence (e.g., the seller in the first scenario, the buyer claiming the shorter term in the second, and the buyer in the third).
4
Determine who bears the burden of proof (al-bayyina)
In all three scenarios, the buyer must provide evidence to overcome the default. The buyer is the one making the positive claim that differs from the default.
The text says: 'البينة بينته المشتري في الوجهين' — the buyer's evidence is his in both cases (meaning both first and second scenarios, and by extension the third as well).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا اختلفا في الأجل فالقول لمن ينفيه لأن الأصل عدمه، وكذا إذا اختلفا في قدره فالقول لمدعي الأقل والبينة بينته المشتري في الوجهين وإن اتفقا على قدره واختلفا في مضيه فالقول للمشتري أنه لم يمض والبينة بينته أيضاً لأن البينة مقدمة على الدعوى؛ كذا في الجوهرة وقيدنا بتأجيل الثمن لأن تأجيل المبيع المعين لا يجوز ويفسده كما في الجوهرة، ولا يرد على المصنف السلم مع أنه به في بابه من أن من شرائطه الأجل كما لا بيع بجنسه فإنه لا يصح مؤجلاً لما سنذكره في باب الربا.
And if they differ regarding the term, the word is of the one who denies it because the default is its absence. Similarly, if they differ regarding its amount, the word is of the one claiming the lesser amount, and the buyer's evidence is his in both cases. And if they agree on its amount but differ on its passing, the word is of the buyer that it has not passed, and his evidence is also his because evidence takes precedence over a mere claim; thus in al-Jawhara. And we restricted that to deferment of the price because deferment of the specified sold item is not permissible and invalidates it, as in al-Jawhara. And the author is not refuted by salam, even though it is in its chapter, for among its conditions is a term, just as a sale of the same genus is not valid on a deferred basis, as we will mention in the chapter on riba.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lays down procedural rules for when the buyer and seller dispute the existence of a deferment term (ajal) in a sale contract. The author states three scenarios: (1) If they dispute whether a term was agreed upon at all, the word is given to the one who denies the term (usually the seller) because the default (asal) is that the price is immediately due. (2) If they agree that a term exists but dispute its length (e.g., one says one month, the other says two months), the word is given to the one claiming the shorter period (usually the buyer), and the burden of proof is on the buyer (since he is claiming a longer term, he must provide evidence). (3) If they agree on the length of the term but dispute whether it has already passed, the word is given to the buyer who claims it has not passed, and his evidence is accepted because he is making a claim (da'wa) and the seller is denying, and evidence (bayyina) beats denial. The commentator notes that these rules apply only to deferment of the price (thaman), not to deferment of the actual sold item (mabi'), because deferring delivery of the sold item is invalid and voids the sale. He also clarifies that the rules do not contradict the validity of salam (forward sale), which requires a term, because salam is a distinct contract with its own conditions. The lesson illustrates the Hanafi approach to resolving disputes: the one with the stronger claim (based on default or evidence) prevails.

Key Terms
الأجل (al-ajal): The deferred term for payment in a sale contract.
الأصل (al-asl): The default legal presumption; here, 'original absence' (adam) of the term.
البينة (al-bayyina): Evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the buyer claims a one-month term and the seller claims no term was agreed, who has the burden of proof?
A) The buyer
B) The seller
C) Neither
D) Both

Key Takeaway
In any dispute over a deferment term, the buyer must provide evidence to overcome the default that payment is due immediately.

Deferment of Price to an Unknown Time: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on deferring price to unknown time after sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani on deferring the price to an unknown time, explain their proofs, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Deferring price to unknown time invalidates sale per Abu Hanifa; Muhammad sees it as valid voluntary deferment.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi disagreement about whether a seller can defer payment to an unknown time like harvest after the sale contract. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents Abu Hanifa's view that such deferment invalidates the sale, and Muhammad al-Shaybani's view that it is permissible as a voluntary act. We will analyze their proofs and see how later scholars resolved the conflict.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Determine what ruling is being debated: deferring the price to an unknown time (e.g., harvest) after the sale contract.
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa: sale invalid (fasid). Muhammad: sale valid, deferment valid.
3
Examine Muhammad's proof
Muhammad argues deferment after sale is a voluntary act (tabarru'), valid even with unknown time, analogized to kafalah.
4
Examine Abu Hanifa's proof and al-Nasafi's objection
Abu Hanifa analogizes to qard: deferment in loan is invalid, so also in sale. Al-Nasafi highlights inconsistency: if deferment fails in qard, it should fail in sale. Unknown term causes gharar.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later scholars prefer Abu Hanifa's view of fasad, though some fatawa tolerate Muhammad's view. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq supports Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية : رجل باع شيئاً بيعاً جائزاً وأخر الثمن إلى الحصاد أو الدياس قال يفسد البيع في قول أبي حنيفة، وعن محمد أنه لا يفسد البيع ويصح التأخير لأن التأخير بعد البيع تبرع فيقبل التأجيل إلى الوقت المجهول كما لو كفل بمال إلى الحصاد أو الدياس. وقال القاضي الإمام أبو علي النسفي رحمه الله : هذا يشكل بما إذا أقرض رجلاً وشرط في القرض أن يكون مؤجلاً لا يصح التأجيل، ولو أقرض ثم أخر لا يصح أيضاً فكان الصحيح من الجواب ما قال الشيخ الإمام أنه يفسد البيع أجله إلى هذه الأوقات في البيع أو بعده اهـ . قلت : سيذكر المؤلف عن السراج في هذه المقولة أن تأجيل الثمن الدين المجهول بنوعيه لا يجوز، وهو بإطلاقه شامل للتأجيل بعد العقد، وظاهره أن للتأجيل نفسه لا للعقد. وفي منية المفتي: من باع بثمن حال ثم أجله أجلاً معلوماً أو مجهولاً متقارباً كالحصاد والدياس والنيروز ونحوها صار مؤجلاً .اهـ. وهذا بناء على ما روي عن محمد كما تقدم ويبقى النظر في كلام السراج فتأمله وفي غرر الأفكار شرح درر البحار : لا يجوز تأجيل ثمن دين إلى النيروز والمهرجان وصوم النصارى وفطرهم والحصاد والدياس وقدوم الحاج لجهالة الأجل حتى لو كان كلاهما معلوماً عندهم أي العاقدين صح البيع والأجل، وكذا لو شرع النصراني في الصوم فأجل إلى الفطر ولو باع مطلقاً ثم أجل الثمن إلى هذه الأوقات صح البيع فقط اهـ. وهذا لا يناسب كلاً من القولين المذكورين في الخانية.
And in al-Khaniyya: A man sold something in a valid sale and deferred the price until the harvest or threshing; he said the sale is invalid according to Abu Hanifa, and from Muhammad that the sale is not invalid and the deferment is valid because the deferment after the sale is a voluntary act, so deferment to an unknown time is accepted, just as if one guaranteed a debt until harvest or threshing. Judge Imam Abu Ali al-Nasafi, may Allah have mercy on him, said: This is problematic because if a man lent and stipulated in the loan that it be deferred, the deferment is not valid, and if he lent and then deferred, it is also not valid. So the correct answer is what the Shaykh Imam said: that the sale is invalid with deferment to these times, whether in the sale or after it. I say: The author will mention from al-Siraj in this statement that deferring a price that is a debt with an unknown time of both types is not permissible, and it is absolute, including deferment after the contract, and apparently it is for the deferment itself, not the contract. And in Munyat al-Mufti: Whoever sold for an immediate price then deferred it a known or unknown time that is close, like harvest, threshing, Nawruz, and the like, it becomes deferred. This is based on what is reported from Muhammad as above, and the consideration of al-Siraj's statement remains. In Ghurar al-Afkar, a commentary on Durar al-Bihar: It is not permissible to defer the price of a debt to Nawruz, Mihrajan, the Christians' fasts and their breakings, harvest, threshing, and the arrival of the pilgrims due to the unknownness of the term, unless both times are known to them, i.e., the contracting parties, then the sale and term are valid. Similarly, if the Christian begins fasting and defers until the breaking, and if one sells absolutely then defers the price to these times, only the sale is valid. This does not correspond to either of the two opinions mentioned in al-Khaniyya.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding deferring the price (ta'jil al-thaman) to an unknown time after the contract, specifically to harvest (al-hasad) or threshing (al-diyas). The text first quotes al-Khaniyya: Abu Hanifa rules that such a deferment invalidates the sale (fasd al-bay'), while Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that the sale remains valid and the deferment is effective. Muhammad's reasoning is that deferment after the contract is a donation (tabarru') and can be effective even to an unknown time, analogizing to kafalah (guarantee) where one can guarantee a debt until harvest. The commentator then reports the objection of al-Qadi al-Nasafi, who points out an inconsistency with the rules of qard (loan): if deferment is stipulated in a loan, it is invalid, and even if the lender later defers, it is also invalid. Therefore, al-Nasafi prefers Abu Hanifa's view that the sale is void, whether the deferment is included in the contract or added later. The author (of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) then adds his own comment about al-Siraj's statement that deferring a debt to an unknown time is impermissible, and that this applies even if the deferment is after the contract. He then cites Munyat al-Mufti, which aligns with Muhammad's position that deferment to a known or near-unknown time (like harvest) is effective. Finally, he quotes Ghurar al-Afkar which distinguishes between deferment to religious festivals (unknown to the general public) and known times, and concludes that this view does not match either of the two opinions from al-Khaniyya. The lesson highlights the tension between principle (no deferment to unknown times) and practice (some later scholars allowing it), and the student should understand the authority weight: the conflicting positions are from the Zahir al-Riwayah (Abu Hanifa vs. Muhammad), while the later fatawa texts show variation.

Key Terms
تأجيل (ta'jil): Deferring the payment of a debt or price to a future date.
تبرع (tabarru'): A voluntary act or donation without consideration.
الحصاد (al-hasad): The harvest season, considered an unknown time because its exact date is not fixed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of Abu Hanifa when a seller defers the price to the harvest season after the sale contract?
A) The sale is valid and the deferment is valid.
B) The sale is invalid (fasid) and the deferment is invalid.
C) The sale is valid but the deferment is invalid.
D) The sale is invalid but the deferment is valid.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, deferring a price to an unknown time after a sale invalidates the sale according to the relied-upon view of Abu Hanifa.

Effect of Debtor's Statements and Prepayment on Deferred Term
Series of rulings on what invalidates or reinstates a deferred payment term

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between statements that do and do not invalidate a deferred term and explain the effect of early payment, mutual rescission, and defect return on the term.

Core Idea
A deferred term is only voided by explicit cancellation; early repayment with clawback restores the term, but mutual rescission permanently extinguishes it.

In Hanafi sales law, a deferred payment term is a valid condition, but the debtor's statements or actions can affect its validity. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq collects several key rulings that clarify exactly which statements invalidate the term and which events restore or extinguish it permanently.

Guided Path
1
Identify non-invalidating statements
The debtor saying 'I am free from the term' or 'I have no need of it' does not invalidate the term because these are not explicit cancellations.
2
Identify invalidating statements
Explicit words like 'I have relinquished it', 'I have annulled it', or 'I have made it due immediately' clearly cancel the term, making the debt payable immediately.
3
Analyze effect of early payment with subsequent clawback
If the debtor pays early, but the payment is later reclaimed (e.g., goods found defective or coins counterfeit), the original term is restored because the early payment is treated as if it never happened.
4
Analyze effect of mutual rescission (taqayul)
If the buyer purchases from the debtor and later they mutually rescind that purchase, the original deferred term does not return; it is permanently waived.
5
Analyze effect of return due to defect with court order
If the buyer returns the item due to a defect and a court orders the return, the term returns because this is a legal cancellation, not voluntary.
6
Note effect on guarantor and seller's right of detention
A guarantor's obligation does not return in either rescission or defect return; and if the seller agreed to deferment, he cannot withhold the goods after the term expires.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المديون برئت من الأجل أو لا حاجة لي به لا يبطل ولو قال تركته أو أبطلته أو جعلت المال حالاً بطل الأجل، ولو عجل الدين قبل الحلول ثم استحق المقبوض أو وجده زيوفاً فرده عاد الأجل، ولو اشترى من المديون شيئاً ثم تقايلا لا يعود الأجل ولو رده بعيب بقضاء عاد، ولو كان لهذا الدين المؤجل كفيل لا تعود الكفالة في الوجهين؛ كذا في الخانية. وإذا رضي البائع بالتأجيل فقد أسقط حقه في حبس المبيع، فلو حل الأجل قبل قبضه فللمشتري قبضه قبل نقد الثمن ؛ كذا في المحيط. وسيأتي مسائل حبس المبيع آخر الباب.
The debtor: 'I am free from the term' or 'I have no need of it' does not invalidate. But if he said 'I have relinquished it' or 'I have annulled it' or 'I have made the money due immediately', the term is invalidated. And if he paid the debt before its due date, then the payment was reclaimed or he found it counterfeit and returned it, the term returns. And if he bought something from the debtor, then they mutually rescinded, the term does not return. And if he returned it due to a defect by court order, the term returns. And if this deferred debt has a guarantor, the guarantee does not return in both cases; as in al-Khaniyya. And if the seller agreed to deferment, he has forfeited his right to detain the sold item. So if the term expires before delivery, the buyer may take possession before paying the price; as in al-Muhit. And issues of detaining the sold item will come at the end of the chapter.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq collects several rulings from al-Khaniyya and al-Muhit concerning the validity of a deferred payment term in a sale. The rulings are presented as a series of cases: 1) If the debtor merely expresses disinterest in the term or claims to be free from it, these statements are not explicit cancellations and do not invalidate the term. 2) Explicit words like 'I have relinquished it' or 'I have annulled it' or 'I have made it due immediately' are clear expressions of cancellation and thus void the term. 3) If the debtor pays early but later the payment is reclaimed (e.g., due to a defect in the goods sold or because the coins were found counterfeit), the original term is restored; the early payment is treated as though it never happened. 4) If the buyer purchases something from the debtor and they later mutually rescind that purchase (taqayul), the original deferred term does not return; it is permanently waived. 5) However, if the buyer returns the purchased item due to a defect and a court order for return is issued, the original term returns because this is a legal cancellation, not a voluntary one. 6) If there is a guarantor (kafil) for the deferred debt, the guarantee does not return in either of the two scenarios (mutual rescission or defect return). 7) If the seller agrees to deferment, he loses his right to withhold the goods; the buyer can take delivery before payment if the term expires. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi position on the stability of deferred terms unless explicitly cancelled by clear words or actions.

Key Terms
أجل (ajal): A fixed future date for payment or delivery in a sale contract.
تأجيل (ta'jil): The act of granting a deferred term; the seller's agreement to postpone payment.
تقايل (taqayul): Mutual rescission of a contract by agreement of both parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which statement by the debtor invalidates the deferred term?
A) 'I am free from the term'
B) 'I have relinquished it'
C) 'I have no need of it'
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
A deferred payment term is only invalidated by explicit cancellation; early payment followed by clawback restores it, but mutual rescission permanently extinguishes it.

Death of Parties and Start of Deferred Term
Rulings on death, start of term, and seller prevention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on how death of buyer/seller affects a deferred payment, determine the start of the term, and apply the ruling on seller's prevention.

Core Idea
Buyer's death accelerates the debt; seller's death does not; term begins from delivery or after option.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents several key rulings on deferred payment terms. Understanding these rules is essential for structuring valid sale contracts with delayed payment. The text also clarifies when the term actually starts, especially in cases of seller prevention or options.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? A deferred payment term in a sale contract. The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) unless void by a specific cause.
2
Determine the effect of death of parties
If the seller dies, the term remains valid. If the buyer dies, the debt becomes due immediately. Understand the illa (reason): the purpose of deferment is trading for profit; upon death, the estate is for debts.
The rational illa is provided in the text.
3
Determine the start of the term
The term begins from the time of delivery, not from the contract date. If there is an option (khiyar), the term starts after the option falls.
This applies unless the seller prevents delivery.
4
Apply the seller prevention rule
If the seller prevents the buyer from taking delivery, the buyer gets an additional term starting from actual delivery. This is a separate rule from the start of the original term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البزازية : له على آخر ألف من ثمن مبيع فقال اعطه كل شهر مائة درهم لا يكون تأجيلاً ويملك طلبه في الحال وفي الملتقط عليه ألف ثمن جعله الطالب نجوماً إن أخل بنجم حل الباقي فالأمر كما شرطا اهـ. وفي شرح المجمع : لو مات البائع لا يبطل الأجل، ولو مات المشتري حل المال لأن فائدة التأجيل أن يتجر فيؤدي الثمن من نماء المال، فإذا مات من له الأجل تعين المتروك لقضاء الدين فلا يفيد التأجيل اهـ . وفي المجمع : وللمشتري أجل سنة ثانية لمنع البائع السلعة سنة الأجل اهـ. فابتداؤه من وقت التسليم. وكذا لو كان فيه خيار يعتبر الأجل من حين سقوط الخيار عنده؛ كذا في الخانية. وفي التجنيس فرق بين هذا وبين ما إذا اشترى إلى رمضان فمنعه حتى دخل رمضان كان المال حالاً في قولهم جميعاً اهـ. وهكذا في الخانية. ولا خصوص لرمضان وإنما خلاف الصاحبين في السنة المنكرة، أما في السنة المعينة فلا يبقى الأجل بعد مضيها، والمراد بمنعه عدم قبض المشتري المبيع مجازاً لكون منعه سبباً له؛ كذا في شرح المجمع لكن نقل بعض الفضلاء عن الفتاوى الهندية أن محل الاختلاف فيما إذا امتنع البائع من التسليم أما إذا لم يمتنع فابتداؤه ومن وقت العقد إجماعاً اهـ . قال : إذا علمت ذلك تعلم أن ما في شرح المجمع لا وجه له . قلت : وما نقله عن الهندية سيذكره المؤلف قبل باب خيار الشرط عند قول الماتن ومن باع سلعة بثمن سلمه أولاً
And in al-Bazzaziyya: Someone owes another a thousand dirhams as price of a sold item, and he said 'Give him every month one hundred dirhams' – this is not a deferment, and he has the right to demand it immediately. And in al-Multaqat: He owed a thousand dirhams price, and the creditor made it into installments: if he defaults on one installment, the rest become due – the matter is as they stipulated, end. And in Sharh al-Majma': If the seller dies, the term is not invalidated; but if the buyer dies, the money becomes due immediately, because the benefit of deferment is that he trades and pays the price from the profit of the wealth, so when the one who has the term dies, the estate is designated for debt payment, so deferment becomes useless, end. And in al-Majma': The buyer has a second year of term due to the seller preventing the goods for a year of term, end. So its beginning is from the time of delivery. Similarly, if there is an option, the term is counted from the time the option falls, as in al-Khaniyya. And in al-Tajnis, there is a difference between this and the case where he buys until Ramadan, and he prevents him until Ramadan enters, then the money is due immediately according to all of them, end. And this is also in al-Khaniyya. And there is no specificity for Ramadan; rather, the dispute of the two companions (Sahibayn) is concerning the indefinite year. As for the definite year, the term does not remain after its passing. And by 'prevention' is meant figuratively the buyer not taking delivery, because his prevention is the cause; as in Sharh al-Majma'. But some virtuous scholars transmitted from al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya that the point of disagreement is when the seller refrains from delivery; but if he does not refrain, then its beginning is from the time of contract by consensus, end. He said: If you know this, you know that what is in Sharh al-Majma' has no basis. I (the author) say: What he transmitted from al-Hindiyya, the author will mention it before the chapter on option of condition when the author says 'And whoever sells goods for a price, he delivers first.'
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq covers several distinct rulings related to deferred payment. First, from al-Bazzaziyya: Telling a debtor to pay monthly installments of one hundred does not constitute a binding deferment; the creditor may demand immediate payment. Second, from al-Multaqat: If the creditor stipulates that default on any installment accelerates the remainder, that condition is valid. Third, from Sharh al-Majma': Death of the seller does not cancel the term, but death of the buyer causes immediate maturity of the debt. The reasoning ('illa) given is that the purpose of deferment is to allow the buyer to trade and generate profit to pay the price; upon death, his remaining estate is already designated for debt payment, so deferment no longer serves its purpose. Fourth, from al-Majma': If the seller prevents the buyer from taking delivery for a year, the buyer gets an additional year of term, starting from delivery. Fifth, the term begins from delivery, or from the fall of any option if applicable. Sixth, al-Tajnis distinguishes this from buying 'until Ramadan' with seller's prevention; in that specific case, upon entering Ramadan the debt becomes due by consensus of the school. Then the text discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute: the Sahibayn differ from Abu Hanifa regarding the 'indefinite year' (al-sana al-nakira). The relied-upon position is that the term does not continue after a definite year passes. The marginal note from the Indian Fatawa clarifies that the dispute applies only when the seller prevents delivery; otherwise, the term starts from the contract by consensus. The author notes that this will be mentioned later in the chapter on option of condition.

Key Terms
تأجيل (ta'jil): The act of granting a term or deferment for payment.
خيار (khiyar): An option clause in a contract that allows one or both parties to cancel within a specified period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the deferred debt if the buyer dies before the term expires?
A) The term continues and the heirs pay at its end
B) The debt becomes due immediately
C) The debt is forgiven
D) The debt transfers to the seller's heirs

Key Takeaway
Buyer's death accelerates the debt; seller's death does not; term begins from delivery or after option, and seller prevention grants an additional term.

The Indefinite Year Dispute and the Exception for Seller's Prevention
Dispute on indefinite year and the Ramadan exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a definite and indefinite year term, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and identify the consensus exception for a specified time like Ramadan.

Core Idea
The indefinite year term is disputed; the definite year ends after its passing; seller's prevention triggers an exception.

This part of the passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq focuses on a nuanced ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school regarding the validity of a term specified as an indefinite year. The text also clarifies that when a specific time (like Ramadan) is mentioned, consensus holds that the debt becomes due upon arrival if the seller prevented delivery.

Guided Path
1
Identify definite vs indefinite year
A definite year is specified by name or date (e.g., 'year of Ramadan 2025'). An indefinite year is just 'one year' without specification. The ruling differs.
2
State the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn disagree on the indefinite year. One view holds the term continues after the year passes; the other holds it ends. The text does not resolve it but notes the ikhtilaf.
The dispute is only about the indefinite year, not the definite year.
3
Apply the Ramadan exception
If a term is 'until Ramadan' and the seller prevents delivery, upon entering Ramadan the debt becomes due immediately by consensus. This is not specific to Ramadan; any named time works.
This shows that when a specific time is mentioned, the term ends at that time regardless of prevention.
4
Understand the marginal note restriction
The Indian Fatawa restricts the dispute to cases where the seller prevents delivery; otherwise, the term begins from the contract date by consensus. This limits the scope of ikhtilaf.
Some later scholars critique the earlier position in Sharh al-Majma'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البزازية : له على آخر ألف من ثمن مبيع فقال اعطه كل شهر مائة درهم لا يكون تأجيلاً ويملك طلبه في الحال وفي الملتقط عليه ألف ثمن جعله الطالب نجوماً إن أخل بنجم حل الباقي فالأمر كما شرطا اهـ. وفي شرح المجمع : لو مات البائع لا يبطل الأجل، ولو مات المشتري حل المال لأن فائدة التأجيل أن يتجر فيؤدي الثمن من نماء المال، فإذا مات من له الأجل تعين المتروك لقضاء الدين فلا يفيد التأجيل اهـ . وفي المجمع : وللمشتري أجل سنة ثانية لمنع البائع السلعة سنة الأجل اهـ. فابتداؤه من وقت التسليم. وكذا لو كان فيه خيار يعتبر الأجل من حين سقوط الخيار عنده؛ كذا في الخانية. وفي التجنيس فرق بين هذا وبين ما إذا اشترى إلى رمضان فمنعه حتى دخل رمضان كان المال حالاً في قولهم جميعاً اهـ. وهكذا في الخانية. ولا خصوص لرمضان وإنما خلاف الصاحبين في السنة المنكرة، أما في السنة المعينة فلا يبقى الأجل بعد مضيها، والمراد بمنعه عدم قبض المشتري المبيع مجازاً لكون منعه سبباً له؛ كذا في شرح المجمع لكن نقل بعض الفضلاء عن الفتاوى الهندية أن محل الاختلاف فيما إذا امتنع البائع من التسليم أما إذا لم يمتنع فابتداؤه ومن وقت العقد إجماعاً اهـ . قال : إذا علمت ذلك تعلم أن ما في شرح المجمع لا وجه له . قلت : وما نقله عن الهندية سيذكره المؤلف قبل باب خيار الشرط عند قول الماتن ومن باع سلعة بثمن سلمه أولاً
And in al-Bazzaziyya: Someone owes another a thousand dirhams as price of a sold item, and he said 'Give him every month one hundred dirhams' – this is not a deferment, and he has the right to demand it immediately. And in al-Multaqat: He owed a thousand dirhams price, and the creditor made it into installments: if he defaults on one installment, the rest become due – the matter is as they stipulated, end. And in Sharh al-Majma': If the seller dies, the term is not invalidated; but if the buyer dies, the money becomes due immediately, because the benefit of deferment is that he trades and pays the price from the profit of the wealth, so when the one who has the term dies, the estate is designated for debt payment, so deferment becomes useless, end. And in al-Tajnis, there is a difference between this and the case where he buys until Ramadan, and he prevents him until Ramadan enters, then the money is due immediately according to all of them, end. And this is also in al-Khaniyya. And there is no specificity for Ramadan; rather, the dispute of the two companions (Sahibayn) is concerning the indefinite year. As for the definite year, the term does not remain after its passing. And by 'prevention' is meant figuratively the buyer not taking delivery, because his prevention is the cause; as in Sharh al-Majma'. Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq covers several distinct rulings related to deferred payment. First, from al-Bazzaziyya: Telling a debtor to pay monthly installments of one hundred does not constitute a binding deferment; the creditor may demand immediate payment. Second, from al-Multaqat: If the creditor stipulates that default on any installment accelerates the remainder, that condition is valid. Third, from Sharh al-Majma': Death of the seller does not cancel the term, but death of the buyer causes immediate maturity of the debt. The reasoning ('illa) given is that the purpose of deferment is to allow the buyer to trade and generate profit to pay the price; upon death, his remaining estate is already designated for debt payment, so deferment no longer serves its purpose. Fourth, from al-Majma': If the seller prevents the buyer from taking delivery for a year, the buyer gets an additional year of term, starting from delivery. Fifth, the term begins from delivery, or from the fall of any option if applicable. Sixth, al-Tajnis distinguishes this from buying 'until Ramadan' with seller's prevention; in that specific case, upon entering Ramadan the debt becomes due by consensus of the school. Then the text discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute: the Sahibayn differ from Abu Hanifa regarding the 'indefinite year' (al-sana al-nakira). The relied-upon position is that the term does not continue after a definite year passes. The marginal note from the Indian Fatawa clarifies that the dispute applies only when the seller prevents delivery; otherwise, the term starts from the contract by consensus. The author notes that this will be mentioned later in the chapter on option of condition.

Key Terms
سنة منكرة (sana nakira): An indefinite year, i.e., a year term that is not specified by name or date.
سنة معينة (sana mu'ayyana): A definite year, specified by name or date.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the point of ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding deferred terms?
A) Whether a definite year term continues after its passing
B) Whether an indefinite year term continues after its passing
C) Whether seller prevention affects the term
D) Whether the death of buyer accelerates the debt

Key Takeaway
The indefinite year term is disputed between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn; the definite year term ends after its passing; seller prevention may trigger consensus exceptions.
