Installment Calculation with Overlapping Payment Terms
Worked example of a payment schedule with concurrent obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the daily amounts due when a sale requires payment 'every day one dirham and every two days two dirhams' over a six-day period.

Core Idea
Overlapping payment terms create a unique daily pattern; each term matures independently.

This example from al-Khaniyya and al-Tajnis illustrates how a buyer pays for a garment with a promise to pay daily and every two days. The calculation shows that the total of ten dirhams is paid in uneven daily installments over six days. Understanding this pattern helps grasp how multiple concurrent obligations accumulate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the promise
The buyer promises 'every day one dirham and every two days two dirhams'. Note the use of 'kull' (every) which implies repetition.
The word 'kull' triggers a recurring obligation each time the period recurs.
2
List each term's schedule
Separate the daily term: one dirham due each day. And the two-daily term: two dirhams due every second day (day 2, 4, 6...).
3
Calculate daily total
For each day, add the daily dirham (due each day) plus any two-daily dirhams that mature on that day. For day 1: only the daily dirham. Day 2: daily dirham (due) + two-daily (2 dirhams) = 3. Day 3: only daily dirham. And so on.
The two-daily term matures on days 2, 4, and 6, giving 2 dirhams each.
4
Verify total
Sum the daily amounts: 1+3+1+3+1+1 = 10 dirhams. The final day (6) has only the daily dirham because the two-daily term on day 6 already gave two dirhams; the remaining one from the ten is paid as a daily dirham on day 6.
The author explicitly accounts for the remaining one dirham on day 6.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية والتجنيس : رجل قال لآخر بعت منك هذا الثوب بعشرة على أن تعطيني كل يوم درهماً وكل يومين درهمين يعطيه عشرة في ستة أيام اليوم الأول درهماً وثلاثة في اليوم الثاني ودرهماً في اليوم الثالث وثلاثة في اليوم الرابع ودرهماً في اليوم الخامس ودرهماً في اليوم السادس، أما في اليوم الأول يعطيه درهماً ظاهر، وفي اليوم الثاني يعطيه ثلاثة لأنه جعل اليوم أجلاً للدرهم الواحد بكلمة كل الموجبة للتكرار فكلما جاء يوم يلزمه درهم، وفي اليوم الثاني يلزمه درهم بمجيء اليوم الثاني ودرهمان بمجيء يومين ودرهم في اليوم الثالث لحلول نجم آخر ولم يحل للدرهمين أجل آخر، وفي الرابع يلزمه ثلاثة واحد بمضي الرابع ودرهمان بمجيء أجل آخر للدرهمين، وفي الخامس يلزمه درهم بمجيء الخامس ولم يحل للدرهمين أجل آخر بقي من العشرة واحد يعطيه في اليوم السادس اهـ وفي الواقعات : اشترى شيئاً ودفع إلى البائع دراهم صحاحاً فكسرها البائع فوجدها نبهرجة فردها فلا شيء عليه لأنه لم يتلف عليه شيء، وكذا لو دفع إليه إنسان (۱) . وفي السراج الوهاج: الآجال على ضربين : معلومة ومجهولة والمجهولة على ضربين متقاربة ومتفاوتة. فالمعلومة السنون والشهور والأيام والمجهولة متقاربة كالحصاد والدياس والنيروز والمهرجان وقدوم الحاج وخروجهم والجذاذ والقطاف وصوم النصارى وفطرهم. والمتفاوتة كهبوب الريح وإلى أن تمطر السماء وإلى قدوم فلان وإلى الميسرة فتأجيل الثمن الدين المجهول بنوعيه لا يجوز وإن كان الثمن عيناً فسد بالتأجيل ولو معلوماً، وإذا أجل الدين أجلاً مجهولاً بجهالة متقاربة ثم أبطله المشتري قبل محله وقبل فسخه للفساد انقلب جائزاً، وإن مضت المدة قبل إبطاله تأكد فساده . وإن كانت جهالته متفاوتة فإن أبطله المشتري قبل التفرق انقلب جائزاً اهـ. وهنا مسائل في الواقعات متعلقة بالثمن أحببت ذكرها هنا : الأولى المأذون له في البيع إذا باع ومات فجاء المالك فليس له مطالبة وارث البائع ما لم يثبت قبضه ولا يقبل قول المشتري عليه ولا مطالبة له على المشتري إلا برضا الوارث لأن الوكيل بالبيع إذا مات لا ينتقل حق المطالبة بالثمن إلى موكله وإنما ينتقل إلى وارثه أو وصيه إن كان، فإن لم يكن نصب القاضي عنه وصياً ليقبض، وكأحد المتفاوضين إذا مات كان قبض الثمن إلى وصيه الثاني بياع عنده بضائع للناس أمروه ببيعها فباعها ونقد الثمن من ماله على أن يكون الثمن له فأفلس المشتري كان للبائع أن يسترد
And in al-Khaniyya and al-Tajnis: A man said to another, 'I sold you this garment for ten dirhams on condition that you give me every day a dirham and every two days two dirhams.' He gives ten in six days: the first day one, second day three, third day one, fourth day three, fifth day one, sixth day one. As for the first day, he gives one dirham, obviously. On the second day, he gives three because he made the day a term for the single dirham by the word 'every' which necessitates repetition. So whenever a day comes, he is liable for one dirham. On the second day, he is liable for one dirham due to the coming of the second day, and two dirhams due to the coming of two days (i.e., he now owes for day 2 and also for the two-day term that matured on day 2). On the third day, he gives one dirham for the arrival of a new installment, and the two dirhams had no further term. On the fourth, he gives three: one for the passage of the fourth, and two for the arrival of another term for the two dirhams. On the fifth, he gives one for the arrival of the fifth, and the two dirhams had no further term. One dirham remains from the ten, which he gives on the sixth day, end. And in al-Waqi'at: He bought something and paid sound dirhams to the seller. The seller broke them and found them counterfeit, and returned them. Then nothing is owed because he caused no loss. Similarly, if someone paid him (and they were counterfeit). And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: Deferment periods are of two types: known and unknown. The unknown is of two types: close and far. The known are years, months, days. The unknown close are like harvest, threshing, Nowruz, Mihrajan, arrival of pilgrims, their departure, fruit-cutting, grape-picking, Christian fast and their breaking. The far are like wind blowing, rain falling, arrival of so-and-so, and financial ease. Deferring the price of debt to an unknown period, of both types, is not permissible. If the price is a specific item, the sale becomes corrupt due to deferment even if the period is known. And if one defers a debt to an unknown period of close ignorance, then the buyer annuls it before its time and before he rescinds the corrupt sale, it becomes valid. But if the period passes before annulment, its corruption is confirmed. And if the ignorance is far, and the buyer annuls it before separation (from the meeting), it becomes valid, end. And here are issues in al-Waqi'at related to the price that I liked to mention: First, an agent authorized to sell who sells and dies; then the owner cannot demand from the seller's (agent's) heir without proving receipt, and the buyer's statement against him is not accepted, and he has no claim against the buyer except with the consent of the heir, because when the agent dies, the right to claim the price does not transfer to his principal but rather to his heir or executor if any. If there is none, the judge appoints an executor to receive. And like one of two partners in a partnership (mutafawidan) when he dies, the receipt of the price goes to the second partner's executor. A seller who has goods belonging to people, and they order him to sell, so he sells and pays the price from his own wealth on condition that the price be his, then the buyer becomes insolvent, the seller may reclaim (the goods).
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This lengthy passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq contains three main topics. First, a detailed calculation example from al-Khaniyya and al-Tajnis illustrating how a promise to pay 'every day one dirham and every two days two dirhams' actually works out: the buyer ends up paying a total of ten dirhams over six days but with uneven daily amounts (1,3,1,3,1,1). The explanation shows how each day's obligation arises from the combination of daily and two-daily terms. Second, a ruling from al-Waqi'at: If the buyer pays in sound dirhams and the seller breaks them and finds them counterfeit and returns them, the buyer owes nothing because the seller caused the loss by breaking them. Third, a comprehensive classification of deferment periods from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. Deferment periods are divided into known (years, months, days) and unknown. Unknown are further divided into near (like events with a typical but indefinite timeframe, e.g., harvest, festivals) and far (completely uncertain events like wind). The ruling: deferring payment to an unknown period is impermissible; if the price is a specific item (not a debt), the sale becomes corrupt (fasid) even if the period is known. However, if one makes such a corrupt deferment, the buyer can rectify it by annulling the term before its expiration, and the sale becomes valid. If the time has already passed without annulment, the corruption is confirmed. For far unknown periods, annulment must occur before the parties separate from the contract session. Finally, two issues from al-Waqi'at: (1) When an authorized agent dies after selling, the right to claim the price goes to the agent's heir, not the principal. (2) A seller who uses his own money to pay the price on behalf of the buyer and then the buyer becomes insolvent, the seller can reclaim the goods.

Key Terms
آجال معلومة (ajal ma'luma): Known deferment periods such as years, months, days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 1
B) 2
C) 3
D) 4

Key Takeaway
Overlapping recurring payment terms require day-by-day accumulation, and the total must equal the agreed price.

Ruling on Counterfeit Coins Discovered After Payment
A short ruling on liability for counterfeit coins when the seller breaks them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a seller breaks sound dirhams to find them counterfeit, and explain why the buyer is not liable.

Core Idea
If the seller destroys the coins, the buyer owes nothing because the seller caused the loss.

This short passage from al-Waqi'at addresses a common transaction problem: a buyer pays with sound-looking coins, but the seller breaks them and discovers they are counterfeit. The ruling is clear: the buyer is not liable because the seller, by breaking the coins, caused the loss. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that liability follows from causation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
The buyer pays sound dirhams; the seller breaks them and finds them counterfeit; then the seller returns them to the buyer.
The coins were initially accepted as sound.
2
Determine the ruling
The text says 'nothing is on him' (i.e., the buyer owes nothing). The seller cannot claim the price because he destroyed the coins.
The ruling applies whether the buyer knew or not.
3
Understand the reasoning
The seller 'caused no loss' because he broke the coins himself. If the coins were counterfeit, the buyer would have been liable if the seller had not broken them, but the seller's act of breaking transfers the liability.
Hanafi principle: damage caused by one's own action bars compensation.

Source Text

Classification of Deferment Periods and Their Legal Effect
Classification of deferment periods and associated rulings, plus two auxiliary issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify deferment periods as known, unknown-near, or unknown-far, and apply the Hanafi ruling that unknown periods render the sale corrupt unless timely annulled.

Core Idea
Unknown deferment invalidates a sale; cure exists only for near unknown periods if annulled before expiry.

This passage from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj provides a systematic classification of deferment periods (ajal). Known periods like years and days are acceptable. Unknown periods are divided into near (e.g., harvest) and far (e.g., wind). The key ruling: deferring payment to an unknown period is impermissible; if the price is a specific item, the sale becomes corrupt (fasid). However, for near unknown periods, the buyer can cure the corruption by annulling the term before it elapses. For far unknown periods, annulment must occur before the parties separate from the contract session. The passage also includes two practical issues from al-Waqi'at: when an authorized agent dies, the right to claim the price transfers to the agent's heir; and a seller who pays the price from his own pocket can reclaim goods if the buyer becomes insolvent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of deferment period
Determine if the period is known (ma'luma) like years, months, days, or unknown (majhula). If unknown, further classify as near (mutagariba) or far (mutafawita).
Examples of near: harvest, festival arrival. Far: rain, wind.
2
Apply the basic ruling
If the period is known, the deferment is valid. If unknown, the deferment is invalid. If the price is a debt (dayn), the deferment is impermissible. If the price is a specific item (ayn), the sale becomes corrupt (fasid).
Corrupt sale (fasid) means the contract is defective but can be rectified.
3
Consider the cure
For near unknown periods, the buyer (or debtor) can annul the deferment term before the period arrives, and the sale becomes valid. If the period passes without annulment, corruption is confirmed. For far unknown periods, annulment must happen before the contract session ends (before separation).
This distinction gives more flexibility for near periods.
4
Apply to agent and seller issues
When an authorized agent dies, the right to claim the price from the buyer passes to the agent's heir, not the principal. If a seller pays the price from his own funds and the buyer becomes insolvent, the seller can reclaim the goods.
These are additional rulings related to the price after sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية والتجنيس : رجل قال لآخر بعت منك هذا الثوب بعشرة على أن تعطيني كل يوم درهماً وكل يومين درهمين يعطيه عشرة في ستة أيام اليوم الأول درهماً وثلاثة في اليوم الثاني ودرهماً في اليوم الثالث وثلاثة بمجيء أجل آخر للدرهمين، وفي الخامس يلزمه درهم بمجيء الخامس ولم يحل للدرهمين أجل آخر بقي من العشرة واحد يعطيه في اليوم السادس اهـ وفي الواقعات : اشترى شيئاً ودفع إلى البائع دراهم صحاحاً فكسرها البائع فوجدها نبهرجة فردها فلا شيء عليه لأنه لم يتلف عليه شيء، وكذا لو دفع إليه إنسان (۱) . وفي السراج الوهاج: الآجال على ضربين : معلومة ومجهولة والمجهولة على ضربين متقاربة ومتفاوتة. فالمعلومة السنون والشهور والأيام والمجهولة متقاربة كالحصاد والدياس والنيروز والمهرجان وقدوم الحاج وخروجهم والجذاذ والقطاف وصوم النصارى وفطرهم. والمتفاوتة كهبوب الريح وإلى أن تمطر السماء وإلى قدوم فلان وإلى الميسرة فتأجيل الثمن الدين المجهول بنوعيه لا يجوز وإن كان الثمن عيناً فسد بالتأجيل ولو معلوماً، وإذا أجل الدين أجلاً مجهولاً بجهالة متقاربة ثم أبطله المشتري قبل محله وقبل فسخه للفساد انقلب جائزاً، وإن مضت المدة قبل إبطاله تأكد فساده . وإن كانت جهالته متفاوتة فإن أبطله المشتري قبل التفرق انقلب جائزاً اهـ. وهنا مسائل في الواقعات متعلقة بالثمن أحببت ذكرها هنا : الأولى المأذون له في البيع إذا باع ومات فجاء المالك فليس له مطالبة وارث البائع ما لم يثبت قبضه ولا يقبل قول المشتري عليه ولا مطالبة له على المشتري إلا برضا الوارث لأن الوكيل بالبيع إذا مات لا ينتقل حق المطالبة بالثمن إلى موكله وإنما ينتقل إلى وارثه أو وصيه إن كان، فإن لم يكن نصب القاضي عنه وصياً ليقبض، وكأحد المتفاوضين إذا مات كان قبض الثمن إلى وصيه الثاني بياع عنده بضائع للناس أمروه ببيعها فباعها ونقد الثمن من ماله على أن يكون الثمن له فأفلس المشتري كان للبائع أن يسترد
And in al-Khaniyya and al-Tajnis: A man said to another, 'I sold you this garment for ten dirhams on condition that you give me every day a dirham and every two days two dirhams.' He gives ten in six days: the first day one, second day three, third day one, fourth day three, fifth day one, sixth day one. As for the first day, he gives one dirham, obviously. On the second day, he gives three because he made the day a term for the single dirham by the word 'every' which necessitates repetition. So whenever a day comes, he is liable for one dirham. On the second day, he is liable for one dirham due to the coming of the second day, and two dirhams due to the coming of two days (i.e., he now owes for day 2 and also for the two-day term that matured on day 2). On the third day, he gives one dirham for the arrival of a new installment, and the two dirhams had no further term. On the fourth, he gives three: one for the passage of the fourth, and two for the arrival of another term for the two dirhams. On the fifth, he gives one for the arrival of the fifth, and the two dirhams had no further term. One dirham remains from the ten, which he gives on the sixth day, end. And in al-Waqi'at: He bought something and paid sound dirhams to the seller. The seller broke them and found them counterfeit, and returned them. Then nothing is owed because he caused no loss. Similarly, if someone paid him (and they were counterfeit). And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: Deferment periods are of two types: known and unknown. The unknown is of two types: close and far. The known are years, months, days. The unknown close are like harvest, threshing, Nowruz, Mihrajan, arrival of pilgrims, their departure, fruit-cutting, grape-picking, Christian fast and their breaking. The far are like wind blowing, rain falling, arrival of so-and-so, and financial ease. Deferring the price of debt to an unknown period, of both types, is not permissible. If the price is a specific item, the sale becomes corrupt due to deferment even if the period is known. And if one defers a debt to an unknown period of close ignorance, then the buyer annuls it before its time and before he rescinds the corrupt sale, it becomes valid. But if the period passes before annulment, its corruption is confirmed. And if the ignorance is far, and the buyer annuls it before separation (from the meeting), it becomes valid, end. And here are issues in al-Waqi'at related to the price that I liked to mention: First, an agent authorized to sell who sells and dies; then the owner cannot demand from the seller's (agent's) heir without proving receipt, and the buyer's statement against him is not accepted, and he has no claim against the buyer except with the consent of the heir, because when the agent dies, the right to claim the price does not transfer to his principal but rather to his heir or executor if any. If there is none, the judge appoints an executor to receive. And like one of two partners in a partnership (mutafawidan) when he dies, the receipt of the price goes to the second partner's executor. A seller who has goods belonging to people, and they order him to sell, so he sells and pays the price from his own wealth on condition that the price be his, then the buyer becomes insolvent, the seller may reclaim (the goods).
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This lengthy passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq contains three main topics. First, a detailed calculation example from al-Khaniyya and al-Tajnis illustrating how a promise to pay 'every day one dirham and every two days two dirhams' actually works out: the buyer ends up paying a total of ten dirhams over six days but with uneven daily amounts (1,3,1,3,1,1). The explanation shows how each day's obligation arises from the combination of daily and two-daily terms. Second, a ruling from al-Waqi'at: If the buyer pays in sound dirhams and the seller breaks them and finds them counterfeit and returns them, the buyer owes nothing because the seller caused the loss by breaking them. Third, a comprehensive classification of deferment periods from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. Deferment periods are divided into known (years, months, days) and unknown. Unknown are further divided into near (like events with a typical but indefinite timeframe, e.g., harvest, festivals) and far (completely uncertain events like wind). The ruling: deferring payment to an unknown period is impermissible; if the price is a specific item (not a debt), the sale becomes corrupt (fasid) even if the period is known. However, if one makes such a corrupt deferment, the buyer can rectify it by annulling the term before its expiration, and the sale becomes valid. If the time has already passed without annulment, the corruption is confirmed. For far unknown periods, annulment must occur before the parties separate from the contract session. Finally, two issues from al-Waqi'at: (1) When an authorized agent dies after selling, the right to claim the price goes to the agent's heir, not the principal. (2) A seller who uses his own money to pay the price on behalf of the buyer and then the buyer becomes insolvent, the seller can reclaim the goods.

Key Terms
آجال معلومة (ajal ma'luma): Known deferment periods such as years, months, days.
آجال مجهولة (ajal majhula): Unknown deferment periods, further divided into near (mutagariba) and far (mutafawita).
مأذون له في البيع (ma'dhun lahu fi al-bay'): An agent authorized to sell (wakil bil bay').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, what is the ruling if a specific item is sold with payment deferred to 'the grape-picking season'?
A) The sale is valid because the season is known
B) The sale is corrupt because the deferment is unknown-near
C) The sale is void because deferment is completely uncertain
D) The sale is valid only if the buyer is ready to pay early

Key Takeaway
Deferment periods must be known; unknown periods render the sale corrupt, but a cure exists for near types through annulment.

Calculating Daily Installments with the Word 'Kull'
Explains calculation of daily installments when term is set as 'each day'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the amount due on each day when a deferred payment is structured as 'one dirham each day' using the word 'kull'.

Core Idea
Using 'kull' for daily installments causes overlapping terms, yielding 1, 3, 1, 3, 1, 1 dirhams over six days.

In Hanafi sales law, the precise wording of a deferred payment contract determines the schedule of installments. This lesson examines a case from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq where the seller sets the term as 'each day' using the word 'kull'. The text works through the resulting calculations, revealing an accumulation pattern that may seem counterintuitive at first. We will walk through the reasoning step by step to understand how the obligations unfold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract terminology
The contract uses the word 'kull' (every) to set the term as 'each day' for one dirham. In Hanafi fiqh, 'kull' necessitates repetition (takrar). Thus, each day brings a new obligation of one dirham, and previous obligations have their own terms.
The word 'kull' is key; it distinguishes this from a fixed monthly installment.
2
Determine Day 1 obligation
On the first day, one dirham becomes due because the term for that day has arrived. The debt for that dirham is payable at the end of day 1.
3
Analyze Day 2 obligation
On the second day, the text states 'one dirham due with the arrival of the second day and two dirhams due with the arrival of two days.' This totals three dirhams. The reasoning: the second day is both 'from each day' (the new day) and 'from each two days' (the completion of a pair), so an extra dirham is due from the pair. The first dirham (from day 1) is also due now, making three.
This overlapping is the unique effect of 'kull'.
4
Continue the pattern for Days 3-6
Day 3: only one new dirham due (no pair completion). Day 4: like Day 2, three dirhams (one for day 4, two for the completion of two days from the pair). Day 5: one dirham. Day 6: the remaining one dirham to complete the total of ten dirhams.
The pattern: odd days (1,3,5) give one; even days (2,4) give three; day 6 gives one.
5
Derive the general rule
When the term is 'each day' with 'kull', the payments accumulate in a repeating pattern: 1, 3, 1, 3, 1, 1, then the cycle may repeat for longer debts. The key is that on the second day and every second day thereafter, an extra dirham is due from the pair of days.
This calculation ensures the total paid equals the principal over the correct number of days.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جعل اليوم أجلاً للدرهم الواحد بكلمة كل الموجبة للتكرار فكلما جاء يوم يلزمه درهم، وفي اليوم الثاني يلزمه درهم بمجيء اليوم الثاني ودرهمان بمجيء يومين ودرهم في اليوم الثالث لحلول نجم آخر ولم يحل للدرهمين أجل آخر، وفي الرابع يلزمه ثلاثة واحد بمضي الرابع ودرهمان بمجيء أجل آخر للدرهمين، وفي الخامس يلزمه درهم بمجيء الخامس ولم يحل للدرهمين أجل آخر بقي من العشرة واحد يعطيه في اليوم السادس اهـ قوله (بكلمة كلما الموجبة للتكرار ) صوابه بكلمة (كل) والذي في الخانية بكلمة توجب التكرار وقد علل في التجنيس والولوالجية بقوله لأن اليوم الثاني من كل يوم ومن كل يومين فيعطى فيه ثلاثة واليوم الرابع بمنزلة اليوم الثاني بقي في اليوم السادس عليه درهم فيعطيه
He made the day a term for one dirham by the word 'every' which necessitates repetition; so each time a day comes, one dirham is due. On the second day, one dirham is due with the arrival of the second day, and two dirhams with the arrival of two days. On the third day, one dirham due to the arrival of another installment, while the two dirhams have no other term due. On the fourth, three dirhams are due: one with the passing of the fourth, and two with the arrival of another term for the two dirhams. On the fifth, one dirham due with the fifth, while the two dirhams have no other term due; one remains from the ten, he gives it on the sixth day. End. His statement (with the word 'kullama' necessitating repetition) is correct with the word 'kull'. What is in al-Khaniyah is 'with a word necessitating repetition'. In al-Tajnis and al-Walwaljiyah, it is reasoned: because the second day is from each day and from each two days, so he gives three on it, and the fourth day is like the second, so on the sixth day he has one dirham due, he gives it.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a case from the Hanafi works on deferred payment. It considers a scenario where someone sets a daily installment of one dirham by using the word 'kull' (every), which necessitates repetition. The commentary works through the calculation over several days, showing how accumulated payments are due. It also includes a marginal correction noting that the correct wording is 'kull' not 'kullama' as found in some copies.

Key Terms
أجل (ajal): Term or deadline for payment of a debt.
كل (kull): Every; used to indicate repetition such as daily installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi example, how many dirhams are due on the fourth day?
A) One dirham
B) Two dirhams
C) Three dirhams
D) Four dirhams

Key Takeaway
The word 'kull' in a deferred payment contract necessitates overlapping terms, leading to a specific calculation pattern: 1,3,1,3,1,1 dirhams over six days for a ten-dirham debt.

Liability for Broken Counterfeit Coins in Sale
Ruling on seller's liability for broken counterfeit coins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a seller is not liable for breaking counterfeit coins and apply the condition of 'no loss' to analogous cases.

Core Idea
Breaking counterfeit coins causes no loss; thus no liability.

In a sale, if a buyer hands over coins that appear genuine and the seller breaks them to verify, only to discover they are counterfeit, the seller is not liable for the broken coins. This ruling, recorded in al-Waqi'at, rests on a key principle of Hanafi fiqh: liability only arises when genuine property is damaged. Since counterfeit coins hold no legal value, no loss is incurred, and the seller may simply return the pieces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The buyer pays with coins that appear sound (sahih). The seller breaks them to check their authenticity, and they turn out to be counterfeit (nabhraja).
The coins were initially thought genuine but were actually worthless.
2
Identify the ruling
The seller is not liable for the broken coins. He may return the pieces to the buyer without any compensation.
The text explicitly states: 'There is no liability on him because he did not cause any loss.'
3
Identify the legal basis
The basis is the absence of loss: counterfeit coins have no value, so breaking them did not damage the buyer's legitimate property.
In Hanafi law, liability (damān) requires damage to a legally protected property (māl mutaqawwim).
4
Apply the principle
If the coins had been genuine, the seller would be liable. The critical condition is the value of the item broken. Apply this to any property that turns out to be legally worthless.
For example, testing a fake banknote with a marker pen does not incur liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الواقعات : اشترى شيئاً ودفع إلى البائع دراهم صحاحاً فكسرها البائع فوجدها نبهرجة فردها فلا شيء عليه لأنه لم يتلف عليه شيء، وكذا لو دفع إليه إنسان (۱) . قوله وفي الواقعات إلى آخر هذه العبارة كتب عليه المحشى بخطه أنه زائد فأثبتناه ونبهنا عليه اهـ مصححه .
And in al-Waqi'at: Someone bought something and gave the seller sound coins; the seller broke them and found them to be nabhraja (counterfeit), so he returned them. There is no liability on the seller because he did not cause any loss to him. Similarly, if someone gave him (coins). His statement: 'And in al-Waqi'at...' The commentator wrote in his own hand that it is additional, so we have included it and brought attention to it. Corrector.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage from al-Waqi'at presents a ruling: if a buyer pays with sound coins and the seller breaks them to check, discovering they are counterfeit, the seller may return them without liability because he did not damage the buyer's property (the coins were already worthless). The marginal note indicates some scholars consider this passage an addition to the text, but it is included.

Key Terms
نبهرجة (nabhraja): Counterfeit or defective coins that are not legally valid currency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the seller not liable for the broken counterfeit coins?
A) Because the buyer allowed it
B) Because the coins were not genuine property
C) Because the seller is always free of liability
D) Because the buyer did not complain

Key Takeaway
A seller who breaks coins that turn out counterfeit is not liable because no loss of valuable property occurred.

Classification of Terms in Deferred Payment Sales
Classification and ruling on deferred payment terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify payment terms as known/unknown and close/varying, and state the Hanafi ruling on deferring a debt or a specific item with an unknown term, including the effect of annulment.

Core Idea
Unknown terms invalidate deferred payment, but annulment before the term or separation may rectify.

In Islamic sales, certainty of the payment term is essential. This passage from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj classifies terms and explains when a deferred sale is valid or can be corrected. You will learn to distinguish between known and unknown terms, and between close and varying ignorance, and apply the rulings on annulment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon (deferring payment in a sale) and the hukm level (invalid/corrupt for unknown terms).
The passage distinguishes between debt (dayn) and specific item (ayn) as the price.
2
Classify the term
Using the passage's classification, decide if the term is known (years/months/days) or unknown. If unknown, further classify as close (approximate time known) or varying (no approximation).
Examples: harvest = close; rain = varying.
3
Apply the ruling
For unknown terms (both types) on a debt, the sale is not permissible (invalid). For a specific item, the sale becomes invalid even if the term is known. For close ignorance, annulment before the term or before rescission due to corruption can make it valid; for varying ignorance, annulment before separation (tafarruq) can make it valid.
The annulment is done by the buyer cancelling the deferred term.
4
Note the special case of Nawruz
From al-Khaniyah: if the parties know the exact date of Nawruz, the term is considered known and the sale is permissible. This shows that context can transform an apparently unknown term into a known one.
Apply the general principle: knowledge removes ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي السراج الوهاج: الآجال على ضربين : معلومة ومجهولة والمجهولة على ضربين متقاربة ومتفاوتة. فالمعلومة السنون والشهور والأيام والمجهولة متقاربة كالحصاد والدياس والنيروز والمهرجان وقدوم الحاج وخروجهم والجذاذ والقطاف وصوم النصارى وفطرهم. والمتفاوتة كهبوب الريح وإلى أن تمطر السماء وإلى قدوم فلان وإلى الميسرة فتأجيل الثمن الدين المجهول بنوعيه لا يجوز وإن كان الثمن عيناً فسد بالتأجيل ولو معلوماً، وإذا أجل الدين أجلاً مجهولاً بجهالة متقاربة ثم أبطله المشتري قبل محله وقبل فسخه للفساد انقلب جائزاً، وإن مضت المدة قبل إبطاله تأكد فساده . وإن كانت جهالته متفاوتة فإن أبطله المشتري قبل التفرق انقلب جائزاً اهـ. قوله : (والنيروز والمهرجان) قال في الخانية : رجل اشترى شيئاً بثمن إلى النيروز ذكر في الأصل أنه لا يجوز قالوا: هذا إذا لم يعلم البائع والمشتري بما بقي إلى النيروز فإن علما جاز .اهـ. وسيأتي متناً في باب البيع الفاسد.
And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: Terms are of two types: known and unknown. The unknown is of two types: close and varying. The known are years, months, and days. The unknown close are like harvest, threshing, Nawruz, Mihrajan, the arrival of pilgrims and their departure, fruit-plucking, grape-picking, the fasts of Christians and their breaking of fast. The varying are like blowing of wind, until the sky rains, until the coming of so-and-so, and until easy circumstances. So deferring the price (when the debt is for a deferred payment) with an unknown term, whether close or varying, is not permissible. If the price is a specific item (ayn), the sale becomes invalid by deferment even if the term is known. If one defers a debt with an unknown term of a close ignorance, then the buyer annuls it before its due time and before his termination due to corruption, it becomes valid. If the term passes before his annulment, its corruption is confirmed. If the ignorance is varying, then if the buyer annuls it before separation, it becomes valid. End. His statement: (Nawruz and Mihrajan) said in al-Khaniyah: A man bought something with a price deferred to Nawruz. It is mentioned in al-Asl that it is not permissible. They said: This is if the seller and buyer do not know the time remaining until Nawruz; if they know, it is permissible. It will come in the chapter of invalid sale.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj classifies terms (ajal) into known and unknown. Known terms are precise periods like years, months, days. Unknown terms are divided into close (where the time is approximately known, such as harvest time) and varying (where the time is unpredictable, such as wind or rain). The ruling: deferring a debt with any unknown term invalidates the sale (if the price is a debt) or renders it void (if the price is a specific item). However, if the buyer annuls the contract before the term arrives or before separation (depending on the type of ignorance), the contract becomes valid. The marginal note adds a case from al-Khaniyah about the Persian festivals Nawruz and Mihrajan, where knowledge of the exact date matters.

Key Terms
أجل (ajal): Term or deadline for payment; known (معلوم) or unknown (مجهول).
مجهولة متقاربة (majhulah mutaqaribah): Unknown but close term where the approximate time is known, e.g., harvest season.
مجهولة متفاوتة (majhulah mutafawitah): Unknown and varying term with no approximate time, e.g., 'when it rains'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer defers payment of a debt with a close unknown term (e.g., 'until harvest') and later annuls the term before the harvest arrives, what is the ruling?
A) The sale remains invalid.
B) The sale becomes valid.
C) The sale becomes makruh.
D) The sale becomes void.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, deferred payment terms must be known; otherwise, the sale is corrupt but can be rectified by timely annulment.

Death of Selling Agent – Who Claims the Price?
First issue from al-Waqi'at on agent's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default transfer of the right to demand the price upon the agent's death and identify the condition under which the owner can claim directly.

Core Idea
Upon the agent's death, the right to the price passes to his heirs, not the owner.

When a person authorized to sell (ma'dhun) dies after making a sale, the right to demand the price from the buyer does not revert to the principal (owner) as one might expect. Instead, it becomes a right of the agent's estate. This rule arises because the agency relationship ends with death, and the price becomes a debt owed to the agent's heirs. Only if the owner can prove that the agent had already received the price does the owner acquire a claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the event
Who are the parties? The agent (ma'dhun lah), the principal (owner), the buyer, and the agent's heir. The event: the agent sells and then dies before collecting the price.
The agent is a general agent for sale, not a specific agent.
2
State the default rule
Upon the agent's death, the right to demand the price transfers to the agent's heir or executor, not to the principal. The principal cannot claim from the buyer directly.
This is because the debt is owed to the agent, not the principal, once the sale is concluded.
3
Identify the exception
The principal can claim from the heir if he proves that the agent had already received the price (qabd). Without proof, the principal has no claim on the heir.
Proof of receipt is key: the owner must show that the agent had collected the money.
4
Apply the rule to a similar case
The same rule applies to one of two partners in a mufawadah (unrestricted partnership) – if he dies, receipt of the price goes to his executor, not the surviving partner automatically.
The partnership agreement does not override the inheritance rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهنا مسائل في الواقعات متعلقة بالثمن أحببت ذكرها هنا : الأولى المأذون له في البيع إذا باع ومات فجاء المالك فليس له مطالبة وارث البائع ما لم يثبت قبضه ولا يقبل قول المشتري عليه ولا مطالبة له على المشتري إلا برضا الوارث لأن الوكيل بالبيع إذا مات لا ينتقل حق المطالبة بالثمن إلى موكله وإنما ينتقل إلى وارثه أو وصيه إن كان، فإن لم يكن نصب القاضي عنه وصياً ليقبض، وكأحد المتفاوضين إذا مات كان قبض الثمن إلى وصيه الثاني بياع عنده بضائع للناس أمروه ببيعها فباعها ونقد الثمن من ماله على أن يكون الثمن له فأفلس المشتري كان للبائع أن يسترد من المالك ما دفعه إليه. الثالثة بايع أقواماً ثم مات وعليهم ديون ولم يعرف له وارث فأخذ السلطان ديونه ثم ظهر له وارث لا يبرأ الغرماء وعليهم الأداء ثانياً إلى الوارث اهـ. قوله (لا يبرأ الغرماء الخ) قال الرملي وترجع الغرماء على السلطان فإن لم يدفع لهم فقد ظلم ولهم المطالبة في الآخرة
And here are issues in al-Waqi'at related to the price that I wanted to mention here. First: If a person authorized to sell sells and dies, and the owner comes, the owner has no right to demand from the heir of the seller unless he proves receipt, and the statement of the buyer against him is not accepted, and he has no claim against the buyer except with the consent of the heir, because when the agent for sale dies, the right to demand the price does not transfer to his principal, but rather transfers to his heir or executor if there is one. If there is none, the judge appoints an executor for him to receive. Similarly, if one of the partners in a mutual partnership (mufawadah) dies, receipt of the price goes to his executor. Second: A seller who has goods of people, they ordered him to sell them, so he sold them and paid the price from his own money on condition that the price would be his (i.e., he advanced the payment), then the buyer became insolvent, the seller has the right to reclaim from the owner what he had paid to him. Third: A person sold to groups, then died, and they owed debts, and no heir was known for him, so the ruler took his debts. Then an heir appeared; the debtors are not discharged, and they must pay again to the heir. End. His statement: ('The debtors are not discharged' etc.) Al-Ramli said: The debtors can seek recourse from the ruler; if he does not pay them, he has wronged them, and they can demand in the Hereafter.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage presents three cases from al-Waqi'at concerning the price (thaman) after death. The first case: If an agent (ma'dhun) sells and dies, the right to demand the price passes to his heir or executor, not to the principal (owner). The owner must prove receipt to claim from the heir. The second case: If a seller receives goods on consignment, sells, and pays the price from his own pocket (advancing), then if the buyer becomes insolvent, the seller can reclaim from the goods' owner. The third case: If a creditor dies without known heir, the ruler collects; but if an heir later appears, debtors must pay again. Al-Ramli adds that debtors can then claim from the ruler.

Key Terms
مأذون له في البيع (ma'dhun lahu fi al-bay'): A person authorized to sell (general agent).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Ali appoints Zaid as agent to sell his smartphone. Zaid sells to Bilal for $300. Zaid dies before collecting. Bilal has not paid. Who has the right to demand payment from Bilal?
A) Ali, because he is the owner.
B) Zaid's heirs, because the debt is owed to Zaid's estate.
C) The judge, because there is no one else.
D) No one, the debt is extinguished.

Key Takeaway
When the selling agent dies, the right to the price goes to his heirs; the principal cannot claim without proof of receipt.

Seller Advances Price – Insolvent Buyer – Reclaim from Owner
Second issue from al-Waqi'at on seller's advance payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the seller's right to reclaim the price from the owner when he advances payment from his own pocket and the buyer becomes insolvent.

Core Idea
A seller who advances the price to the owner can reclaim it if the buyer becomes insolvent.

The second issue deals with a consignment sale where the seller advances the price from his own funds. This creates a loan-like relationship between seller and owner. If the original buyer fails to pay (insolvent), the seller can recover the advanced amount from the owner because the condition that the price would be his (the seller's) is not fulfilled.

Guided Path
1
Understand the transaction structure
The seller (bayya') has goods belonging to people (the owners). The owners ordered him to sell. He sells the goods and pays the price from his own money, on condition that the price becomes his (i.e., he keeps the sale proceeds as his own and owes the owners their goods' value?). Actually, the text says 'on condition that the price would be his' – meaning he advances the sale price to the owners, so the owners get their money immediately, and the seller now has a claim against the buyer.
This is a form of istighlal or financing arrangement.
2
Identify the insolvency event
After the sale, the buyer becomes insolvent (muflis) and cannot pay the price to the seller.
The buyer's insolvency is a risk that shifts.
3
State the seller's right
The seller has the right to reclaim from the owner the amount he had paid to the owner.
Because the condition (that the price would be his) is not realized – the buyer did not pay.

Ruler Collects Debts – Heir Appears – Debtors Must Pay Again
Third issue from al-Waqi'at on ruler's collection and later heir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when the ruler collects debts of a person with no known heir and an heir later appears, and identify the consequence for the debtors and the ruler.

Core Idea
Debtors are not discharged even if the ruler collects; they must pay again to the heir.

The third issue concerns a deceased creditor with debts owed to him but no known heir. The ruler (sultan) steps in to collect those debts. If later an heir emerges, the debtors are not considered discharged by their payment to the ruler. They must pay again to the heir. Al-Ramli adds that the debtors can then seek recourse from the ruler, who will be liable in the Hereafter if he does not repay them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A person dies with debts owed to him (he is a creditor). No heir is known. The ruler (sultan) takes possession of those debts (i.e., collects them from the debtors).
The ruler acts as a caretaker of estate without heirs.
2
State the event: heir appears
Later, an heir of the deceased appears and claims the inheritance.
The heir is the rightful successor.
3
Apply the ruling: debtors not discharged
The debtors are not freed from their debts by paying the ruler. They must pay again to the heir.
Because the ruler's collection was not with proper authority to transfer ownership permanently.
4
Identify the debtors' recourse
The debtors can then claim the money back from the ruler. If the ruler does not return it, he has wronged them, and they can hold him accountable in the Hereafter.
This is from al-Ramli's commentary.

The Meaning of 'Hal' in Debt Transactions
Defines 'hal' and refines its fiqh meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the term 'hal' as used in Hanafi fiqh and explain the distinction between 'hal' and 'mu'ajjal' using the critical refinement from al-Nahr.

Core Idea
A debt is 'hal' when it is immediately payable, regardless of a prior deferment.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the linguistic and technical meaning of 'hal' (due) in the context of debts. The author cites al-Misbah and al-Qamus but notes that the strict linguistic sense is not intended in the legal text. The refinement by al-Nahr draws a critical distinction that every Hanafi student must grasp: 'hal al-dayn' (debt becoming due) is distinct from 'bai' bihal' (sale on credit).

Guided Path
1
Extract the linguistic definition
From al-Misbah: 'Hal al-dayn' means the debt's term has ended; the debt becomes due. The term 'hal' is only said after a postponement according to this source.
The root 'h-l-l' carries the sense of 'becoming permissible' but in this context 'ending of term'.
2
Identify the refinement
The commentator immediately states that this strict linguistic sense (only after prior deferment) is not intended in the book (Kanz al-Daqa'iq). This alerts the reader that fiqh usage is broader.
The phrase 'laysa bi murad fi al-kitab' signals a critical refinement.
3
Analyze the distinction from al-Nahr
Al-Nahr points out a clear difference between 'hal al-dayn' (the debt became due) and 'ba'ahu bihal' (sold it on credit). The former is about the debt itself becoming incumbent; the latter refers to a sale where the price is deferred but the contract is valid.
This distinction prevents confusion between a debt's status and a sale's payment term.
4
Confirm the fiqh meaning
Al-Maghrib and al-Qamus confirm that 'hal' means the debt is obligatory and binding, and 'al-dayn al-hal' is simply the opposite of 'mu'ajjal' (deferred). Thus, in fiqh, 'hal' denotes immediate payability without any necessary prior deferment.
The key takeaway: 'hal' = due/payable now.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المصباح : حل الدين يحل - بالكسر - حلولاً انتهى أجله فهو حال وأجل الشيء مدته ووقته الذي يحل فيه، وهو مصدر أجل الشيء أجلاً من باب تعب وأجل أجولاً من باب قعد لغة، وأجلته تأجيلاً جعلت له أجلاً .اهـ. فظاهره لا يقال حل إلا بعد تأجيل وليس بمراد في الكتاب، وفي القاموس : حل الدين صار حالاً. قوله : فظاهره لا يقال حل إلا بعد تأجيل الخ قال في النهر : فيه نظر للفرق البين بين حل الدين وباعه بحال، ويدل عليه ما في المغرب حل الدين وجب ولزم والدين الحال خلاف المؤجل.
And in al-Misbah: Hal al-dayn means it becomes due (with kasr) 'halulan' meaning its term has ended, so it is 'hal' (due). The term of something is its duration and time in which it becomes due. It is the masdar of 'ajala' something 'ajalan' from the form of ta'iba, and 'ajala yajulu' from the form of qa'ada as a language. 'Ajaltuhu ta'jilan' means I set a term for it. End. So its apparent meaning is that 'hal' is only said after a postponement, and that is not intended in the book. In al-Qamus: 'Hal al-dayn' means it became due. His statement: 'So its apparent meaning...' Al-Nahr commented: There is consideration due to the clear difference between 'hal al-dayn' (the debt became due) and 'ba'ahu bihal' (sold it on credit). This is supported by al-Maghrib: 'Hal al-dayn' means it became obligatory and binding, and 'al-dayn al-hal' is the opposite of deferred.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage provides a linguistic definition of the term 'hal' (due) from al-Misbah and al-Qamus, clarifying its meaning in the context of debts. The commentator notes that the apparent meaning from al-Misbah suggests 'hal' implies a prior postponement, but this is not intended in the legal text. Al-Nahr's marginal note refines this by distinguishing between a debt becoming due and a sale on credit, citing al-Maghrib that 'hal' means obligated and binding, and 'dayn hal' is the opposite of deferred.

Key Terms
حل (hal): A debt or term becoming due; end of the deferment period.
حال (hal): Due, payable immediately; not deferred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The debt is postponed to a future date.
B) The debt is due and payable immediately.
C) The debt is sold on credit.
D) The debt is forgiven.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, 'hal' means a debt is due and payable immediately, without any requirement of prior deferment.

Specification of Currency in Sales: Default to Common Currency
Explains default currency rules and validity when currencies differ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default currency rule for an absolutely mentioned price, explain the validity of a sale when currencies differ, and apply the remedy of later specification.

Core Idea
Absolute price defaults to common currency of the contract's place; differing currencies require specification or sale is invalid.

When you agree to buy something for a certain number of dirhams, but don't say which kind, what happens? The answer depends on custom and the place of contract. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling: the absolute price refers to the common currency of that locality, and if the currencies are mixed and not specified, the sale is invalid until clarified in the session.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling for Absolute Mention of Price
The author states that when the price is mentioned absolutely (e.g., 'ten dirhams'), it automatically refers to the common currency (al-naqd al-ghalib) of the place where the contract is made. Identify this as the default ruling.
The default applies to both dirhams and dinars.
2
Understand the Role of Custom as Dalil
The ruling is based on custom (urf). Two types are discussed: verbal custom (urf qawli) where the word 'dirhams' is commonly understood to mean a specific type, and operational custom (urf 'amali) where the actual dealing is predominantly with that type. Both make the absolute term definite.
The Hanafi school treats custom as a valid source to specify general terms.
3
Apply to the Scenario of Differing Currencies
When currencies differ (e.g., multiple types with equal circulation but different values), the sale is invalid (fasid) due to ignorance (jahalah) leading to dispute. The passage gives examples from Cairo: banduqi, qa'itabayi, etc.
Invalidity is lifted if one party specifies the type in the same session and the other agrees.
4
Recognize the Remedy of Later Specification
The author clarifies that specification can occur after the contract but within the same session. This is considered like contemporaneous specification because the defect is removed before it is established.
If specification is done at the time of contract, it's no longer an 'absolute' case; the passage focuses on absolute mentions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: ('And its absolute mention [of the price] refers to the common currency') means the absolute price by specifying its amount and type without its description. Restriction to a locality: if the sale takes place with ten dirhams or dinars, it refers to the common currency of that locality because it is customary, so the absolute is referred to it. If the absolute usage of the name 'dirhams' in custom is specific to them while other dirhams exist, then it is specification of dirhams by verbal custom, which is a case of abandoning the literal meaning by indication of custom. If dealing is predominantly with them, it is abandoning by indication of habit. Both are obligatory to observe to ensure validity and not to disregard the speech of a rational person; thus in Fath al-Qadir. But in al-Tahrir, he affirmed that habit is operational custom and that the issue of dirhams is from verbal custom. In Sharh al-Majma': If he sells to a specific term and stipulates that the buyer give him any currency that is current on that day, the sale is invalid. Taj al-Shari'ah mentioned that by 'locality' is meant the place where the sale occurred, not the place of the contracting parties. His statement: ('And if the currencies differ, the sale is invalid if not specified') means the sale is invalid due to the ignorance leading to dispute. If the ignorance is removed by one of them specifying in the session and the other agrees, it becomes valid because the defect is removed before its establishment, so it becomes like a contemporaneous specification. By 'specification' in his speech is meant later specification, because contemporaneous specification takes the issue out of its subject matter, as its subject matter is absolute. Understand. By 'difference of currencies' is meant difference in their value while they are equal in circulation, like banduqi, qa'itabayi, salimi, maghribi, and ghuri in Cairo now. The conclusion is that the issue is fourfold because it is either...
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage discusses the specification of currency in sales. It explains that when the price is mentioned absolutely (e.g., 'ten dirhams'), it refers to the common currency of the place of the contract due to custom. Two types of custom are distinguished: verbal custom (urf qawli) and operational custom (urf 'amali). The commentator cites Fath al-Qadir and al-Tahrir. It also addresses the case where currencies differ (multiple types in circulation); then the sale is invalid unless the type is specified. However, if one party specifies in the session and the other agrees, it becomes valid. Examples of different currencies in Cairo (banduqi, etc.) are given. The passage ends with a note that the issue is fourfold, which likely refers to all combinations of specifying or not specifying for the two parties.

Key Terms
عرف قولي (urf qawli): Verbal custom where a word is used in a specific sense due to common usage, overriding literal meaning.
عرف عملي (urf 'amali): Operational custom where behavior or practice gives meaning to a term.
نقد غالب (naqd ghalib): The common or prevalent currency of a locality.
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance regarding the subject matter that leads to dispute, invalidating a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Any dirhams

Key Takeaway
Absolute price defaults to the common currency of the contract's place; if currencies differ, the sale is invalid unless specification occurs in the same session.

Resolving Disputes over Price and Deferment: Imam Muhammad's Reconciliation
Unique ruling from Imam Muhammad on conflicting testimonies in sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Imam Muhammad's method of reconciling conflicting testimonies in a sale dispute over price and term, and apply the calculation method to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
When both parties bring witnesses, Imam Muhammad accepts both and combines them into a single payment schedule.

This passage presents a remarkable case from Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani, where buyer and seller disagree on both the price (50 vs 100 dirhams) and the deferment period (20 months vs 10 months), yet both produce witnesses. Instead of rejecting one claim, Imam Muhammad accepts both testimonies and reconciles the differences into a single payment plan. Understanding this case sharpens your appreciation for Hanafi fiqh's nuanced approach to evidence and equity in contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting claims
Note the buyer's claim (50 dirhams over 20 months at 2.5/month) and seller's claim (100 dirhams over 10 months at 10/month).
2
Understand the legal problem
Normally, if both parties have contradictory evidence, one might be preferred, but here both are accepted.
3
Follow Imam Muhammad's reconciliation
He constructs a payment schedule: first six months at 10 dirhams (seller's rate), seventh month at 7.5 dirhams, then 2.5 dirhams per month until total reaches 100.
4
Analyze the logic
The schedule preserves the seller's total amount (100) but spreads it over the buyer's claimed period (20 months), with a higher portion early on.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وذكر في الظهيرية من باب الاختلاف الخ) هي على ما في منتخب الظهيرية للإمام العيني قال محمد بن الحسن في رجلين تبايعا شيئاً واختلفا في الثمن فقال المشتري اشتريته بخمسين درهماً إلى عشرين شهراً على أن أؤدي إليك كل شهر درهمين ونصفاً وقال البائع بعتك بمائة درهم إلى عشرة أشهر على أن تؤدي إلي كل شهر عشرة دراهم وأقاما البينة قال محمد تقبل شهادتهما ويأخذ البائع من المشتري ستة أشهر كل شهر عشرة وفي الشهر السابع سبعة ونصفاً، ثم يأخذ بعد ذلك كل شهر درهمين ونصفاً إلى أن له مائة وهذه مسألة عجيبة يتم
His statement: (And it is mentioned in al-Dhahiriyyah from the chapter of differences, etc.) It is as in Muntakhab al-Dhahiriyyah by al-'Ayni. Muhammad ibn al-Hasan said: In the case of two men who bought and sold something and disagreed over the price; the buyer said: 'I bought it for fifty dirhams over twenty months, with me paying you two and a half dirhams each month.' The seller said: 'I sold it to you for one hundred dirhams over ten months, with you paying me ten dirhams each month.' They each brought witnesses. Muhammad said: Their testimonies are accepted. The seller takes from the buyer for six months, ten dirhams each month; in the seventh month, seven and a half dirhams; then after that, two and a half dirhams each month until he receives one hundred. This is an amazing case. End.

This passage from al-Dhahiriyyah presents a dispute between buyer and seller about the price and deferment terms. Both bring witnesses, and Imam Muhammad accepts both testimonies. The calculation splits the difference: for the first six months, the seller receives the buyer's claimed lower monthly payment? Actually the text says: 'the seller takes from the buyer for six months, ten dirhams each month' which is the seller's claimed monthly payment for the first six months (since seller says ten months, buyer says twenty months, the first six months overlap?), then the seventh month 7.5, then 2.5 each month until total 100. This is a unique calculation that reconciles both claims.

Key Terms
اختلاف (ikhtilaf): Disagreement between buyer and seller regarding the terms of the sale.
إقامة البينة (iqamat al-bayyinah): Presenting evidence or witnesses to support one's claim.

Debt Not Discharged by Ruler's Collection When Heir Appears
Ruling on debts collected by ruler before heir appears.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a ruler collects debts from debtors of a deceased with no known heir, and a heir later appears, and explain why the debtors are not discharged.

Core Idea
Payment to the ruler does not discharge the debt when an heir later appears.

This lesson presents a specific scenario from sales (buyūʿ) where a creditor dies, leaving debts owed to him and no known heir. The ruler (sulṭān) collects the debts from the debtors. Later, an heir emerges. The ruling is clear: the debtors are not released from their obligation and must pay again to the heir. This underscores the principle that debts are only validly discharged when paid to the rightful owner or his legal representative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their roles
Recognize the seller (deceased creditor), the debtors (ghuramā'), the ruler, and the heir. Understand the sequence: sale on credit, death with debt owed, no known heir, ruler collects, heir appears.
2
State the central ruling
The text explicitly says 'lā yabra'u al-ghuramā' wa 'alayhim al-adā' thāniyan ilā al-wārith' – the debtors are not discharged and must pay again to the heir.
This is a categorical statement from the matn.
3
Understand the rationale
The ruler's collection was done in the absence of an heir, but when the heir appears, the ruler's authority over the estate ends. The debtors' payment to the ruler was not a valid transfer of ownership to the rightful owner, so the debt remains.
Compare with cases where the heir is known from the start.
4
Apply to a new scenario
If a similar situation occurs today (e.g., government collects debts from a deceased's debtors, then a legitimate heir is found), the same ruling applies: the debtors must pay the heir again.
Ensure the debtors have not been unjustly burdened – they can seek reimbursement from the ruler if possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة بايع أقواماً ثم مات وعليهم ديون ولم يعرف له وارث فأخذ السلطان ديونه ثم ظهر له وارث لا يبرأ الغرماء وعليهم الأداء ثانياً إلى الوارث
The third: If a person sold to people, then died while they owed him debts, and he had no known heir, so the ruler took his debts, then an heir appeared, the debtors are not discharged and they must pay again to the heir.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the text. It presents a scenario where a person sells on credit, dies with debts owed to him and no known heir. The ruler (sultan) collects the debts from the debtors. Later, an heir emerges. The ruling is that the debtors are not released from liability; they must pay again to the heir. This is because the ruler's collection was not a valid transfer of ownership to the estate without an heir. The debt remains the responsibility of the debtors until the rightful heir receives it. This underscores the principle that debts are not discharged by payment to an unauthorized party.

Key Terms
الغرماء (al-ghuramā'): The debtors who owe money to the deceased.
السلطان (al-sulṭān): The ruler or authority who manages the estate when there is no known heir.
الوارث (al-wārith): The heir who appears after the ruler collected the debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The debtors are discharged and do not pay again.
B) The debtors must pay half to the ruler and half to the heir.
C) The debtors must pay again to the heir.
D) The heir must return the money to the debtors.

Key Takeaway
Debtors are not discharged when they pay the ruler if an heir appears later; they must pay the heir again, preserving the heir's right.

Default Currency and Currency Ambiguity in Sales
Explanation of two currency rulings with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for interpreting an absolute price in sales and explain when a sale is void due to currency ambiguity.

Core Idea
Custom determines the default currency; uncertainty that leads to dispute invalidates the contract.

When a price is stated in absolute terms, such as 'ten dirhams,' Hanafi law turns to the prevalent currency of the place to resolve ambiguity. But if multiple currencies circulate with differing values, the sale becomes void unless the currency is specified. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind these rules, the role of custom, and the nuances when currencies differ in value or circulation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Default Currency Rule
Identify that when a price is mentioned absolutely (e.g., 'ten dirhams') without specifying the type, it refers to the prevalent currency (al-naqd al-ghālib) of the country where the contract is made. This is based on custom.
The rule applies even if the literal name 'dirham' could refer to multiple types.
2
Recognize the Role of Custom
Distinguish between verbal custom (al-'urf al-qawlī) and practical custom (al-'urf al-'amalī). Verbal custom specifies the term's meaning; practical custom reflects habitual dealing. Both are valid to determine the intended currency.
Hanafis treat both as authoritative to avoid voiding the contract.
3
Identify When Differing Currencies Cause Invalidity
If the sale involves multiple currencies that differ in value (even if equal in circulation) and no specification is made, the sale is void due to ignorance leading to dispute (al-jahāla al-mufdiyya ilā al-munāza'a). However, if the currency is specified later in the same session with mutual consent, it becomes valid.
The invalidity is fasid (voidable) initially, but can be cured.
4
Analyze the Four Scenarios and the Thunā'ī/Thulāthī Example
Work through the four scenarios of equality/difference in value and circulation. Invalidity occurs only when currencies are equal in circulation but differ in value. Then examine the thunā'ī/thulāthī example: the correct view (from al-Hidāyah) is that they are equal in value per piece, so the buyer may choose which to pay. Understand the commentator's resolution of the apparent contradiction.
This example illustrates the importance of precise understanding of the currency's nature.

بدرهم وأطلق لفظ الدرهم وكانت الدراهم بعضها ثنائية وبعضها ثلاثية صح وخير المشتري، ويدل على أن هذا مراد الهداية ما في الجوهرة من قوله فالثنائي ما كان منه اثنان دائقاً والثلاث ما كان الثلاثة منه دانقاً، ففي هذه الصورة يجوز البيع إذا أطلق اسم الدراهم لأنه لا منازعة ولا اختلاف في المالية .اهـ. قلت : ومثله في زماننا الذهب فإنه يكون كاملاً ويكون
His statement: 'And its absolute [coin] refers to the prevalent currency' means the absolute price when its quantity and type are stated but not described. The restriction to a country: if the sale is for ten dirhams or dinars, it refers to the prevalent coin of that country because it is customary, so the absolute is referred to it. If the name 'dirham' in custom is specific to them even though other dirhams exist, this is specification of dirhams by verbal custom, which is a type of abandoning the literal meaning due to custom. If dealing is generally with them, it is abandoning the literal meaning due to practical custom. Both are necessary to seek validity and not waste the speech of a rational person. This is from Fath al-Qadir. But in al-Tahrir, he asserts that custom is practical custom and that the dirham issue is one of verbal custom. In Sharh al-Majma': If he sells for a fixed term and stipulates that the buyer give any currency that circulates that day, the sale is void. Taj al-Shari'ah stated that what is meant by 'country' is the country where the sale takes place, not the country of the parties. And in al-Zahiriyyah, from the chapter on disputes between seller and buyer, there is a subtle issue. Muhammad ibn al-Hasan said: Two men exchanged something and disagreed on the price. The buyer said: I bought it for fifty dirhams on a twenty-month term, paying two and a half dirhams each month. The seller said: I sold it for one hundred dirhams on a ten-month term, paying ten dirhams each month. They produced evidence. Muhammad said: Their testimony is accepted. The seller takes from the buyer six months at ten dirhams each, in the seventh month seven and a half, then after that he takes each month two and a half dirhams until he has one hundred. This is a strange case. His statement: '(And if coins differ, it is void if not specified)' means the sale is void due to ignorance leading to dispute. If the ignorance is removed by specifying one of them in the session and the other agrees, it becomes valid because the invalidating factor is removed before it is established, so it is like a simultaneous specification. What is meant by 'specification' in his speech is subsequent specification, because simultaneous specification is outside the subject of the issue, since its subject is absolute. Understand this. What is meant by 'difference of coins' is difference in value with equal circulation, like the Bunduqi, Qaytabayi, Sulaymi, Maghribi, and Ghuri in Cairo now. The summary is that the issue is fourfold: either they are equal in circulation and value, or differ in both, or equal in one and differ in the other. Invalidity occurs in one case: equality in circulation and difference in value. Validity in three cases: when they differ in both circulation and value, it refers to the more common; when they differ in circulation and equal in value, it also refers to the more common; when they are equal in both. If the difference is only in name, like Egyptian and Damascene, the buyer may choose which to pay. If the seller requests one, the buyer may pay another because the seller's refusal to accept what the buyer pays without superiority is obstinacy. Hence we say that the coin is not specified in exchange contracts. Al-Hidayah gave the example of equality in value with the thunā'ī and thulāthī. Al-'Inayah criticized it as an invalid example because two of one type are a dāniq and three of the other are a dāniq, so they are not equal in value. But they could be equal in circulation. The thunā'ī and thulāthī are interpreted in al-Mi'rāj as in al-'Inayah. In Fath al-Qadir: thunā'ī and thulāthī are names of dirhams in their land with different values. Similarly, al-Ruknī and al-Khalīfī in gold, where al-Khalīfī was of higher value, and al-'Adālī is a name for dirhams. Al-Zayla'ī interpreted thunā'ī as two coins per dirham, and thulāthī as three coins per dirham. The summary: thunā'ī is two pieces of silver, either a dāniq or a dirham, and thulāthī is three pieces, either a dāniq or a dirham. If he sells a commodity for a dirham in a town where there are dirhams, the buyer may pay either two pieces of thunā'ī or three of thulāthī. The correct view is that of al-Hidayah about equality in value because the value of a thunā'ī piece equals the value of a thulāthī piece. It is not meant to refer to the piece itself such that it would be a case of differing values. However, if he sells something by the piece, it is void because a piece of thunā'ī is half a dirham and a piece of thulāthī is one-third of a dirham. This is what has occurred to me in solving this point, and I have not seen it from others. He restricted it to sale because in a will, if they differ in value but are equal in circulation, the will is executed with the least valuable coin; if they differ in circulation but are equal in value, the will is referred to the prevalent coin. In al-Bazzaziyyah from the book of claims: If he claims a weighed item, mentioning the type, gold or silver, even if struck, he says such-and-such dinars, Khwarazmian or Bukharan, good or bad. He needs to mention the description when currencies differ. If it is one currency, no. If there are multiple currencies all equal in circulation with no advantage of some over others, the sale is valid and the buyer may pay whichever he wishes. But one-third of a dirham? Two pieces and a dirham? Three? Choose? End. The author will mention the passage from al-Zahiriyyah in more detail in the book of claims in the chapter of dispute at the text: 'If they differ in the term or in the condition of option'. His statement: 'So the truth is what is in al-Hidayah' etc. Summary: Al-Hidayah meant that if he buys for a dirham and utters the word 'dirham' absolutely while dirhams are partly thunā'ī and partly thulāthī, it is valid and the buyer may choose. Evidence for this is from al-Jawharah: 'The thunā'ī is two of which are a dāniq, and the thulāthī is three of which are a dāniq.' In this case, the sale is valid if the name dirham is used absolutely because there is no dispute and no difference in value. End. I say: Likewise in our time, gold might be complete and might be...
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This is a commentary passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq explaining two related rulings from Kanz al-Daqā'iq. The first ruling: when a price is stated in absolute terms (e.g., 'ten dirhams') without specifying which type of dirham, it is interpreted as the prevalent currency of the place of sale. The commentator explains that this is based on both verbal custom (al-'urf al-qawlī) and practical custom (al-'urf al-'amalī), citing sources like Fath al-Qadīr and al-Tahrīr. The second ruling: if there are multiple currencies circulating and they differ in value or circulation, the sale is void unless the currency is specified at the time of contract. However, if specification is made later in the same session with mutual consent, the sale becomes valid. The commentator then provides a detailed analysis of four possible scenarios regarding equality/difference in value and circulation, noting that invalidity only occurs when currencies are equal in circulation but differ in value. The passage also includes a fascinating example from al-Zahiriyyah involving a dispute between buyer and seller over the price and installment terms, where Imam Muhammad gives a unique solution that partially accepts both claims. Further, the commentator discusses the example of thunā'ī and thulāthī dirhams from al-Hidāyah, resolving apparent contradictions in its interpretation. Throughout, the commentary emphasizes that the key factor is potential for dispute (al-jahāla al-mufdiyya ilā al-munāza'a) and that the ruling aims to prevent conflict. The passage concludes with a cross-reference to similar rules in wills and in the book of claims.

Key Terms
النقد الغالب (al-naqd al-ghālib): The prevalent or most commonly used currency in a given locality.
العرف القولي (al-'urf al-qawlī): Verbal custom: when the general usage of a term in speech indicates a specific meaning, even if not literal.
العرف العملي (al-'urf al-'amalī): Practical custom: when people habitually deal with a specific type of currency.
الجهالة المفضية إلى المنازعة (al-jahāla al-mufdiyya ilā al-munāza'a): Ignorance of a material element in a contract that leads to dispute; this is a cause for invalidity in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a sale invalid due to differing currencies?
A) When currencies differ in both circulation and value.
B) When currencies are equal in circulation but differ in value.
C) When currencies differ in circulation but are equal in value.
D) When currencies are equal in both circulation and value.

Key Takeaway
Custom determines the default currency; uncertainty that leads to dispute invalidates the contract, but specification within the session cures it.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
8 min
Four Scenarios of Currency Difference in Sale
Opening classification of currency scenarios for sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three scenarios where a sale with unspecified currency is valid and the one scenario where it is invalid, based on equality or difference in marketability (rawāj) and value (māliyya).

Core Idea
A sale with unspecified currency is invalid only when currencies have equal marketability but different values.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq lays out the Hanafī rules for sales when different types of dirhams or dinars circulate. The key distinction is between marketability (rawāj) and monetary value (māliyya). Understanding these scenarios is essential for evaluating the validity of everyday transactions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the four possible combinations of rawāj and māliyya
Currencies can be equal or different in both rawāj and māliyya, or equal in one and different in the other. The passage reduces these to four scenarios, but the ruling depends on the combination.
Rawāj is how widely accepted a currency is; māliyya is its purchasing power.
2
Identify the single invalid scenario
The sale is fasid (invalid) only when the currencies are equal in rawāj but differ in māliyya. This causes ambiguity (gharar) about the counter-value.
This is the only case where the contract is automatically invalid without specification.
3
Recognize the three valid scenarios
(1) Different in both rawāj and māliyya → it turns to the more marketable (arwaj); (2) Different in rawāj, equal in māliyya → also turns to the arwaj; (3) Equal in both → buyer may choose whichever type.
In scenarios 1 and 2, the sale is valid and the arwaj type is implicitly intended.
4
Apply the rule when only the name differs
If the currencies are equal in both rawāj and māliyya and differ only in name (e.g., Egyptian vs. Damascene), the buyer may give either. The seller cannot insist on a specific type because that would be obstinacy (ta'annut).
This is derived from the principle: currency is not specified in commutative contracts (al-naqd lā yata'ayyan fī al-mu'āwaḍāt).
