Book of Sale. And it is that they are equal in marketability and value together, or they differ in both, or they are equal in one without the other. Invalidity is in one scenario: equality in marketability and difference in value. Validity is in three scenarios: when they differ in marketability and value, then it turns to the more marketable; when they differ in marketability but are equal in value, then it also turns to the more marketable; and when they are equal in both. If the difference is only in name, like Egyptian and Damascene, then the buyer may choose to give whichever he wishes. If the seller demands one of them, the buyer may give something else because the seller's refusal to accept what the buyer gives without superiority is obstinacy. Hence we said: currency is not specified in commutative contracts. Al-Hidayah cited an example of equality in value with thunā'ī and thulāthī. Al-ʿInāyah objected that it is not a valid example because what two of them make a daniq and what three of them make a daniq are not equal in value, but they could be equal in marketability. Thunā'ī and thulāthī are interpreted in al-Miʿrāj as in al-ʿInāyah, and in Fatḥ al-Qadīr: thunā'ī and thulāthī are names of dirhams that were in their lands with different values, likewise ruknī and khalīfatī in gold—khalīfatī was better in value with them, and ʿadālī is a name for dirhams. Al-Zaylaʿī interpreted thunā'ī as what two are a dirham, and thulāthī as what three are a dirham. In summary, thunā'ī is two pieces of silver, either a dāniq or a dirham, and thulāthī is three pieces, either a dāniq or a dirham. If someone sells a commodity for a dirham in a town where there is the buyer's dirham, if he wishes he may pay two thunā'ī pieces or three thulāthī pieces. The truth is what is in al-Hidāyah of equality in value because the value of thunā'ī equals the value of thulāthī, and the intent is not the piece such that it would be a case of difference in value. Yes, if he sold something for a piece, it would be invalid because a thunā'ī piece is half a dirham and a thulāthī piece is... this is what appeared to me in solving this place, and I have not seen it from others. It is restricted to sale because in a will, if [the currencies] differ in value but are equal in marketability, his bequests are executed with the lowest currency; and if they differ in marketability but are equal in value, the bequest turns to the predominant currency. In al-Bazzāziyyah from Kitāb al-Daʿwā: If someone claims a weighed amount mentioning the type, gold or silver, even if minted, he says such-and-such Khwārizmī or Bukhārī dīnārs, good or bad, and he needs to mention the quality when currencies differ, even if of one type; but if they are all equal in marketability and no part has superiority over another, the sale is valid and the buyer may give whichever he wishes. However, [then there is a diagram?] 'one-third dirham, two pieces, and dirham, three pieces, choice'. End quote. The author will mention the Ẓāhiriyyah expression more simply in Kitāb al-Daʿwā at the author's statement in the chapter on disagreement: 'And if they differ in term or in condition of option.' His statement: 'So the truth is what is in al-Hidāyah...' its summary is that the intent of al-Hidāyah is that if he bought for a dirham and uttered the word dirham absolutely, and the dirhams were some thunā'ī and some thulāthī, it is valid and the buyer has choice. This is supported by what is in al-Jawhara: 'Thunā'ī is what two of them are a dāniq, and thulāthī is what three of them are a dāniq. In this scenario, the sale is valid when the name dirham is uttered absolutely because there is no dispute nor difference in value. End quote. I say: similar in our time is gold, for it may be complete (kāmil) and may be... [page break] ...invalid. In claims, specification is necessary. If one of them is more marketable, the sale turns to the more marketable. According to al-Nīsābūrī, it requires mentioning that it is red (i.e., gold). Quality must be mentioned according to the common people. Imām al-Nasafī said: if he says 'pure red' and does not mention quality, it suffices. The mint must be mentioned, it is said, but it is not a condition. If he says that they are inspected (muntagidah), he does not need to mention quality according to the correct view. Al-Lāmishī mentioned: if the currencies in the town are different and one is more marketable, the claim is not valid unless he specifies; likewise if he acknowledges ten red dīnārs and in the town there are different red currencies without specification, unlike sale, for it turns to the more marketable. In al-Dhakhīra: when currencies differ in the town and are equal in marketability, neither sale nor claim is valid without specification; if there is apparent superiority in marketability, it turns to it and it is considered like the wording in claim, so there is no need for specification unless a long time has passed from the time of the dispute to the time of claim such that the more marketable is not known; then specification is needed for what was more marketable at contract time. This is what is in al-Bazzāziyyah from Daʿwā. He mentioned in Ṣulḥ: if the substitute is dirhams, it needs specification of amount and quality, and it falls on the currency of the town for dirhams and dīnārs when uttered absolutely; if currencies differ, then on the prevalent; if equal, it is not valid without specification. In al-Tatārkhāniyyah from the chapter of Mahr, citing al-Ḥujja: if a man marries a woman for one thousand and in the town currencies differ, it turns to the prevalent; if there is none, then look to her mahr al-mithl; whichever matches her mahr al-mithl is decreed for her. This covers the chapters of sale, will, ṣulḥ, claim, acknowledgment, and mahr. Remaining: khulʿ—if he divorces her for one thousand dirhams without specification; and the waqf—if the endower stipulates dirhams or dīnārs for him, it is appropriate that he gets the least; likewise in gift (hibah), but gift is not complete except by taking possession, which is the cause of ownership and by which ambiguity is removed. Remaining: ijārah. Al-Bazzāziyyah from Ijārāt said: it is based on the prevalent currency of the town; if prevalence differs, it is invalid like sale. So the conclusion is that sale, ijārah, and ṣulḥ are the same. In claims, specification is necessary in all respects like acknowledgment. In mahr, judgment is given for what matches mahr al-mithl. In a will, he gets the least. In al-Khāniyyah from Kitābah: what is valid as mahr is valid as substitute in kitābah; its implication is that if he contracts kitābah for one thousand and in the town currencies are equal, judgment is given for what matches the value. In al-Mujtabā: if someone buys for one hundred mithqāl of unspecified silver or gold, it is not valid until he describes it as good or otherwise; if he says 'for one thousand nabaḥrajah or zayūf', it is not valid unless they are known in the town. We have already stated that if he pointed to hidden dirhams and when they were uncovered they turned out to be zayūf or contrary to the currency of the town, he is entitled to good ones of the town's currency. His statement: 'And food is sold... dirham halves for gold and becomes quarters, each four for gold, and each of the complete, the halves, and the four quarters are equal in value. So if he buys for gold, he may pay the complete or the broken.' His statement: 'It is not valid without specification.' Al-Ramlī said: meaning nothing is established without it, unlike sale where the more marketable is established without specification. It will come in acknowledgment that it is valid with the unknown and specification is incumbent. His statement: 'And it is appropriate that he gets the least.' Al-Nahr said: it should be restricted...
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharḥ) on the rulings regarding the effect of different currencies (specifically dirhams and dīnārs) on the validity of sales and other commutative contracts. The author begins by categorizing the scenarios based on equality or difference in marketability (rawāj) and value (māliyya). He declares that invalidity occurs only when the currencies are equal in marketability but differ in value. In all other cases—whether they differ in both, differ in marketability but equal in value, or equal in both—the sale is valid. When currencies differ in name only (e.g., Egyptian vs. Damascene dirhams of equal value), the buyer may give either, and the seller cannot insist on a specific type because such insistence would be obstinacy. The author then discusses the example of thunā'ī and thulāthī dirhams cited in al-Hidāyah as an example of equality in value. He defends al-Hidāyah against al-ʿInāyah's objection by explaining that the intended equality is in the overall value of the pieces, not per piece; thunā'ī and thulāthī are categories, not individual pieces of different values. He then extends the ruling to other contracts: in a will (waṣiyya), if the currencies differ in value but are equal in marketability, the legatee receives the lowest; if they differ in marketability but equal in value, the bequest turns to the predominant currency. The passage then quotes from al-Bazzāziyyah's Kitāb al-Daʿwā, contrasting the requirements for claims (daʿwā) and acknowledgments (iqrār) with those for sales. In claims and acknowledgments, the currency must be specified (quality, type, even mint) especially when currencies differ; otherwise, the claim is invalid. However, in sales, if one type is more marketable (arwaj), the contract turns to that type without specification. If all types are equal in both marketability and value, the sale is valid and the buyer may give whichever he chooses. The passagen then summarizes rulings for various contracts: sale, ijārah (lease), and ṣulḥ (compromise) are treated the same way—they turn to the prevalent currency. In daʿwā and iqrār, specification is always required. In mahr (dower), the judgment goes to the value that matches mahr al-mithl (the customary dower). In waṣiyya, the legatee gets the least. In khulʿ (divorce by redemption) and waqf, the beneficiary likely gets the least. In hibah (gift), the ambiguity is resolved by possession. In ijārah, if prevalence differs, the contract is invalid like sale. Finally, the passage cites further rules from other works about describing gold and silver quality and the issue of zayūf (defective) coins.

Key Terms
الرواج (al-rawāj): The circulation or marketability of a currency; how commonly it is accepted and used in transactions.
المالية (al-māliyya): The monetary value or worth of a currency; its purchasing power.
الأروج (al-arwaj): The more marketable or widely circulated currency among several types.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sale where the currency is unspecified and there are two types of dirhams in the town: Type A has higher marketability (arwaj) but lower value, Type B has lower marketability but higher value. What is the Hanafī ruling?
A) The sale is invalid because they are not equal in both value and marketability.
B) The sale is valid, and the buyer must give Type A because it is more marketable.
C) The sale is valid, and the buyer may give either type.
D) The sale is valid, and the seller may demand Type A.

Key Takeaway
A sale with unspecified currency is invalid only when the currencies are equally marketable but differ in value; otherwise it is valid, often with the buyer giving the more marketable type or having a choice.

Currency Specification in Contracts: The Principle of Prevalence (Arwaj)
Explains need for currency specification across contracts and differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of currency prevalence (arwaj) to determine the validity of a contract when the type of currency is not specified.

Core Idea
In a sale, unqualified currency defaults to the prevalent type; in a claim, specification is mandatory.

When multiple currencies circulate in a country, ambiguity about which currency is intended can invalidate contracts. Hanafi jurists have developed a nuanced approach: the prevalent (arwaj) currency resolves ambiguity in sale, lease, and similar contracts, but claims and acknowledgments require explicit specification. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and expands it across various chapters (sale, lease, sulh, dower, bequest, gift, kitaba).

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline ruling for sale
Find where the text states that in a sale, if one currency is more prevalent, the contract is directed to that prevalent currency without needing specification. This is the anchor ruling.
Look for 'ينصرف البيع إلى الأروج' (the sale is directed to the more prevalent).
2
Distinguish claim and acknowledgment from sale
Note that for a claim (da'wa) or acknowledgment (iqrar), specification is required generally, unless prevalence is clear and no long time has elapsed. Understand the rationale: claims involve potential dispute, so ambiguity is less tolerable.
Contrast: 'في الدعوى لا بد من التعيين' (in a claim, specification is necessary).
3
Handle the condition of equal prevalence
If currencies are equal in prevalence, even a sale is invalid without specification. This shows that prevalence is the decisive factor for defaulting.
From al-Dhakhira: 'إذا استوى الرواج لا يصح البيع بلا بيان'.
4
Extend the sale ruling to other contracts
Apply the same principle to lease (ijara) and sulh (settlement). The text explicitly compares them: 'فالحاصل أن البيع والإجارة والصلح سواء' (sale, lease, and sulh are the same).
For dower and bequest, different rules apply (mahr al-mithl and minimum, respectively).
5
Recognize subsidiary cases and the role of possession
In gift (hiba), possession resolves ambiguity. In khul' and kitaba, the ruling is analogous to dower. Note that for bequest (wasiyya), the legatee takes the least amount that could be intended.
The text ends with a reminder about pointing to bad coins: the buyer gets good coins of the country's currency.

And specification of quality is necessary according to the general public. Imam al-Nasafi said: If he mentions 'pure red' and does not mention quality, it suffices him. And mention of the strike [i.e., mint] of any house is necessary, and it is said it is not a condition. And if he mentions that it is minted, he does not need to mention quality according to the correct view. In al-Dhakhira: When currencies differ in the country and are equal in prevalence, neither the sale nor the claim is valid without specification. If there is a slight preference in prevalence, it is directed to it, and it is considered like the wording in a claim, so there is no need for specification unless a long time has passed from the time of litigation to the time of the claim such that the more prevalent is not known; then specification of what was more prevalent at the time of the contract is necessary. Thus far from al-Bazzaziyya regarding claims. If the currencies differ, then on the predominant; if equal, it is not valid without specification. In al-Tatar Khaniyya from the chapter on dower, citing al-Hujja: If a man marries a woman for one thousand and in the country there are different currencies, it is directed to the predominant. If there is none, then the dower of her peers is considered; whichever corresponds to her peer's dower is adjudged for her. Now the chapters of sale, bequest, sulh, claim, acknowledgment, and dower have been covered. Similarly, it is appropriate in gift, but a gift is not complete except by possession, which is the cause of ownership, and by it uncertainty is removed. Remaining: lease; he said in al-Bazzaziyya from the section on leases: It is on the predominant currency of the country; if predominance differs, it becomes corrupt like a sale. The conclusion is that sale, lease, and sulh are the same. In a claim, specification is needed in all respects like acknowledgment. In dower, judgment is given according to what agrees with the dower of her peers. In bequest, he receives the least. In Kitab al-Khaniyya: Whatever is valid as a dower is valid as a substitute in kitaba; its implication is that if he makes a kitaba with him for one thousand and there is equal currency in the country, then judgment is given according to the value. In al-Mujtaba: If he buys for one hundred mithqals of silver unspecified or gold, it is not permitted unless he describes it as good or otherwise. If he says 'for one thousand nabarjis' or 'zuyuf', it is not valid unless these are known in the country. And we have previously mentioned that if he pointed to covered dirhams, and when they were uncovered they turned out to be zuyuf or contrary to the country's currency, he is entitled to good coins of the country's currency.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with comparative analysis across different chapters of fiqh. It addresses the issue of specifying the type of currency in transactions when multiple currencies exist in a country. The core principle is that in a sale, if one currency is more prevalent (arwaj), the contract is automatically interpreted as referring to that prevalent currency without need for explicit specification. However, in a claim (da'wa) or acknowledgment (iqrar), specification is required unless the prevalence is clear and time has not obscured it. The text cites various Hanafi authorities: al-Naysaburi requires mention of 'red' for dinars; al-Nasafi holds that 'pure red' suffices without explicit quality; al-Lamishi confirms the distinction between sale and claim. In al-Dhakhira, if currencies are equal in prevalence, even a sale is invalid without specification. For sulh (settlement) and lease (ijara), the ruling follows that of sale: reference to the prevalent currency. For dower (mahr), judgment is by the peer's mahr if prevalence is unclear. For bequest (wasiyya), the legatee receives the least amount that could be intended. For gift (hiba), the issue is resolved by possession. The author summarises: sale, lease, and sulh share the same ruling; claim and acknowledgment require specification; dower goes by mahr al-mithl; bequest takes the minimum. The passage concludes with a reminder of the earlier ruling: if one points to covered dirhams that turn out to be bad, he gets good coins of the country's currency.

Key Terms
أروج (arwaj): More prevalent in circulation; a currency type that is most commonly used in transactions in a given locality.
نقد (naqd): Currency; coined money (dirhams or dinars) used as a medium of exchange.
بيان (bayan): Specification; clarifying the type, quality, or quantity of the currency in a contract or claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The sale is valid and directed to the prevalent currency
B) The sale is fasid (invalid) because of ambiguity
C) The sale is suspended until the currency is specified
D) The buyer chooses the currency

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the default for exchange contracts like sale is the prevalent currency; but when specification is required (claim, equal prevalence), omission invalidates the transaction.

Selling Food by Kail and Juzaf: General Rule and Riba Exception
Ruling on selling food by measure or guess with riba exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default permissibility of selling food by measure or guess and identify the specific condition under which such a sale becomes prohibited due to riba.

Core Idea
Selling by guess is allowed except when exchanging the same ribawi genus without ensuring equality.

This passage from Ibn Nujaym's commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies a common point of confusion: while selling food by volume (kail) or by guess (juzaf) is generally permissible, an important exception arises when dealing with the same genus of ribawi items. The ruling is rooted in a hadith and the principle that the possibility of riba is treated like its reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
The passage begins by stating that food (grains) can be sold by kail or juzaf. Determine the default hukm: mubah (permissible).
This is the baseline; the exception follows.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is a hadith from Bukhari: 'If these species differ, then sell as you wish.' Understand that this allows freedom in the method of sale when types differ.
The hadith is used to justify permissibility of juzaf when species differ.
3
Understand the exception for same genus
The text clarifies that exchanging the same genus by juzaf is not allowed due to the risk of riba. This applies to a genus like wheat for wheat.
The exception applies only when the items are of the same ribawi genus.
4
Grasp the nuance: possibility of riba like reality
The passage introduces a principle: 'the possibility of riba is like its reality.' This means that even a chance of disparity in value triggers prohibition. However, if disparity is impossible (e.g., exchanging identical weights of silver in sealed pans), juzaf becomes permissible.
This principle is key to understanding edge cases.
5
Apply to examples
Work through the examples: selling wheat for wheat by juzaf is invalid unless equality is apparent; but buying barley ears for barley by kail or juzaf is allowed because they are not the same genus (one is grain, the other a plant).
Genus determination is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فسد إن لم يبين ويباع الطعام كيلاً وخرافاً وبإناء أو حجر لا يعرف قدره ومن باع صبرة عليه الميزان حتى (1) كيلاً وجزافاً) لحديث البخاري فإذا اختلفت هذه الأصناف فبيعوا كيف شئتم ) ولا يرد الجنس بالجنس من الربا مجازفة لما سيأتي في باب الربا من أنه غير جائز إلا إذا كان بيع قليلاً. وفي البزازية : بيع الحنطة بالحنطة مجازفة لا يجوز إلا إذا ظهر تساويهما اهـ. يعني في المجلس كما سيأتي في باب الربا. وفي جامع الفصولين : شراء قصيل البر بالبر كيلاً وجزافاً جاز لعدم الجناس .اهـ. ولأن احتمال الربا كحقيقته حتى لو لم يحتمل كأن باع كفة ميزان من فضة بكفة منها فإنه يجوز وإن كان مجازفة لعدم احتمال التفاضل كما في فتح القدير وهكذا في البزازية.
It becomes invalid if not clarified, and food is sold by measure and by guess, and with a vessel or stone whose capacity is unknown, and whoever sells a stack of grain with a scale on it until (it is weighed) by measure and by guess, due to the hadith of Bukhari: 'If these species differ, then sell as you wish.' And one genus is not exchanged for the same genus by guess due to riba, as will come in the chapter of riba: it is not permissible unless it is a small sale. And in al-Bazzaziyya: Selling wheat for wheat by guess is not permissible unless their equality becomes apparent. Meaning in the assembly, as will come in the chapter of riba. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: Buying barley ears for barley by measure and by guess is permissible due to lack of genus [match]. And because the possibility of riba is like its reality, so if there is no possibility, like if one sells a scale pan of silver for another scale pan of it, then it is permissible even if by guess, due to the lack of possibility of disparity, as in Fath al-Qadir, and likewise in al-Bazzaziyya.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof from the commentary (sharh) of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author, Ibn Nujaym, explains that selling foodstuffs by kail (measure) or juzaf (guess) is valid, but with conditions to avoid riba. The key proof is the hadith: 'If these species differ, then sell as you wish,' which allows discretion when the items are of different types. However, when exchanging the same genus (e.g., wheat for wheat), juzaf is prohibited unless it is a small quantity and equality is apparent. The text also notes that if riba is impossible (as in exchanging identical items by weight), juzaf becomes permissible. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi position that riba is prohibited by a zanni text, and caution is required.

Key Terms
كيلاً (kaylan): By measure, using a standard volume measure.
جزافاً (juzāfan): By guess, without knowing precise measure or weight.
الطعام (al-ṭaʿām): Generally refers to staple grains like wheat, but in context includes all grains.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Permissible if equality is apparent or it is a small quantity
D) Never permissible

Key Takeaway
Selling by juzaf is generally mubah, but when trading the same ribawi genus, equality must be ensured or the transaction is prohibited.

Juzaf: Definition, Linguistic Origin, and Legal Scope
Defines juzaf and traces its Persian origin, with applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of juzaf, identify its linguistic origin, and apply the Hanafi rule that juzaf is permissible except in same-genus ribawi transactions.

Core Idea
Juzaf sells by guess — valid except when riba lurks in same-genus exchange.

This lesson explores the concept of juzaf, a sale where the quantity is unknown at the time of contract. The Hanafi school permits juzaf in most transactions but prohibits it when it coincides with riba, particularly in exchanges of the same genus of items sold by measure or weight. We'll trace its Persian linguistic roots and examine key examples from the texts.

Guided Path
1
Define juzaf legally
Understand that juzaf is a sale without exact knowledge of measure or weight. The buyer and seller agree on a price without knowing the precise quantity.
The term appears in contrast to kail (measure) and wazn (weight).
2
Trace its linguistic origin
Note that juzaf is a Persian loanword (kazāf) meaning 'taking in abundance' or 'by guess'. It entered Arabic and is irregular in form.
Ibn al-Sikkit and Ibn Faris confirm its foreign origin.
3
State the default ruling
Juzaf is permissible for all commodities except when the exchange involves ribawi items of the same genus, where exact measure/weight is required.
The prohibition is due to gharar that may lead to riba.
4
Apply the rule to division (qisma)
Division of jointly owned ribawi property by juzaf is invalid because it is like a sale of same-genus ribawi items.
Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj explicitly states this.
5
Recognize exceptions and tricks
Selling wheat in the ear by measure is valid. The trick to avoid riba when selling a guarantee involves a two-step sale with a garment.
These illustrate how the school balances practical needs with riba avoidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجزاف بيع شيء لا يعلم كيله ولا وزنه وهو اسم من جازف مجازفة من باب قاتل. والجزاف بالضم خارج عن القياس وهي فارسية معرب كزاف ومن هنا قيل أصل الكلمة وصل إلى العربية. قال ابن السكيت: القطاع جزف في الكيل جزفاً أكثر منه ومنه الجزاف والمجازفة في البيع وهي كلمة دخيلة في العربية ويؤيده قول ابن فارس : الجزف الأخذ بكثرة كلمة فارسية، ويقال لمن يرسل كلامه إرسالاً من غير قانون جازف في كلامه فأقيم نهج الصواب مقام الكيل والوزن اهـ. وفي السراج الوهاج : القسمة كالبيع إذا وقعت فيما يجري فيه الربا مجازفة لا تصح. وفي العمدة اشترى حنطة رجل قبل أن تحصد مكايلة جاز لأن الحنطة موجودة وكذلك القوائم والتبن قبل الكدس قبل التذرية وفي القنية : يجوز بيع الحنطة في سنبلها مكايلة أو موازنة وإن لم تشتد الحبوب بعد .اهـ. ولو قال المصنف ويجوز بيع ووزناً وجزافاً بغير جنسه لكان أولى كما لا يخفى. وفي البزازية : وبيع الحنطة بالدراهم وزناً يجوز ويجوز بيع كل ما لا يتفاوت كالبر بلا إشارة ولا إضافة لو كان في ملكه قدر المبيع كله، ولو قال بعتك مائة من من هذه الحنطة وأعطاها من كدس آخر لا يجوز لأن يتعين بالتعيين. له عليه حنطة أكلها فباعها منه نسيئة لا يجوز لأنه الضمان والحيلة أن بيع يبيعها بثوب ويقبض الثوب ثم يبيعه بدراهم إلى أجل .اهـ والكدس وزان قفل ما يجمع من الطعام في البيدر فإذا ديس ودق فهو العرمة والصبرة ؛ كذا في المصباح. وفي الظهيرية : رجل له زرع قد استحصد فباع حنطته جاز لأنه باع موجوداً مقدور التسليم، ولو باع تبنها لم يجز لأن التبن لا يكون إلا بعد الدوس والتذرية فكان بيع المعدوم واستحصاد الزرع إدراكه . وفي الذخيرة : ادعى رجل على غيره شيئاً مما يكال أو يوزن أو يعد فاشتراه المدعى عليه من بمائة دينار ثم تصادقا أنه لم يكن للمدعي على المدعى عليه شيء فالعقد باطل، تفرقا المدعي ' الذمة أو لم يتفرقا لأن العقد يتعلق بالكر في ذمته بالإضافة إليه، فإذا تبين أنه لم يكن في تبين أنه باع المعدوم وبيع المعدوم باطل ولو ادعى دراهم أو دنانير أو فلوساً اشتراها المدعى عليه بدراهم ونقد الدراهم ثم تصادقا أنه لم يكن عليه شيء ففي مسألة الدراهم والدنانير إذا لم يتفرقا ورجع بمثل ما اشترى يصح العقد ثم يتعلق بالمسمى في الذمة، ولو تفرقا بطل العقد، وفي الفلوس لا يبطل العقد وإن تفرقا قبل قبض ما اشترى لأن في بيع الفلوس بالدراهم يكتفى بقبض أحد البدلين حقيقة، وإذا اشترى شيئاً بدراهم دين وهما يعلمان أن لا دين لم يجز. ومن مسائل الحنطة ودعواها قال في دعوى البزازية : ادعى عشرة أقفزة حنطة لا يصح بلا بيان السبب لأنه لو سلماً يطالب في الموضع الذي عين عنده وإن قرضاً أو ثمن مبيع تعين مكان البيع والقرض وإن غصباً واستهلاكاً تعين مكان الغصب والاستهلاك اهـ. وفي السراج الوهاج والمنتقى المشتري إذا قال بعني هذا الكر الحنطة فباعه فهو على الكيل فإن قبضه بغير كيل ثم كاله بغير محضر من البائع جاز إلا أن المشتري لا يصدق على ما يدعي من النقصان لأنه قد صدق على وفاء الكيل وإنما كيله تحليل لموافقة السنة اهـ.
And juzaf is the sale of something whose measure or weight is not known, and it is a verbal noun from jāzafa mujāzafatan from the verb qātala. And al-juzāf (with damm) is irregular (out of qiyas) and it is a Persian loanword arabicized from kazāf, and from here it is said the original word reached Arabic. Ibn al-Sikkit said: The people of al-Qitāʿ cut (juzf) in measure excessively, and from it comes al-juzāf and al-mujāzafa in sale, and it is a foreign word in Arabic. Ibn Faris supports that: al-jazf means taking in abundance, a Persian word. And it is said of one who sends his speech without regulation: he jāzafa in his speech. So the correct path was established in place of measure and weight. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: Division (qisma) is like sale; if it occurs in something subject to riba by juzaf, it is not valid. In al-ʿUmda: If one buys a man's wheat before it is harvested by measure, it is permissible because the wheat exists, and likewise the stalks and straw before the stack and before winnowing. In al-Qunyah: Selling wheat in its ears by measure or weight is permissible even if the grains have not hardened yet. If the author had said 'And it is permissible to sell by weight and by juzaf of a different genus' it would have been better, as is not hidden. In al-Bazzaziyya: Selling wheat for dirhams by weight is permissible, and it is permissible to sell anything that does not differ (like wheat) without indication or addition if the entire sold amount is in his possession. If he says 'I sell you a hundred from this wheat' and gives from another stack, it is not permissible because it requires specification. If someone owes him wheat and he ate it, then sells it to him on credit, it is not permissible because it is a guarantee. The trick is that he sells it for a garment, takes the garment, then sells it for dirhams on credit. Al-kadus (stack) is like qufl: what is gathered from food on the threshing floor; when trampled and beaten, it is al-ʿurma and al-subra. In al-Zahiriyya: A man has ripe crops, he sells his wheat – it is permissible because he sold something existing and deliverable. If he sells its straw, it is not permissible because straw only exists after threshing and winnowing, so it is selling a non-existent thing. In al-Dhakhira: If a man claimed something from another that is sold by measure, weight, or number, and the defendant bought it from him for one hundred dinars, then they both agree that the plaintiff had no claim against the defendant – the contract is void, whether they separated or not, because the contract was based on something in his liability, and when it becomes clear there was nothing, it becomes clear he sold a non-existent thing, and sale of non-existent is void. If he claimed dirhams or dinars or fulus, and the defendant bought them with dirhams and paid the dirhams, then they agree there was no debt – in the dirham and dinar case, if they have not separated and he returns the equivalent of what he bought, the contract is valid, then it becomes a liability for the named amount. If they separate, the contract is void. In the case of fulus, the contract is not void even if they separate before delivery, because in sale of fulus with dirhams, it suffices to receive one of the two considerations physically. If one buys something with dirhams as a debt while both know there is no debt, it is not permissible. From the issues of wheat and claims: in Da'wa al-Bazzaziyya: If he claims ten qafiz of wheat, it is not valid without stating the cause, because if it is a loan, he demands in the place he specified; if it is a purchase or loan, the place of sale or loan becomes specific; if it is usurpation or waste, the place of usurpation or waste becomes specific. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and al-Muntaqa: If the buyer says 'Sell me this kor of wheat' and he sells it, it is on the basis of measure. If he takes delivery without measuring, then measures it without the seller's presence, it is permissible, except that the buyer is not trusted regarding the deficiency he claims, because he has confirmed full measure, and his measuring is only a voluntary act to follow the Sunnah.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a definition ruling from the sharh, explaining the term 'juzaf' and its linguistic history. The author clarifies that juzaf is a sale where the exact quantity (whether by volume or weight) is unknown. Linguistically, it is a Persian loanword (from kazāf) meaning 'taking in abundance' or 'by guess'. The passage then provides numerous furu' (subsidiary rulings) to illustrate the application: division (qisma) by juzaf in ribawi items is invalid; selling wheat in ears by measure is permissible; selling wheat for dirhams by weight is permissible; the trick to avoid riba when selling a guarantee; and various cases about claims and disputes. These examples show that the Hanafi school is meticulous about precision in sales to avoid riba and uncertainty. The commentary follows the author's order, starting with definition, then providing illustrative cases.

Key Terms
جزاف (juzāf): Sale by guess without exact measure or weight.
القسمة (al-qisma): Division of jointly owned property among co-owners.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a valid sale by juzaf?
A) Selling wheat for wheat by juzaf.
B) Dividing a joint pile of barley by juzaf.
C) Selling wheat for dirhams by juzaf.
D) Selling a house by juzaf without stating the area.

Key Takeaway
Juzaf is a permissible mode of sale except when it involves the exchange of same-genus ribawi items, where exact measure or weight is required to avoid riba.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Selling Grains by Measure, Weight, and Guess
Rulings on valid methods of selling grains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when selling grains by measure (kayl) or weight (wazn) is valid and when guess (juzaf) is prohibited for ribawi items.

Core Idea
Grains sold by measure are valid even in the ear; guess (juzaf) is invalid for ribawi items.

This lesson presents a series of rulings on the sale of grains, focusing on the permissibility of selling by measure (mukāyalah) and weight (wazn) versus guess (juzāf). The Hanafi school allows selling wheat in its ear by measure or weight even before hardening, but strictly prohibits guess sales for ribawi commodities. Understanding these distinctions ensures valid transactions and avoids usury.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commodity and method of sale
Determine whether the commodity is measured by volume (kayl) or weight (wazn), and whether the sale is by measure, weight, or guess (juzāf).
Grains like wheat are customarily measured, so kayl is standard.
2
Check if the item is ribawi
If the item is ribawi (like wheat for wheat), guess sale is invalid due to uncertainty (gharar). For non-ribawi, guess may be allowed.
Ribawi items include those subject to the six ribawi categories.
3
Verify existence and deliverability
The sold item must exist and be deliverable. Selling wheat in the ear is valid because it exists, but selling straw before threshing is invalid as non-existent.
Crops before harvest exist in the field; stalks and straw exist after harvesting but before processing.
4
Apply the ruling on division (qisma)
Division of ribawi items by guess is invalid, as it is treated like a sale. For non-ribawi, division by guess is permissible.
This is derived from the principle that qisma is a type of sale.
5
Check for specific indications
If the seller specifies a particular heap, delivery must be from that heap; otherwise, the contract is invalid.
Specification by pointing (ishara) is binding.

ولو ادعى دراهم أو دنانير أو فلوساً اشتراها المدعى عليه بدراهم ونقد الدراهم ثم تصادقا أنه لم يكن عليه شيء ففي مسألة الدراهم والدنانير إذا لم يتفرقا ورجع بمثل ما اشترى يصح العقد ثم يتعلق بالمسمى في تعين مكان الغصب والاستهلاك اهـ. وفي السراج الوهاج والمنتقى المشتري إذا قال بعني هذا الكر الحنطة فباعه فهو على الكيل فإن قبضه بغير كيل ثم كاله بغير محضر من البائع جاز إلا أن المشتري لا يصدق على ما يدعي من النقصان لأنه قد صدق على وفاء الكيل وإنما كيله تحليل لموافقة السنة اهـ. ولعله إنما لا الحنطة (وفي القنية يجوز بيع في سنبلها مكايلة الخ) قال الرملي : نحو عشرة أمداد مثلاً منها بكذا من الثمن لأنه مبیع موجود مغطى بسنبله فلا مانع من جوازه قوله : ( عليه حنطة أكلها فباعها منه الخ) يصدق أن القول للقابض لإقراره بقوله يعني هذا الكر.
Book of Sale: Grains by measure except for the two precious metals. For one who sends his speech without a rule, he talks rashly, so the path of correctness was established in place of measure and weight. End. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: Division (qisma) is like sale; if it occurs in what involves riba by guess, it is not valid. In al-Umda: A man bought wheat of another before it is harvested by measure—it is valid because the wheat exists. Likewise the stalks and straw before stacking and winnowing. In al-Qunyah: It is permissible to sell wheat in its ear by measure or weight even if the grains have not hardened yet. End. If the author had said 'and it is permissible to sell by weight and by guess with other than its genus', it would have been better, as is not hidden. In al-Bazzaziyyah: Selling wheat for dirhams by weight is permissible. It is permissible to sell everything that does not vary, like wheat, without indication or specification if the seller owns the entire amount of the sold item. If he said, 'I sell you a hundred from this wheat' and gave her from another heap, it is not valid because it becomes specified by specification. He owes him wheat that he ate and sold to him on credit—it is not valid because it is a guarantee. The trick is that he sells it to a third party for a garment, takes possession of the garment, then sells it for dirhams on credit. End. Al-kuds (heap) on the scale of 'qifl' is what is collected of food in the threshing floor. When it is threshed and beaten, it is called al-urma and al-sabra; this is in al-Misbah. In al-Zahiriyyah: A man has ripe crops and sold his wheat—it is valid because he sold an existing thing capable of delivery. If he sold its straw, it is not valid because straw only exists after threshing and winnowing, so it is selling a non-existent thing. 'Istahsad' of the crop is its ripening. In al-Dhakhirah: A man claimed from another something that is measured, weighed, or counted; the defendant bought it from him for a hundred dinars; then they agreed that the claimant had no claim on the defendant. The contract is void, whether they parted or not, because the contract is related to the karr in his dhimmah through addition. When it becomes clear that he was not (owed), it becomes clear that he sold a non-existent thing, and selling a non-existent thing is void. If he claimed dirhams or dinars or fulus, and the defendant bought them with dirhams and paid the dirhams, then they agreed that he owed nothing. In the case of dirhams and dinars, if they did not part and he returns the like of what he bought, the contract is valid, then it attaches to the named (amount) in the dhimmah. If they parted, the contract is void. In the case of fulus, the contract is not void even if they part before receiving what was bought, because in selling fulus for dirhams, it suffices to receive one of the two substitutes actually. If he bought something for dirhams of a debt while both know there is no debt, it is not valid. From the issues of wheat and claims: In Da'wa al-Bazzaziyyah: He claimed ten qafizes of wheat; it is not valid without stating the reason. Because if it is a salam, he demands in the place where it was confirmed; if it is a loan or price of a sale, the place of sale and loan is determined; if it is usurpation or destruction, the place of usurpation and destruction is determined. End. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and al-Muntaqa: If the buyer said, 'Sell me this karr of wheat' and he sold it, it is by measure. If he took it without measuring then measured it without the seller's presence, it is valid, except that the buyer is not believed in the deficiency he claims because he affirmed the fulfillment of the measure; his measurement is only an analysis to comply with the Sunnah. End. Perhaps it is not... wheat (In al-Qunyah: It is permissible to sell wheat in its ear by measure etc.) al-Ramli said: For example, ten mudd from it for such and such price, because it is an existing sold item covered by its ear, so there is no preventing its validity. His statement: (He owes him wheat that he ate and sold it to him etc.) It is true that the word is for the recipient because he affirms by his saying meaning this karr.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of various Hanafi rulings on the sale of grains, debts, and claims. It begins by quoting a previous statement about selling grains by measure except for gold and silver (النقدين), criticizing those who speak without rule. It then cites al-Siraj al-Wahhaj stating that division (qisma) is like sale regarding riba; if done by guess in ribawi items, it is invalid. Next, it quotes al-Umda that buying wheat before harvest by measure is valid because the wheat exists, and similarly stalks and straw before stacking and winnowing. al-Qunyah allows selling wheat in its ear by measure or weight even before the grains harden. The commentator notes that the author (المصنف) should have added 'by weight and by guess with other than its genus' for completeness. Then it presents a ruling from al-Bazzaziyyah: selling wheat for dirhams by weight is permissible, and selling items that do not vary (like wheat) without specific indication is allowed if the seller owns the entire quantity. However, if the seller says 'a hundred from this wheat' but delivers from another heap, it is invalid due to specification. It also discusses a case where a debtor sells the wheat he owes on credit—this is invalid as a guarantee; the trick (حيلة) is to sell it to a third party for a garment, take possession, then sell the garment for dirhams on credit. Lexical notes define 'كدس' as a heap of grain, 'عرمة' and 'صبرة' after threshing. From al-Zahirah: selling ripe wheat is valid as existing; selling straw is invalid as non-existent. From al-Dhakhira: a scenario where a claimant claims something measurable/weighable, the defendant buys it, but they later agree the debt was false. The contract is void because it was selling a non-existent thing. Different rules apply if the claimed item is dirhams/dinars vs. fulus. Then from Da'wa al-Bazzaziyyah: claiming wheat without specifying the reason (salam, loan, etc.) is invalid. Finally, from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and al-Muntaqa: a buyer who asks to be sold a specific karr of wheat, receives it without measuring, then measures it alone—the measurement is valid, but he is not believed about any deficiency because he already affirmed the full measure. Al-Ramli comments on the permissibility of selling wheat in the ear. The passage concludes that the seller's word is taken due to his affirmation.

Key Terms
مكايلة (mukāyalah): Selling or measuring by dry measure (kayl), as opposed to weight or guess.
جزاف (juzāf): Sale by guess or lump sum without precise measurement or weight.
الذمة (al-dhimmah): Legal liability or obligation in a person's conscience; often used for debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on selling wheat by guess (juzaf)?
A) It is always permissible as long as the wheat exists.
B) It is invalid because wheat is ribawi and guess leads to uncertainty.
C) It is valid only if the buyer measures it later.
D) It is valid only if both parties agree.

Key Takeaway
Selling grains by measure or weight is valid, but by guess (juzaf) is invalid for ribawi items like wheat.

The Legal Trick (Hila) for Selling a Debt of Wheat
Ruling on selling a debt and the legal trick (hila) to make it valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi hila that allows selling a debt of wheat on credit and apply its steps.

Core Idea
Selling a debt of wheat on credit is invalid as a guarantee; the hila involves a third-party garment sale.

The passage presents a case where a debtor owes wheat, which he then sells back to the creditor on credit. This is ruled invalid because it amounts to a guarantee (damān) of debt. However, Hanafi law provides a legitimate trick (hila) to achieve the same economic outcome by involving a third party. This lesson walks through that hila step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited transaction
A owes B wheat. A (debtor) sells that same wheat back to B on credit. This is invalid because it is a guarantee of debt, i.e., B is buying what he is already owed, on credit, creating uncertainty.
The debt is already in B's dhimmah; selling it back on credit resembles riba.
2
Understand the underlying prohibition
The sale of a debt for a debt (bay' al-kali' bi al-kali') is prohibited. Here, the wheat is owed (in dhimmah) and sold on credit, both deferred, making it invalid.
Hanafis strictly prohibit selling debt except with specific conditions.
3
Apply the hila: involve a third party
The debtor A sells the wheat (which he owes) to a third party C for a garment (spot). A takes possession of the garment. Then A sells the garment to the original creditor B on credit for dirhams.
This converts the debt into a tangible item (garment) and then into a new credit sale.
4
Verify conditions of the hila
The sale of wheat to C must be valid (spot), and A must take constructive possession of the garment. The subsequent sale of garment to B on credit is a separate contract, not involving the original debt directly.
The hila avoids selling the debt itself.
5
Conclude the permissibility
After these steps, B ends up owing A the price of the garment (dirhams) on credit, effectively replacing the wheat debt with a dirham debt. This is permissible.
This is a recognized hila in Hanafi fiqh, cited in al-Bazzaziyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع الحبوب كيلاً غير النقدين لمن يرسل كلامه إرسالاً من غير قانون جازف في كلامه فأقيم نهج الصواب مقام الكيل والوزن اهـ. وفي السراج الوهاج : القسمة كالبيع إذا وقعت فيما يجري فيه الربا مجازفة لا تصح. وفي العمدة اشترى حنطة رجل قبل أن تحصد مكايلة جاز لأن الحنطة موجودة وكذلك القوائم والتبن قبل الكدس قبل التذرية وفي القنية : يجوز بيع الحنطة في سنبلها مكايلة أو موازنة وإن لم تشتد الحبوب بعد .اهـ. ولو قال المصنف ويجوز بيع ووزناً وجزافاً بغير جنسه لكان أولى كما لا يخفى. وفي البزازية : وبيع الحنطة بالدراهم وزناً يجوز ويجوز بيع كل ما لا يتفاوت كالبر بلا إشارة ولا إضافة لو كان في ملكه قدر المبيع كله، ولو قال بعتك مائة من من هذه الحنطة وأعطاها من كدس آخر لا يجوز لأن يتعين بالتعيين. له عليه حنطة أكلها فباعها منه نسيئة لا يجوز لأنه الضمان والحيلة أن بيع يبيعها بثوب ويقبض الثوب ثم يبيعه بدراهم إلى أجل .اهـ والكدس وزان قفل ما يجمع من الطعام في البيدر فإذا ديس ودق فهو العرمة والصبرة ؛ كذا في المصباح. وفي الظهيرية : رجل له زرع قد استحصد فباع حنطته جاز لأنه باع موجوداً مقدور التسليم، ولو باع تبنها لم يجز لأن التبن لا يكون إلا بعد الدوس والتذرية فكان بيع المعدوم واستحصاد الزرع إدراكه . وفي الذخيرة : ادعى رجل على غيره شيئاً مما يكال أو يوزن أو يعد فاشتراه المدعى عليه من بمائة دينار ثم تصادقا أنه لم يكن للمدعي على المدعى عليه شيء فالعقد باطل، تفرقا المدعي ' الذمة أو لم يتفرقا لأن العقد يتعلق بالكر في ذمته بالإضافة إليه، فإذا تبين أنه لم يكن في تبين أنه باع المعدوم وبيع المعدوم باطل ولو ادعى دراهم أو دنانير أو فلوساً اشتراها المدعى عليه بدراهم ونقد الدراهم ثم تصادقا أنه لم يكن عليه شيء ففي مسألة الدراهم والدنانير إذا لم يتفرقا ورجع بمثل ما اشترى يصح العقد ثم يتعلق بالمسمى في الذمة، ولو تفرقا بطل العقد، وفي الفلوس لا يبطل العقد وإن تفرقا قبل قبض ما اشترى لأن في بيع الفلوس بالدراهم يكتفى بقبض أحد البدلين حقيقة، وإذا اشترى شيئاً بدراهم دين وهما يعلمان أن لا دين لم يجز. ومن مسائل الحنطة ودعواها قال في دعوى البزازية : ادعى عشرة أقفزة حنطة لا يصح بلا بيان السبب لأنه لو سلماً يطالب في الموضع الذي عين عنده وإن قرضاً أو ثمن مبيع تعين مكان البيع والقرض وإن غصباً واستهلاكاً تعين مكان الغصب والاستهلاك اهـ. وفي السراج الوهاج والمنتقى المشتري إذا قال بعني هذا الكر الحنطة فباعه فهو على الكيل فإن قبضه بغير كيل ثم كاله بغير محضر من البائع جاز إلا أن المشتري لا يصدق على ما يدعي من النقصان لأنه قد صدق على وفاء الكيل وإنما كيله تحليل لموافقة السنة اهـ. ولعله إنما لا الحنطة (وفي القنية يجوز بيع في سنبلها مكايلة الخ) قال الرملي : نحو عشرة أمداد مثلاً منها بكذا من الثمن لأنه مبیع موجود مغطى بسنبله فلا مانع من جوازه قوله : ( عليه حنطة أكلها فباعها منه الخ) يصدق أن القول للقابض لإقراره بقوله يعني هذا الكر.
Book of Sale: Grains by measure except for the two precious metals. For one who sends his speech without a rule, he talks rashly, so the path of correctness was established in place of measure and weight. End. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: Division (qisma) is like sale; if it occurs in what involves riba by guess, it is not valid. In al-Umda: A man bought wheat of another before it is harvested by measure—it is valid because the wheat exists. Likewise the stalks and straw before stacking and winnowing. In al-Qunyah: It is permissible to sell wheat in its ear by measure or weight even if the grains have not hardened yet. End. If the author had said 'and it is permissible to sell by weight and by guess with other than its genus', it would have been better, as is not hidden. In al-Bazzaziyyah: Selling wheat for dirhams by weight is permissible. It is permissible to sell everything that does not vary, like wheat, without indication or specification if the seller owns the entire amount of the sold item. If he said, 'I sell you a hundred from this wheat' and gave her from another heap, it is not valid because it becomes specified by specification. He owes him wheat that he ate and sold to him on credit—it is not valid because it is a guarantee. The trick is that he sells it to a third party for a garment, takes possession of the garment, then sells it for dirhams on credit. End. Al-kuds (heap) on the scale of 'qifl' is what is collected of food in the threshing floor. When it is threshed and beaten, it is called al-urma and al-sabra; this is in al-Misbah. In al-Zahiriyyah: A man has ripe crops and sold his wheat—it is valid because he sold an existing thing capable of delivery. If he sold its straw, it is not valid because straw only exists after threshing and winnowing, so it is selling a non-existent thing. 'Istahsad' of the crop is its ripening. In al-Dhakhirah: A man claimed from another something that is measured, weighed, or counted; the defendant bought it from him for a hundred dinars; then they agreed that the claimant had no claim on the defendant. The contract is void, whether they parted or not, because the contract is related to the karr in his dhimmah through addition. When it becomes clear that he was not (owed), it becomes clear that he sold a non-existent thing, and selling a non-existent thing is void. If he claimed dirhams or dinars or fulus, and the defendant bought them with dirhams and paid the dirhams, then they agreed that he owed nothing. In the case of dirhams and dinars, if they did not part and he returns the like of what he bought, the contract is valid, then it attaches to the named (amount) in the dhimmah. If they parted, the contract is void. In the case of fulus, the contract is not void even if they part before receiving what was bought, because in selling fulus for dirhams, it suffices to receive one of the two substitutes actually. If he bought something for dirhams of a debt while both know there is no debt, it is not valid. From the issues of wheat and claims: In Da'wa al-Bazzaziyyah: He claimed ten qafizes of wheat; it is not valid without stating the reason. Because if it is a salam, he demands in the place where it was confirmed; if it is a loan or price of a sale, the place of sale and loan is determined; if it is usurpation or destruction, the place of usurpation and destruction is determined. End. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and al-Muntaqa: If the buyer said, 'Sell me this karr of wheat' and he sold it, it is by measure. If he took it without measuring then measured it without the seller's presence, it is valid, except that the buyer is not believed in the deficiency he claims because he affirmed the fulfillment of the measure; his measurement is only an analysis to comply with the Sunnah. End. Perhaps it is not... wheat (In al-Qunyah: It is permissible to sell wheat in its ear by measure etc.) al-Ramli said: For example, ten mudd from it for such and such price, because it is an existing sold item covered by its ear, so there is no preventing its validity. His statement: (He owes him wheat that he ate and sold it to him etc.) It is true that the word is for the recipient because he affirms by his saying meaning this karr.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of various Hanafi rulings on the sale of grains, debts, and claims. It begins by quoting a previous statement about selling grains by measure except for gold and silver (النقدين), criticizing those who speak without rule. It then cites al-Siraj al-Wahhaj stating that division (qisma) is like sale regarding riba; if done by guess in ribawi items, it is invalid. Next, it quotes al-Umda that buying wheat before harvest by measure is valid because the wheat exists, and similarly stalks and straw before stacking and winnowing. al-Qunyah allows selling wheat in its ear by measure or weight even before the grains harden. The commentator notes that the author (المصنف) should have added 'by weight and by guess with other than its genus' for completeness. Then it presents a ruling from al-Bazzaziyyah: selling wheat for dirhams by weight is permissible, and selling items that do not vary (like wheat) without specific indication is allowed if the seller owns the entire quantity. However, if the seller says 'a hundred from this wheat' but delivers from another heap, it is invalid due to specification. It also discusses a case where a debtor sells the wheat he owes on credit—this is invalid as a guarantee; the trick (حيلة) is to sell it to a third party for a garment, take possession, then sell the garment for dirhams on credit. Lexical notes define 'كدس' as a heap of grain, 'عرمة' and 'صبرة' after threshing. From al-Zahirah: selling ripe wheat is valid as existing; selling straw is invalid as non-existent. From al-Dhakhira: a scenario where a claimant claims something measurable/weighable, the defendant buys it, but they later agree the debt was false. The contract is void because it was selling a non-existent thing. Different rules apply if the claimed item is dirhams/dinars vs. fulus. Then from Da'wa al-Bazzaziyyah: claiming wheat without specifying the reason (salam, loan, etc.) is invalid. Finally, from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and al-Muntaqa: a buyer who asks to be sold a specific karr of wheat, receives it without measuring, then measures it alone—the measurement is valid, but he is not believed about any deficiency because he already affirmed the full measure. Al-Ramli comments on the permissibility of selling wheat in the ear. The passage concludes that the seller's word is taken due to his affirmation.

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Guarantee or liability, often used for debt.
حيلة (ḥīlah): A legal trick or stratagem to achieve a permissible outcome while avoiding a prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first step in the hila to validly sell a debt of wheat on credit?
A) The debtor sells the wheat to the creditor for cash.
B) The debtor sells the wheat to a third party for a tangible item (spot).
C) The creditor forgives the debt.
D) The debtor transfers the debt to a third party.

Key Takeaway
Selling a debt on credit is invalid, but a permissible hila exists by involving a third party and a spot purchase of a tangible item.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Rulings on Claims and Contracts When the Debt is Nonexistent
Rulings on the validity of a purchase when the underlying claim is false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the validity of a purchase when the underlying claim is found to be false, distinguishing between measured/weighed items and currency.

Core Idea
If a claimed debt is later proven nonexistent, the contract to buy that debt is void for goods but may survive for currency under certain conditions.

This lesson examines a scenario from al-Dhakhirah: a claimant (C) claims a quantity of measured/weighed/counted goods from the defendant (D). D then buys those goods from C for a price, but later both agree that C had no right. The contract's validity depends on whether the parties have separated and on the type of thing claimed (goods vs. dirhams/dinars vs. fulus). This lesson unpacks these nuanced rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of the claimed item
Is it something measurable/weighable (e.g., wheat) or currency (dirhams/dinars/fulus)? The ruling differs.
Measurable items are considered generic in dhimmah; currency can be specified.
2
Determine if parties have separated
If they have parted (tafarruq) before discovering the truth, the contract is void for measurable items and for dirhams/dinars, but not for fulus.
Parting is relevant because it signifies finality of the sale.
3
Apply the rule for measured/weighed/counted items
For such items, the contract is void whether they parted or not, because the subject matter (the debt) never existed.
The sale was of a non-existent thing (ma'dum).
4
Apply the rule for dirhams and dinars
If they have not parted and the buyer returns the same amount, the contract is valid; if they have parted, it is void.
Before parting, the contract can be corrected by replacing the subject matter with a real debt.
5
Apply the rule for fulus (copper coins)
The contract remains valid even after parting, because in a sale of fulus for dirhams, receipt of one side (the dirhams) is sufficient for validity.
Fulus are considered non-ribawi with dirhams, so the sale is not dependent on the existence of the debt.

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of various Hanafi rulings on the sale of grains, debts, and claims. It begins by quoting a previous statement about selling grains by measure except for gold and silver (النقدين), criticizing those who speak without rule. It then cites al-Siraj al-Wahhaj stating that division (qisma) is like sale regarding riba; if done by guess in ribawi items, it is invalid. Next, it quotes al-Umda that buying wheat before harvest by measure is valid because the wheat exists, and similarly stalks and straw before stacking and winnowing. al-Qunyah allows selling wheat in its ear by measure or weight even before the grains harden. The commentator notes that the author (المصنف) should have added 'by weight and by guess with other than its genus' for completeness. Then it presents a ruling from al-Bazzaziyyah: selling wheat for dirhams by weight is permissible, and selling items that do not vary (like wheat) without specific indication is allowed if the seller owns the entire quantity. However, if the seller says 'a hundred from this wheat' but delivers from another heap, it is invalid due to specification. It also discusses a case where a debtor sells the wheat he owes on credit—this is invalid as a guarantee; the trick (حيلة) is to sell it to a third party for a garment, take possession, then sell the garment for dirhams on credit. Lexical notes define 'كدس' as a heap of grain, 'عرمة' and 'صبرة' after threshing. From al-Zahirah: selling ripe wheat is valid as existing; selling straw is invalid as non-existent. From al-Dhakhira: a scenario where a claimant claims something measurable/weighable, the defendant buys it, but they later agree the debt was false. The contract is void because it was selling a non-existent thing. Different rules apply if the claimed item is dirhams/dinars vs. fulus. Then from Da'wa al-Bazzaziyyah: claiming wheat without specifying the reason (salam, loan, etc.) is invalid. Finally, from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and al-Muntaqa: a buyer who asks to be sold a specific karr of wheat, receives it without measuring, then measures it alone—the measurement is valid, but he is not believed about any deficiency because he already affirmed the full measure. Al-Ramli comments on the permissibility of selling wheat in the ear. The passage concludes that the seller's word is taken due to his affirmation.

Key Terms
مكيل (makīl): Measured by volume (kayl).
موزون (mawzūn): Weighed item.
معدود (ma'dūd): Counted item (e.g., eggs, nuts).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a false claim of dirhams, when can the purchase contract be valid?
A) Always, as long as the price is paid.
B) Only if the parties have not parted and the buyer returns the same amount.
C) Only if the claim was for dinars, not dirhams.
D) Never, because it is selling a non-existent thing.

Key Takeaway
When a claimed debt is false, the purchase contract is void for measurable/weighable goods, but may be salvaged for dirhams/dinars if parties haven't parted, and for fulus it remains valid.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Selling with an Unknown Container or Stone: Permissible Ignorance in Immediate Sales
Ruling on validity of selling by measure using an unknown container or stone

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which selling by an unknown container or stone is valid in the Hanafi school, distinguish between permissible and impermissible jahala, and identify the buyer's option (khiyar).

Core Idea
Immediate delivery makes ignorance of quantity harmless – sale is valid with buyer's option.

The Hanafi school permits selling by an unknown container or stone because ignorance that does not lead to dispute is tolerated. This lesson explores the reasoning, conditions, and limits of this permissibility, showing how the school balances the practical need for transactional fluidity with the requirement of certainty in sales.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rationale
Analyze why ignorance of quantity is allowed: because delivery is immediate, destruction before delivery is rare, and thus no dispute arises. This distinguishes it from delayed transactions like salam.
The key idea is 'jahala that does not lead to dispute' (jahala la tufdi ila al-munaza'a).
2
Identify the conditions
Note the restrictions: the container or stone must not be prone to crumbling or decreasing (e.g., wood or iron, not basket or watermelon). Delivery must be hand to hand (immediate).
If the container is likely to change, the sale is not permissible because future dispute becomes probable.
3
Examine the distinction from salam
Salam (forward sale) requires exact quantity because delivery is deferred and destruction is not rare. The unknown container sale is unlike salam because possession is immediate.
This distinction is crucial to avoid confusion with other types of sales where jahala is prohibited.
4
Determine the consequences
The sale is valid (sahih) but not binding immediately; the buyer has an option (khiyar) to rescind upon discovering the true quantity.
This option protects the buyer from hidden disadvantage while allowing the transaction to proceed.

And it was absolute but it is restricted to when the stone does not likely crumble and the container does not likely decrease, like if it is of wood or iron; if it likely does so, it is not permissible like the basket, the sacks, the cucumber, and the watermelon. And some relied upon that, and it is nothing. For indeed sale by the weight of a specific stone is only valid with the condition of prompt delivery, and no drying causes a decrease in that time. And what may occur of its delay a day or two is prohibited; rather it is not permissible just as it is not permissible in salam in the weight of that stone, fear of destruction, so delivery becomes impossible and dispute arises preventing it. And the purpose is that the minimum term of salam is three days, and there is no doubt that delay of delivery to another session leads to dispute because its destruction, if rare, then disagreement about whether it is it or something else and suspicion in it is not rare. And all expressions indicate the restriction of validity of sale in that to promptness, as in the expression of al-Mabsut where he said: if he buys with this container hand to hand, there is no harm. Then, in the specified, sale by approximation is permissible, so by an unknown measure is more appropriate. This is because delivery immediately after the sale, to the end of what he mentioned. End. Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling for selling by volume using a container or stone whose capacity or weight is unknown. The author of al-Hidaya initially states permissibility because such ignorance does not lead to dispute since delivery is immediate and destruction before delivery is rare. He refutes the objection reported by al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (a Nawadir transmission) that it is impermissible due to ignorance. He also distinguishes it from salam (forward sale) where knowing the quantity is necessary because delivery is postponed and destruction is not rare. The permissibility is conditioned on the container or stone not being subject to change (like wood or iron). If it is prone to crumbling or decreasing (like a basket or watermelon), it is not permissible. Then he discusses specific cases: filling a specific waterskin with water from the Nile – Abu Hanifa says not permissible because the volume of the waterskin is unknown; Abu Yusuf allows it if both parties agree after filling. He also cites opinions from al-Muhit, al-Khulasa, and others. The key issue is that the permissibility of sale using an unknown container is for the validity of the contract, not that it is binding. Thus, the buyer has an option (khiyar) to rescind if he discovers the exact quantity. The researcher in Fath al-Qadir criticizes those who interpret Abu Hanifa's 'not permissible' as meaning 'not binding', asserting that the correct position is permissibility with immediate delivery. The passage concludes that all expressions require prompt delivery (hand to hand). This is a classic Hanafi discussion on the threshold of permissible ignorance (jahala) in sales.

Key Terms
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance of the exact measure or weight of the sold item.
خيار (khiyar): Option for the buyer to rescind the sale upon discovering the true quantity.
يداً بيد (yadan bi yad): Hand to hand, i.e., immediate delivery and payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The bag must be made of non-perishable material.
B) The water must be measured before filling.
C) The buyer must have the option to return.
D) The sale must be in a basin.

Key Takeaway
Selling by an unknown container is valid in Hanafi law provided delivery is immediate and the container is durable, with the buyer retaining an option.

Selling a Heap at a Unit Price: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale of heap

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the sale of a heap at a unit price, explain their respective proofs, and evaluate the different tarjih positions among later Hanafi scholars.

Core Idea
Unknown total quantity defaults to the minimum known unit (Abu Hanifa) vs. ignorance is removable (Sahibayn).

The Hanafi school is built on rigorous textual evidence and analogical reasoning, yet even within the school, the Imams sometimes disagreed. This lesson explores a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: when someone sells a heap of grain saying 'each sa' for a dirham,' is the sale valid for the whole heap or only for one sa'? The answer hinges on how we treat ignorance (jahala) of the total quantity and whether it can be removed by the parties. By the end, you will not only know each position but also understand the methodological differences that led to them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and legal issue
First, recognize that the seller offers a heap (sibra) at a unit price per measure (e.g., each sa' for a dirham). The total quantity and total price are unknown at the time of contract. The legal issue: does this jahala invalidate the sale for the entire heap, or does it only affect the contract's scope?
This is a sale of a determinate object (the heap) with an indeterminate quantity attached to a unit price.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his evidence
Abu Hanifa holds that the sale is valid only for one sa' (the minimum known unit) unless the named measures or total price is specified. His proof: the jahala prevents the contract from applying to the whole; it defaults to the least known unit. The ignorance is removed only by naming all measures or measuring in the session.
His reasoning is based on the maxim that jahala in essential terms leads to limitation to the known minimum.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their evidence
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the sale is valid absolutely for the entire heap. Their proof: jahala is removable by the parties (by measuring) and should not invalidate the contract, analogized to the case where a buyer has an option to choose one of two slaves.
They argue that similar removable ignorance is tolerated in other sales, so here it is not a fatal defect.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response to the analogy
Abu Hanifa responds that the analogy is imperfect: in the option case, the ignorance is about which item is selected, but the items themselves are known (specific slaves). Here, the quantity of the whole heap is unknown, so the comparison does not hold.
This is a typical Hanafi approach: strict qiyas requires the underlying cause (illa) to be identical.
5
Evaluate the tarjih among later scholars
Later Hanafi scholars differed on which view to prefer. Al-Hidaya appears to favor the Sahibayn (delaying their proof). Al-Khulasa explicitly states the fatwa is on their position for facilitation (taysir). But scholars like Fath al-Qadir and al-Kafi prefer Abu Hanifa's view for its stronger evidence. You must understand that both preferences exist: one for ease of practice, the other for evidentiary strength.
This illustrates the principle that in furu', tarjih may consider both strength of dalil and practical benefit (taysir).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن باع صبرة كل صاع بدرهم صح في صاع) يعني عند أبي حنيفة إلا أن يسمي جميع قفزانها أو جميع ثمنها. وقالا : يصح مطلقاً. له أنه تعذر الصرف إلى الكل لجهالة المبيع والثمن فينصرف إلى الأقل وهو معلوم إلا أن تزول الجهالة بتسمية جميع القفزان أو بالكيل في المجلس ولهما أن الجهالة بيدهما إزالتها ومثلها غير مانع كما إذا باع عبداً من عبدين على أن المشتري بالخيار ولم يذكر المصنف الخيار على قوله قالوا وله الخيار في الواحد كما إذا رآه ولم يكن رآه وقت البيع. وظاهر ما في الهداية ترجيح قولهما لتأخيره دليلهما كما هو عادته وقد صرح في الخلاصة في نظيره بأن الفتوى على قولهما فقال : رجل اشترى العنب كل وقر بكذا والوقر عندهم معروف؛ إن كان العنب عندهم من جنس واحد يجب أن يجوز في وقر واحد عند أبي حنيفة كما في بيع الصبرة الحجر الخشية الهلاك فيتعذر التسليم وتقع المنازعة المانعة منه والغرض أن أقل مدة السلم ثلاثة أيام، ولا شك أن تأخر التسليم فيه إلى مجلس آخر يفضي إلى المنازعة لأن هلاكه إن ندر فالاختلاف في هو أو غيره والتهمة فيه ليس بنادر وكل العبارات تفيد تقيد صحة البيع في ذلك بالتعجيل كما في عبارة المبسوط حيث قال : لو اشترى بهذا الإناء يداً بيد فلا بأس به، ثم إن في المعين البيع مجازفة يجوز فبمكيال غير معروف أولى، وهذا لأن التسليم عقيب البيع إلى آخر ما ذكر . اهـ كلام المحقق سقى الله ضريحه صيب العفو والرضوان قوله : (وقد صرح في الخلاصة في نظيره الخ) قال في النهر : وفي عيون المذاهب به يفتى لا لضعف دليل الإمام بل تيسيراً على الناس، وكأنه في البحر لم يطلع على هذا فقال : رجح قولهما في الخلاصة في نظيره .اهـ. وعزا في الدر المختار مثل ما في النهر إلى الشرنبلالية عن البرهان والقهستاني عن المحيط وغيره :قلت لكن قرر في الفتح دليل قوله ودليل قولهما ثم قال : وحينئذ ترجح قول أبي حنيفة ثم قال : وتأخير صاحب الهداية دليلهما ظاهر في ترجيحه قولهما وهو ممنوع اهـ. وفي تصحيح الشيخ قاسم قال في شرح الهداية. يرجح قول أبي حنيفة وكذا رجحه في الكافي واعتمده المحبوبي والنسفي وصدر الشريعة، وكذا في بيع القطيع والزرع والله تعالى أعلم .اهـ. وقد يقال : إن هذا ترجيح له من حيث قوة الدليل والأول ترجيح له من
His saying: (And whoever sells a heap, each sa' for a dirham, it is valid for one sa') meaning according to Abu Hanifa unless he names all its measures or all its price. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it is valid absolutely. His saying: (And he explicitly stated in al-Khulasa in its like,) said in al-Nahr: And in Uyun al-Madhahib, the fatwa is given according to it, not due to weakness of the Imam's proof but to facilitate for the people. And it is as if in al-Bahr he did not see this, so he said: he preferred their position in al-Khulasa in its like. End. And he attributed in al-Durr al-Mukhtar like what is in al-Nahr to al-Sharnabaliyya from al-Burhan and al-Qahistani from al-Muhit and others. I said: But in al-Fath he established the proof of his (Abu Hanifa's) position and the proof of their position, then he said: In this case, the position of Abu Hanifa is preferred. Then he said: And the author of al-Hidaya's delaying of their proof is apparent in preferring their position, and that is disputed. End. And in Tashih al-Shaykh Qasim, he said in Sharh al-Hidaya: The position of Abu Hanifa is preferred, and likewise it was preferred in al-Kafi, and relied upon by al-Mahbubi, al-Nasafi, and Sadr al-Shari'a. And likewise in the sale of the flock and the crop. And Allah knows best. End. It may be said: This is a preference for him (Abu Hanifa) in terms of the strength of the proof, and the first is a preference for him in terms of facilitation.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of a heap (sibra) where each unit measure (e.g., sa') is sold for a fixed price (e.g., one dirham). Abu Hanifa rules that the sale is valid only for one sa' (the minimum known quantity) unless the total number of measures or the total price is specified. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule that it is valid absolutely, without restriction. Abu Hanifa's reasoning: since the exact quantity and total price are unknown, the contract cannot apply to the whole heap; it automatically applies to the least known unit (one sa'). This ignorance is removed only if the seller names the total number of measures or measures the heap in the session. The Sahibayn argue that the ignorance can be removed by the parties themselves (by measuring) and similar ignorance does not prevent validity, as in the case of selling one of two slaves with option. The author notes that al-Hidaya appears to favor the Sahibayn by delaying their proof. Al-Khulasa explicitly says the fatwa is on the Sahibayn's position for facilitation (taysir). However, later scholars like Fath al-Qadir and al-Kafi prefer Abu Hanifa's view on strength of proof. The passage ends by noting that there are two types of preference: one based on strong evidence (Abu Hanifa) and one based on facilitation (Sahibayn). The practical effect: today, many Hanafi fatwas follow the Sahibayn for ease.

Key Terms
صبرة (sibrah): A heap or pile of goods (like grain) sold without exact measurement.
جهالة المبيع والثمن (jahalat al-mabi' wa al-thaman): Ignorance of the exact quantity of the sold item and the total price.
قفزان (qafzan): Measures; the plural of qafiz, a unit of volume.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), what is the ruling when someone sells a heap of grain saying 'each sa' for a dirham'?
A) The sale is valid only for one sa'.
B) The sale is void due to jahala.
C) The sale is valid for the entire heap.
D) The sale is valid if the buyer accepts the option.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the sale of a heap at a unit price is valid, but the scope of validity (one unit vs. whole heap) is disputed between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, with later tarjih varying between evidence strength and facilitation.
