المثلي والقيمي لزوال المانع. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا سمى في العقد أو بعده بشرط المجلس وبعده لا لأن ساعات المجلس تعتبر ساعة واحدة دفعاً للعسر فالعلم في المجلس كالعلم حالة العقد، ولا ينقلب جائزاً بالعلم بعد المجلس لتقرر الفساد للجهالة. وما في المحيط عن بعض المشايخ أن عنده يصح في الكل وإن علم بعد المجلس بعيد لما قررناه . وشمل تسمية جميع الثمن وجميع المبيع لما قدمنا أن تسمية جملة الثمن كافية للصحة كتسمية المبيع، وقد صرح به في السراج الوهاج وفي القنية اشترى من البقول عشرة أمناء الجزر من جزر له كثير صح كعشرة أقفزة من الحنطة لأن المشاحة لا تجري فيه، ولو قال على أن اختار منها لا يصح . قال اشتريت منك ألف من من هذه الحنطة فوزنت فإذا هي خمسمائة؛ قيل صح في الموجود، وقيل لا لأن الفساد قوي فيتعدى إليه النقص صح في الموجود اتفاقاً، وكذا في العدديات المتقاربة. وإنما الخلاف في العدديات المتفاوتة إذا وجدها أنقص. وفي البدائع : لو قال بعت منك هذا القطيع كل شاتين بعشرين فالبيع فاسد في الكل إجماعاً وإن علم المشتري العدد في المجلس واختار قوله : (وإن نقص كيل أخذ بحصته أو ترك وإن زاد فللبائع) متفرع على قوله وإن سمى الكل يعني إذا سمى الجملة لو نقص عما سماه في المثليات خير لتفرق الصفقة عليه فلم يتم رضاؤه بالموجود وإن زاد شيء عليه فهو للبائع لأن البيع وقع على مقدار معين والقدر ليس بوصف . وفي غاية البيان: وكذا الحكم في كل مكيل أو موزون ليس في تبعيضه ضرر قيد بكونه بيع مكايلة لأنه لو اشترى حنطة مجازفة في البيت فوجد تحتها دكاناً فله الخيار إن شاء أخذها بجميع الثمن وإن شاء تركها، وكذا لو اشترى بئراً من حنطة على أنها كذا وكذا ذراعاً فإذا أقل من ذلك فله الخيار، ولو كان طعاماً في حب فإذا نصفه تبن يأخذه بنصف الثمن لأن الحب وعاء يكال فيه فصار المبيع حنطة مقدرة، والبيت والبئر لا يكال بهما فصار المبيع حنطة غير مقدرة ولكن البائع أطعمه اشترى عشر شياه من مائة شاة أو عشر بطيخات من وقر فالبيع باطل وكذا الرمان. ولو عزلها البائع وقبلها المشتري جاز استحساناً والعزل والقبول بمنزلة إيجاب وقبول اهـ ومثله في التتارخانية وغيرها. قوله : (ولو) سمى الكل في الكل صح) أي لو المبيع صح في المثلي والقيمي لزوال المانع. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا سمى في العقد أو بعده بشرط المجلس وبعده لا لأن ساعات المجلس تعتبر ساعة واحدة دفعاً للعسر فالعلم في المجلس كالعلم حالة العقد، ولا ينقلب جائزاً بالعلم بعد المجلس لتقرر الفساد للجهالة. وما في المحيط عن بعض المشايخ أن عنده يصح في الكل وإن علم بعد المجلس بعيد لما قررناه . وشمل تسمية جميع الثمن وجميع المبيع لما قدمنا أن تسمية جملة الثمن كافية للصحة كتسمية المبيع، وقد صرح به في السراج الوهاج وفي القنية اشترى من البقول عشرة أمناء الجزر من جزر له كثير صح كعشرة أقفزة من الحنطة لأن المشاحة لا تجري فيه، ولو قال على أن اختار منها لا يصح . قال اشتريت منك ألف من من هذه الحنطة فوزنت فإذا هي خمسمائة؛ قيل صح في الموجود، وقيل لا لأن الفساد قوي فيتعدى إليه النقص صح في الموجود اتفاقاً، وكذا في العدديات المتقاربة. وإنما الخلاف في العدديات المتفاوتة إذا وجدها أنقص. وفي البدائع : لو قال بعت منك هذا القطيع كل شاتين بعشرين فالبيع فاسد في الكل إجماعاً وإن علم المشتري العدد في المجلس واختار قوله : (وإن نقص كيل أخذ بحصته أو ترك وإن زاد فللبائع) متفرع على قوله وإن سمى الكل يعني إذا سمى الجملة لو نقص عما سماه في المثليات خير لتفرق الصفقة عليه فلم يتم رضاؤه بالموجود وإن زاد شيء عليه فهو للبائع لأن البيع وقع على مقدار معين والقدر ليس بوصف . وفي غاية البيان: وكذا الحكم في كل مكيل أو موزون ليس في تبعيضه ضرر قيد بكونه بيع مكايلة لأنه لو اشترى حنطة مجازفة في البيت فوجد تحتها دكاناً فله الخيار إن شاء أخذها بجميع الثمن وإن شاء تركها، وكذا لو اشترى بئراً من حنطة على أنها كذا وكذا ذراعاً فإذا أقل من ذلك فله الخيار، ولو كان طعاماً في حب فإذا نصفه تبن يأخذه بنصف الثمن لأن الحب وعاء يكال فيه فصار المبيع حنطة مقدرة، والبيت والبئر لا يكال بهما فصار المبيع حنطة غير مقدرة ولكن البائع أطعمه اشترى عشر شياه من مائة شاة أو عشر بطيخات من وقر فالبيع باطل وكذا الرمان. ولو عزلها البائع وقبلها المشتري جاز استحساناً والعزل والقبول بمنزلة إيجاب وقبول اهـ ومثله في التتارخانية وغيرها. وفي غاية البيان: وكذا الحكم في كل مكيل أو موزون ليس في تبعيضه ضرر قيد بكونه بيع مكايلة لأنه لو اشترى حنطة مجازفة في البيت فوجد تحتها دكاناً فله الخيار إن شاء أخذها بجميع الثمن وإن شاء تركها، وكذا لو اشترى بئراً من حنطة على أنها كذا وكذا ذراعاً فإذا أقل من ذلك فله الخيار، ولو كان طعاماً في حب فإذا نصفه تبن يأخذه بنصف الثمن لأن الحب وعاء يكال فيه فصار المبيع حنطة مقدرة، والبيت والبئر لا يكال بهما فصار المبيع حنطة غير مقدرة ولكن البائع أطعمه اشترى عشر شياه من مائة شاة أو عشر بطيخات من وقر فالبيع باطل وكذا الرمان. ولو عزلها البائع وقبلها المشتري جاز استحساناً والعزل والقبول بمنزلة إيجاب وقبول اهـ ومثله في التتارخانية وغيرها. ويوجد في بعض النسخ زيادة: ولو اشترى سمكة على أنها عشرة أرطال ووزن البائع عليه فوجد المشتري في بطنها حجراً يزن ثلاثة أرطال فهو بالخيار إن شاء أخذها بجميع الثمن وإن شاء ترك لأن الوزن ههنا جارٍ مجرى الجودة والوزن قد يجري مجرى في بعض الأشياء كما في اللآلئ والجواهر وههنا كذلك وفوات الوزن بمنزلة العيب. فإن شواها قبل أن يعلم والمسألة بحالها تقوم السمكة عشرة أرطال وتقوم سبعة فيرجع بحصة ما بينهما من الثمن لأنه تعذر الرد بالعيب فيرجع بنقصان العيب؛ كذا في المحيط . ومسألة السمكة خارجة عن حكم الموزونات فإن الحكم في الموزونات التخير عند النقصان إن شاء أخذ الموجود بحصته من الثمن، وإن شاء ترك وحكمها التخيير بين الأخذ بجميع الثمن أو الفسخ، ولا خصوصية للسمكة بل كل موزون في تبعيضه ضرر كذلك ولذا قال في الخانية : رجل باع لؤلؤة على أنها تزن مثقالاً فوجدها أكثر سلمت للمشتري لأن الوزن فيما يضره التبعيض وصف بمنزلة الذرعان في الثوب اهـ. وفي الخلاصة : اشترى طشتاً على أنه عشرة أمناء فبان بعد القبض أنه خمسة أمناء خير المشتري لأنه بمنزلة العيب، فإن حدث به عيب عنده وأبى البائع قبوله قوم طشت من عشرة أمناء مثلاً بعشرين، وقوم من خمسة أمناء بعشرة أمناء فالعيب ينقص خمسة ا هـ . والقول للقابض في الزيادة والنقصان وعليها يتفرع ما في الخانية : ولو باع من آخر إبريسماً فوزنه البائع على المشتري فذهب به المشتري ثم جاء بعد مدة وقال وجدته ناقصاً ؛ إن كان يعلم أنه انتقص من الهواء لا شيء على البائع، وكذا لو كان النقصان مما يجري بين الوزنين وإن لم يكن النقصان من الهواء ولا يجري بين الوزنين فإن لم يكن المشتري أقر أنه قبض كذا أمناء فله أن يمنع حصة النقصان من الثمن إن كان لم ينقده الثمن، فإن كان نقده الثمن رجع عليه بذلك القدر، وإن كان المشتري أقر أنه قبض كذا وجدته أقل من ذلك فليس له أن يمنع من البائع شيئاً من الثمن ولا يسترده اهـ. وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان المسمى مشروطاً باللفظ أو بالعادة لما في البزازية : اتفق أهل بلدة على سعر الخبز واللحم وشاع على وجه لا يتفاوت فأعطى رجل ثمناً واشتراه وأعطاه أقل من المتعارف؛ إن كان من أهل البلدة يرجع بالنقصان فيهما من الثمن، وإن كان من غير أهلها رجع في الخبز لأن التسعير فيه متعارف فيلزم الكل لا في اللحم فلا اهـ. وفي البزازية أيضاً: اشترى عنب كرم على أنه ألف من فظهر أنه تسعمائة طالب البائع بحصة مائة من من الثمن، وعلى قياس قول الإمام يفسد العقد في الباقي، وكان قاضي الحرمين يروي عن الإمام فيما وجد، وبه أفتى الصدر هذا. وأفتى الحلواني والسرخسي على أن العقد من جنس أمناء ثم قال يصح يعم الشهيد. وفي المحيط : اشترى نصف ما في الكرم المعين من العنب الذي على الكرم على أنه خمسمائة من بجوز، وجد ذلك القدر أو أقل أو أكثر. وذكر اللامشي أنما يجوز إذا وجد خمسمائة، ولو قال بعت ألف منّ من هذا الكرم إن كان العنب من نوع واحد يجوز. وفي
A group is sold with others attached to it, and it is unknown and not known. If that is in something measured by volume, weight, or an approximate countable, it is permissible as in al-Khaniyya. In al-Qamus: 'al-thulla' is a group of sheep, or many of them, or specifically ewes, and the plural is 'kundur' and 'thalal' – conclusion. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: al-Halwani said – may Allah have mercy on him – the most correct is that with Abu Hanifa, if his knowledge encompasses the number of sheep in the session, the contract does not become valid, but if the seller and buyer are satisfied, the sale is concluded by mutual consent; as in al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyya, and its analog is sale by number – conclusion. And in al-Bada'i': and upon this is the dispute regarding weighed items whose division causes harm, like wrought vessels and boxes – conclusion. His saying: (And if) he specifies the whole in the whole, it is valid – i.e., if the sold item is valid in fungibles and non-fungibles due to removal of the impediment. It is absolute, so it includes if he specifies in the contract or after it on condition of the session, and after it not because the moments of the session are considered one moment to avoid hardship, so knowledge in the session is like knowledge at the time of the contract, and it does not become valid by knowledge after the session because the invalidity due to ignorance is settled. And what is in al-Muhit from some shaykhs that with him it is valid in the whole even if he knows after the session is unlikely for what we have established. And it includes specifying the entire price and the entire sold item because we have stated that specifying the total price is sufficient for validity like specifying the sold item, and it is stated explicitly in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and in al-Qunyah: if one buys ten manns of carrots from his many carrots, it is valid like ten qafiz of wheat because quibbling does not apply to it, but if he says 'on condition that I choose from them', it is not valid. He said: I bought from you a thousand mann of this wheat, then he weighed and it was five hundred; it was said it is valid for what exists, and it was said no because the invalidity is strong and the deficiency affects it; it is valid for what exists by agreement, and similarly in approximate countables. The dispute is only in uneven countables if he finds them less. In al-Bada'i': if he said 'I sold you this flock, every two sheep for twenty', the sale is invalid in the whole by consensus even if the buyer knew the number in the session and chose. His saying: (And if the measure is deficient, he takes for his share or leaves, and if it exceeds, it is for the seller) is a branch of his saying 'and if he specifies the whole', meaning if he specifies the total, if it is less than what he specified in fungibles, he is given a choice because the price is scattered upon him, so his satisfaction with what exists is not complete, and if something exceeds, it is for the seller because the sale was for a specified amount and quantity is not a description. In Ghayat al-Bayan: and similarly the ruling in every measured by volume or weight whose division does not cause harm, restricted to sale by measure because if he bought wheat unsight unseen in the house and found underneath it a storage, he has the option: if he wishes, he takes it for the full price, and if he wishes, he leaves it. Similarly, if he bought a pit of wheat on the basis that it was such and such cubits, and it was less, he has the option. And if food was in a container and half of it was chaff, he takes it for half the price because the container is a vessel in which it is measured, so the sold item becomes estimated wheat, while the house and pit are not measured with them, so the sold item becomes non-estimated wheat, but the seller informed him. He bought ten sheep from a hundred sheep or ten watermelons from a load – the sale is invalid, and similarly pomegranates. If the seller separates them and the buyer accepts, it is permissible by istihsan, and the separation and acceptance are like offer and acceptance – conclusion. Similar is in al-Tatarkhaniyya and others. The ruling: (And if) he specifies the whole in the whole, it is valid – i.e., if the sold item is valid in fungibles and non-fungibles due to removal of the impediment. It is absolute, so it includes if he specifies in the contract or after it on condition of the session, and after it not because the moments of the session are considered one moment to avoid hardship, so knowledge in the session is like knowledge at the time of the contract, and it does not become valid by knowledge after the session because the invalidity due to ignorance is settled. And what is in al-Muhit from some shaykhs that with him it is valid in the whole even if he knows after the session is unlikely for what we have established. And it includes specifying the entire price and the entire sold item because we have stated that specifying the total price is sufficient for validity like specifying the sold item, and it is stated explicitly in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and in al-Qunyah: if one buys ten manns of carrots from his many carrots, it is valid like ten qafiz of wheat because quibbling does not apply to it, but if he says 'on condition that I choose from them', it is not valid. He said: I bought from you a thousand mann of this wheat, then he weighed and it was five hundred; it was said it is valid for what exists, and it was said no because the invalidity is strong and the deficiency affects it; it is valid for what exists by agreement, and similarly in approximate countables. The dispute is only in uneven countables if he finds them less. In al-Bada'i': if he said 'I sold you this flock, every two sheep for twenty', the sale is invalid in the whole by consensus even if the buyer knew the number in the session and chose. His saying: (And if the measure is deficient, he takes for his share or leaves, and if it exceeds, it is for the seller) is a branch of his saying 'and if he specifies the whole', meaning if he specifies the total, if it is less than what he specified in fungibles, he is given a choice because the price is scattered upon him, so his satisfaction with what exists is not complete, and if something exceeds, it is for the seller because the sale was for a specified amount and quantity is not a description. In Ghayat al-Bayan: and similarly the ruling in every measured by volume or weight whose division does not cause harm, restricted to sale by measure because if he bought wheat unsight unseen in the house and found underneath it a storage, he has the option: if he wishes, he takes it for the full price, and if he wishes, he leaves it. Similarly, if he bought a pit of wheat on the basis that it was such and such cubits, and it was less, he has the option. And if food was in a container and half of it was chaff, he takes it for half the price because the container is a vessel in which it is measured, so the sold item becomes estimated wheat, while the house and pit are not measured with them, so the sold item becomes non-estimated wheat, but the seller informed him. He bought ten sheep from a hundred sheep or ten watermelons from a load – the sale is invalid, and similarly pomegranates. If the seller separates them and the buyer accepts, it is permissible by istihsan, and the separation and acceptance are like offer and acceptance – conclusion. Similar is in al-Tatarkhaniyya and others. And in some copies there is an addition: if he bought a fish on the basis that it was ten pounds and the seller weighed it for him, and the buyer found in its stomach a stone weighing three pounds, he has the option: if he wishes, he takes it for the full price, and if he wishes, he leaves because weight here follows the pattern of quality, and weight may follow the pattern in some things as in pearls and jewels, and here similarly, and deficiency in weight is like a defect. If he cooked it before knowing, and the case is as it is, the fish is valued at ten pounds and valued at seven, so he recovers the share of the difference from the price because returning is impossible due to the defect, so he recovers the deficiency due to the defect; as in al-Muhit. And the case of the fish is outside the ruling of weighed items, for the ruling in weighed items is option at deficiency: if he wishes, he takes what exists for its share of the price, and if he wishes, he leaves. Its ruling is option between taking for the full price or rescission, and there is no specificity for fish; rather, every weighed item whose division causes harm is likewise. Therefore al-Khaniyya said: a man sold a pearl on the basis that it weighed a mithqal and found it more – it is delivered to the buyer because weight in what harms its division is a description like the measurement in cloth – conclusion. In al-Khulasa: if he bought a basin on the basis that it was ten manns and it became clear after possession that it was five manns, the buyer has the option because it is like a defect. If a defect occurs in his possession and the seller refuses to accept it, the basin is valued at ten manns e.g. twenty, and valued at five manns e.g. ten, so the defect decreases five – conclusion. And the statement of the possessor is believed concerning increase and decrease. And from it branches what is in al-Khaniyya: if someone sold silk to another and the seller weighed it for the buyer, and the buyer took it and came after a while and said he found it deficient: if it is known that it decreased from the air, nothing is upon the seller. Similarly if the decrease is of the type that occurs between weighings. And if the decrease is not from the air and not of the type between weighings, then if the buyer did not admit that he received such and such manns, he may withhold the share of the deficiency from the price if he had not paid the price. If he had paid the price, he recurs on him for that amount. If the buyer admitted that he received such and such and found it less, he cannot withhold anything from the seller nor recover it – conclusion. And it is absolute, so it includes if the specified amount is conditional by wording or by custom, as in al-Bazzaziyya: the people of a town agreed on the price of bread and meat, and it became widespread such that there is no variation. A man paid a price and bought it and gave less than the conventional amount: if he is from the town, he recovers the deficiency in both from the price; if he is not from the town, he recovers in bread because pricing is conventional in it so it binds everyone, not in meat – conclusion. And in al-Bazzaziyya also: someone bought grapes of a vineyard on the basis that it was a thousand mann and it turned out to be nine hundred – he claims from the seller the share of a hundred mann from the price. On the analogy of the Imam's saying, the contract is invalid for the remainder. And Qadi al-Haramayn narrated from the Imam concerning what exists, and al-Sadr gave fatwa accordingly. And al-Halwani and al-Sarakhsi gave fatwa that the contract is of the type of manns, then he said: it is valid, and it is general, al-Shahid. In al-Muhit: he bought half of what is in the specific vineyard of the grapes that are on the vine on the basis that it is five hundred manns of grapes; he finds that amount or less or more. And al-Lamishi mentioned that it is only permissible if he finds five hundred. And if he said 'I sold a thousand mann of this vineyard' if the grapes are of one type, it is valid.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the rules of sale when the total quantity of the sold item is specified and the actual quantity differs. The author (Zayla'i or Ibn Nujaym) explains various scenarios. The central ruling: if the seller specifies the total amount (e.g., 'I sell you the whole lot for X price'), the sale is valid for both fungibles (mithli) and non-fungibles (qimi). However, this validity requires that the buyer knows the total quantity either at the time of contract or during the session. If knowledge occurs after the session, the sale remains invalid due to uncertainty (jahl). There is a strong difference here: some scholars (like al-Halwani) hold that knowing the number in the session does not make the sale valid by itself; rather, the sale becomes valid only if both parties consent anew. But the author prefers the view that knowledge in the session is equivalent to knowledge at the moment of contract, because the entire session is considered a single unit to avoid hardship. When the actual quantity is less than specified, the buyer has a choice: either take the existing goods at a proportionate price, or cancel the sale. If the actual quantity is more, the excess belongs to the seller, because the sale was for a specific amount and quantity is not a description. The commentary then discusses special cases: items whose division causes harm (like pearls, fish, basins) are treated differently—here weight is considered a quality (sifa), and deficiency is treated like a defect ('ayb). The buyer has the option to take the item at full price or cancel, and if he cannot return it (e.g., after cooking), he gets compensation for the deficiency in value. The passage also covers rules for later claims of shortage: the buyer's word is believed regarding increase or decrease, but if he admitted receipt of a certain amount, his later claim is not accepted. Finally, local custom (like fixed bread prices) can affect whether the buyer can recover a shortfall. Throughout, the commentary references authoritative Hanafi texts: al-Khaniyya, al-Muhit, al-Bada'i', al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, al-Qunyah, al-Tatarkhaniyya, al-Bazzaziyya, and others.

Key Terms
عيب (ayb): A defect in the item that reduces its value or desirability; deficiency in weight in these items is treated as a defect.
تبعيض (tafriq): Division or subdivision; when dividing the item causes harm (like cutting a pearl), the item is treated specially.
وصف (sifa): A description or quality; in these items, weight is a description, not a quantity measure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the distinguishing factor that moves an item from the general deficiency rule to the defect rule?
A) Whether the item is a food product.
B) Whether the item is a living creature.
C) Whether dividing the item causes harm.
D) Whether the weight is specified in the contract.

Key Takeaway
For items whose division causes harm, weight is a quality; deficiency is a defect, not a mere quantity shortage, leading to different remedies.

8 min
The Fish with the Hidden Stone: Option for Deficiency in Indivisible Weighed Items
Ruling on option when a weighed item is deficient due to hidden object

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the ruling for divisible and indivisible weighed items when a deficiency in weight is discovered, and apply the correct option (khiyar) accordingly.

Core Idea
If a weighed item is indivisible without harm, a deficiency gives the buyer only full-price or cancel.

Imagine buying a fish that the seller claims weighs ten pounds, only to find a stone inside that reduces the fish's actual weight to seven. Does the buyer have to accept the deficiency at a reduced price, or can he cancel entirely? In Hanafi fiqh, the answer depends on whether the item can be divided and sold in parts. This passage from al-Muhit, as explained in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, clarifies that for items like fish, pearls, and jewels – where division would harm the value – the buyer's option is different from that for ordinary weighed goods like grain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the discovery
The buyer purchases a fish weighing 10 pounds, but finds a stone of 3 pounds inside. What options does he have initially?
Focus on the hidden stone as an unseen defect affecting weight.
2
Understand the default rule for weighed items (mawzunat)
For divisible weighed items (like grain), the buyer can either take the remaining weight for its share of the price or cancel the sale.
This is the general rule for mawzunat.
3
Note the exception for indivisible items
The author states that the fish case is outside the general rule because division is harmful. For such items, the buyer may only take the whole for full price or cancel; he cannot take a proportionate share.
The key distinction: divisibility vs. indivisibility.
4
Trace the legal reasoning (istihsan)
Weight deficiency is treated as a defect (ayb). If the item cannot be returned (e.g., cooked), compensation is proportional. The underlying principle is that weight here is like quality (joudah), as for pearls and jewels.
Istihsan overrides strict analogy to divisible items.
5
Apply to the cooked scenario
If the buyer cooked the fish before discovering the stone, return is impossible. He then recovers the proportion of the price corresponding to the weight loss, because he cannot exercise the cancellation option.
The compensation is based on valuation, not directly on the stone's weight proportion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لو اشترى سمكة على أنها عشرة أرطال ووزن البائع عليه فوجد المشتري في بطنها حجراً يزن ثلاثة أرطال فهو بالخيار إن شاء أخذها بجميع الثمن وإن شاء ترك لأن الوزن ههنا جارٍ مجرى الجودة والوزن قد يجري مجرى في بعض الأشياء كما في اللآلئ والجواهر وههنا كذلك وفوات الوزن بمنزلة العيب. فإن شواها قبل أن يعلم والمسألة بحالها تقوم السمكة عشرة أرطال وتقوم سبعة فيرجع بحصة ما بينهما من الثمن لأنه تعذر الرد بالعيب فيرجع بنقصان العيب؛ كذا في المحيط . ومطالب السمكة خارجة عن حكم الموزونات فإن الحكم في الموزونات التخير عند النقصان إن شاء أخذ الموجود بحصته من الثمن، وإن شاء ترك وحكمها التخيير بين الأخذ بجميع الثمن أو الفسخ، ولا خصوصية للسمكة بل كل موزون في تبعيضه ضرر كذلك
And if one buys a fish on the basis that it is ten pounds and the seller weighs it for him, then the buyer finds in its belly a stone weighing three pounds, he has the option: if he wishes he takes it for the full price, and if he wishes he leaves it. Because weight here runs in the way of quality, and weight may run in the way in some things like pearls and jewels, and here likewise, and loss of weight is like a defect. Then if he cooks it before knowing, and the case is as it was, the fish is valued at ten pounds and is valued at seven, so he recovers the proportion between them of the price because returning it is prevented by the defect, so he recovers the deficiency due to the defect. This is stated in al-Muhit. The case of the fish is outside the ruling of weighed items; for the ruling regarding weighed items is option at the deficiency: if he wishes he takes what exists for its share of the price, and if he wishes he leaves it. But its ruling is option between taking it for the full price or cancellation. And there is no specificity for the fish; rather every weighed item whose division is harmful is likewise.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from al-Muhit. It presents a specific case: a fish bought with a specified weight, but after weighing by the seller, the buyer discovers a stone inside that reduces the fish's actual weight by three pounds. The ruling: the buyer has an option (khiyar) either to take the fish for the full agreed price or to cancel the sale. The reason is that weight here is treated as a quality attribute, like the weight of pearls and jewels; deficiency in weight is akin to a defect. If the buyer cooks the fish before discovering the stone, returning it is impossible, so he cannot exercise the rescission option but instead claims compensation for the defect. The amount of compensation is the proportion of the price corresponding to the weight loss. Then the author distinguishes this case from the general rule for weighed items (mawzunat): in weighed items that can be divided without harm (like grain), the buyer has the option to take the remaining portion for a reduced price or cancel. But for items where division causes harm (like a fish, pearls, etc.), the buyer can either take the whole item for the full price (despite deficiency) or cancel entirely. The author emphasizes this is not specific to fish but applies to any indivisible weighed item.

Key Terms
الخيار (al-khiyar): The option given to the buyer to either uphold or rescind the sale due to deficiency in weight.
الموزونات (al-mawzunat): Weighed items that are typically sold by weight, such as grain, but here distinguished from items where division causes harm.
العيب (al-ayb): A defect that gives the buyer the option to return the item; here weight loss is treated as a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer purchases a fish weighing ten pounds but later finds a stone inside reducing its weight, what option does the buyer have?
A) He must keep the fish and pay the full price
B) He can only demand a refund of the stone's weight proportion
C) He may either take the fish for the full price or cancel the sale
D) He must return the fish immediately

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, a deficiency in weight of an indivisible item gives the buyer only the choice to take it for the full price or cancel, not to demand a proportionate discount.

Excess in Indivisible Weighed Items: Pearls
Ruling on excess in indivisible weighed items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on excess in weighed items that are harmed by division and apply the analogy to cloth measurement.

Core Idea
For items harmed by division, weight is a description; excess belongs to buyer without extra payment.

This passage from al-Khaniyyah addresses a specific scenario: a pearl sold with a specified weight turns out heavier. The ruling hinges on the nature of the good—pearls are indivisible without damage. The reasoning draws an analogy to cloth measured by the cubit, where excess similarly benefits the buyer. In this lesson, we unpack the legal logic and its implications for sale contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and discovery
A pearl is sold on the basis that it weighs exactly one mithqal. After delivery, the buyer finds it weighs more. Determine the initial condition (wazn as sharat) and the post-possession fact.
The seller specified weight as a description, not a condition of the contract.
2
Recognize the operative concept (tab'iid)
Pearls are harmed by division—cutting them destroys value. This makes weight a description (wasf) rather than a precise condition. The same principle applies to items like fish and cloth.
Tab'iid means splitting; here it refers to the harm caused by dividing the item.
3
Analogize to cloth measurement (qiyas)
The author compares weight of a pearl to the cubit (dhira') in cloth. If cloth is sold with a specified length but turns out longer, the extra goes to the buyer. Likewise, for pearls, excess weight is a benefit for the buyer.
The illa is that weight/measurement is a description that cannot be precisely enforced due to indivisibility.
4
Derive the ruling
The buyer keeps the pearl with the extra weight. The seller cannot demand additional payment, and the buyer does not need to return the excess. The contract is valid and the buyer gets the windfall.
This is a default Hanafi position; some other schools may require returning the excess or adjusting the price.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في الخانية : رجل باع لؤلؤة على أنها تزن مثقالاً فوجدها أكثر سلمت للمشتري لأن الوزن فيما يضره التبعيض وصف بمنزلة الذرعان في الثوب اهـ. وفي الخلاصة : اشترى طشتاً على أنه عشرة أمناء فبان بعد القبض أنه خمسة أمناء خير المشتري لأنه بمنزلة العيب، فإن حدث به عيب عنده وأبى البائع قبوله قوم طشت من عشرة أمناء مثلاً بعشرين، وقوم من خمسة أمناء بعشرة أمناء فالعيب ينقص خمسة ا هـ . والقول للقابض في الزيادة والنقصان وعليها يتفرع ما في الخانية : ولو باع من آخر إبريسماً فوزنه البائع على المشتري فذهب به المشتري ثم جاء بعد مدة وقال وجدته ناقصاً ؛ إن كان يعلم أنه انتقص من الهواء لا شيء على البائع، وكذا لو كان النقصان مما يجري بين الوزنين وإن لم يكن النقصان من الهواء ولا يجري بين الوزنين فإن لم يكن المشتري أقر أنه قبض كذا أمناء فله أن يمنع حصة النقصان من الثمن إن كان لم ينقده الثمن، فإن كان نقده الثمن رجع عليه بذلك القدر، وإن كان المشتري أقر أنه قبض كذا وجدته أقل من ذلك فليس له أن يمنع من البائع شيئاً من الثمن ولا يسترده اهـ
He said in al-Khaniyyah: A man sold a pearl on the basis that it weighs one mithqal, and he found it more; it is conceded to the buyer because weight in what harms division is a description like the cubit in cloth, end. And in al-Khulasah: He bought a basin on the basis that it is ten mann, and it became clear after possession that it is five mann; the buyer has an option because it is like a defect. Then if a defect occurred in his possession and the seller refused to accept it, the basin is valued as ten mann, for example, at twenty, and valued as five mann at ten; so the defect is a reduction of five, end. And the word is of the possessor regarding increase and decrease. Upon it branches what is in al-Khaniyyah: And if one sold to another silk, and the seller weighed it for the buyer, and the buyer took it, then came after a period and said, 'I found it deficient': if he knows that it decreased from the air, nothing is on the seller. Likewise if the decrease is of the sort that occurs between two weighings. And if the decrease is not from the air and does not occur between two weighings, then if the buyer has not acknowledged that he received such-and-such mann, he may withhold the share of the decrease from the price if he has not paid the price; if he has paid the price, he recovers that amount from him. And if the buyer acknowledged that he received such-and-such and found it less than that, he may not withhold anything from the seller from the price nor recover it, end.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage presents three rulings from Hanafi texts. The first, from al-Khaniyyah, concerns a pearl sold with a specified weight (one mithqal). If the buyer finds it heavier, the excess belongs to the buyer because for items like pearls, weight is a description akin to the cubit measurement in cloth; an excess does not harm the contract—the buyer gets the extra without additional payment. The second, from al-Khulasah, concerns a basin sold as ten mann but found to be five after possession. The buyer has an option because deficiency is like a defect. If a subsequent defect occurs in the buyer's possession and the seller refuses to take it back, the compensation is calculated: value of ten-mann basin minus value of five-mann basin, and the buyer recovers that difference. The third ruling, from al-Khaniyyah, deals with a buyer's later claim of deficiency in silk after possession. The key factors: if the loss is natural (from air) or typical between weighings, the seller is not liable. Otherwise, if the buyer did not acknowledge receipt of a specific weight, his word is accepted, and he can withhold or recover the deficiency. But if he acknowledged receipt, he cannot later claim deficiency.

Key Terms
القابض (al-qabid): The person who has taken possession (the buyer); his word is accepted regarding increase or decrease unless he acknowledged a specific amount.
التبعيض (al-tab'iid): Division or splitting; for items like pearls and fish, division causes harm, so the rule differs from divisible items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the buyer keep the excess weight in the pearl scenario?
A) Because the seller is presumed to have given extra as a gift.
B) Because weight in items harmed by division is a description, not a condition.
C) Because the buyer paid a higher price than expected.
D) Because the contract is void and the buyer must return it.

Key Takeaway
For indivisible goods, weight is a description; excess goes to the buyer automatically.

Deficiency in Weight-Specified Goods: Buyer's Option for a Basin
Ruling on deficiency in weight-specified item and option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the buyer's option when a weight-specified item is deficient after possession, including compensation calculation for subsequent defects.

Core Idea
Deficiency in weight is like a defect; buyer has option to retain with price reduction or return for full refund.

The second ruling from al-Khulasah deals with a basin sold as ten mann but found lighter. The deficiency is analogized to a defect ('ayb), giving the buyer a choice. The passage further addresses a complication: if a new defect occurs after possession and the seller refuses return, how should compensation be calculated? This lesson focuses on that scenario and the principle of 'the word of the possessor is accepted' that follows.

Guided Path
1
Note the initial transaction and discovery
A basin is bought for a specified weight (10 mann). After possession (qabd), it is found to be only 5 mann. This deficiency is like a defect.
Deficiency in a specified weight after possession gives rise to khyar al-ayb.
2
Identify the buyer's option
The buyer can choose to either keep the basin and claim the difference in value, or return it to the seller for a full refund.
This is the standard khyar al-ayb right.
3
Understand the complication of subsequent defect
If after discovering the deficiency, a new defect occurs in the buyer's possession, and the seller refuses to take the basin back, compensation is calculated by comparing values.
The seller's refusal implies the buyer cannot return; instead he recovers the loss due to the original deficiency.
4
Perform the compensation calculation
Value of basin if it were as specified (10 mann) is assessed, minus value as it actually is (5 mann). The buyer recovers that difference from the seller.
The text uses example: 10-mann basin worth 20, 5-mann worth 10; deficiency is 10. That is the buyer's recovery.
5
Note the overarching principle: al-qawl li-l-qabid
The possessor's word is accepted regarding increase or decrease. This principle governs the third ruling on silk.
This principle is the basis for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He said in al-Khaniyyah: A man sold a pearl on the basis that it weighs one mithqal, and he found it more; it is conceded to the buyer because weight in what harms division is a description like the cubit in cloth, end. And in al-Khulasah: He bought a basin on the basis that it is ten mann, and it became clear after possession that it is five mann; the buyer has an option because it is like a defect. Then if a defect occurred in his possession and the seller refused to accept it, the basin is valued as ten mann, for example, at twenty, and valued as five mann at ten; so the defect is a reduction of five, end. And the word is of the possessor regarding increase and decrease. Upon it branches what is in al-Khaniyyah: And if one sold to another silk, and the seller weighed it for the buyer, and the buyer took it, then came after a period and said, 'I found it deficient': if he knows that it decreased from the air, nothing is on the seller. Likewise if the decrease is of the sort that occurs between two weighings. And if the decrease is not from the air and does not occur between two weighings, then if the buyer has not acknowledged that he received such-and-such mann, he may withhold the share of the decrease from the price if he has not paid the price; if he has paid the price, he recovers that amount from him. And if the buyer acknowledged that he received such-and-such and found it less than that, he may not withhold anything from the seller from the price nor recover it, end.

Commentary
This passage presents three rulings from Hanafi texts. The first, from al-Khaniyyah, concerns a pearl sold with a specified weight (one mithqal). If the buyer finds it heavier, the excess belongs to the buyer because for items like pearls, weight is a description akin to the cubit measurement in cloth; an excess does not harm the contract—the buyer gets the extra without additional payment. The second, from al-Khulasah, concerns a basin sold as ten mann but found to be five after possession. The buyer has an option because deficiency is like a defect. If a subsequent defect occurs in the buyer's possession and the seller refuses to take it back, the compensation is calculated: value of ten-mann basin minus value of five-mann basin, and the buyer recovers that difference. The third ruling, from al-Khaniyyah, deals with a buyer's later claim of deficiency in silk after possession. The key factors: if the loss is natural (from air) or typical between weighings, the seller is not liable. Otherwise, if the buyer did not acknowledge receipt of a specific weight, his word is accepted, and he can withhold or recover the deficiency. But if he acknowledged receipt, he cannot later claim deficiency.

Key Terms
القابض (al-qabid): The person who has taken possession (the buyer); his word is accepted regarding increase or decrease unless he acknowledged a specific amount.

Key Takeaway
Deficiency in weight after possession gives the buyer a choice; if return is impossible, compensation is the value gap.
